Preface
The Master’s Thesis is carried out at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) at the
Department of Civil Engineering, in the period from August 22nd 2016 to February 22nd
2017. The project was carried out in collaboration with DTU Civil Engineering, the friction
damper manufacturer, DAMPTECH A/S, and the engineering consultancy, NIRAS A/S.
The thesis is nominated to 35 ECTS points and is the ﬁnal part of the requirements for
obtaining a degree as Master of Science, M.Sc., in Civil Engineering.

==PAGE:4==

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In civil engineering, there is an increasing use of structures or structural elements with
low internal damping, caused by development of engineering knowledge. In order to meet
requirements of structural integrity and serviceability for these structures, the demand
for vibration control is increasing. Through the past decades, a vast variety of diﬀerent
possibilities to add external damping to these structures have been developed and practised,
where the passive damping device based on friction has become popular due to its relative
low cost and high reliability. These types of devices have, however, some disadvantages.
Because of their passive nature, they can only be designed in an optimal manner with
respect to a single frequency and vibration mode, or in other words, they can only be
optimised in relation to a single vibration control objective. For the friction damper, the
optimisation possibilities are further limited since the device can only be optimised for a
certain interval of displacement amplitudes. This thesis is a research project in the practical
design of a friction damper that is able to mechanically change the friction force depending
on the displacement amplitude and in the possibilities of vibration control enhancement in
comparison with the general friction damper. The objective is to have multiple levels of
friction force for multi-functional vibration control and friction damping with less amplitude
dependency.
In practice, it was succeeded to create a passive friction damper that is mechanically
changing the damping force depending on the displacement amplitude through a slotted
connection, which is limiting the movement of the low friction force. This device and the
general friction damper were tested extensively in order to verify their performance during
various conditions representing real-life demands of the dampers. Experimental results
demonstrated that both friction dampers are performing well within requirements and with
the desired hysteretic behaviour. Furthermore, a numerical model of both dampers were
developed, updated and compared to experimental results. A very good compliance was
obtained between the numerical model and the experimental results. It is found that the
numerical model can be used for design of multi-level friction dampers that fulﬁls client
requests of speciﬁc force-displacement relations.
Numerical simulations were carried out as a feasibility study of the new damper in order
to investigate the possibilities of multi-functional vibration control and friction damping
with less amplitude dependence. For this purpose, a test case was set-up with a ﬁctive
building subjected to wind and earthquake load, where a proposed procedure was used
for optimisation of the dampers. For the multi-functionality, it was desired to reduce
displacements during earthquakes and reduce accelerations during wind. It is found that
the friction damper with multiple levels is able to successfully improve the vibration control
for both objectives, where the general friction damper is only able to mitigate one vibration
objective. Furthermore, the amplitude dependency of the new friction damper compared
==PAGE:6==
iv
Abstract
to the general friction damper was analysed by calculating the ratio of dissipated input
energy during varying load intensities. From this study, it is found that the new friction
damper is able to overcome the amplitude dependence of the optimisation of the general
friction damper. Basically, it can be stated that a passive device with some of the beneﬁts
of semi-active damping is developed.
==PAGE:7==
Resumé
I bygge- og anlægsbranchen er der på baggrund af ingeniørmæssig udvikling en stigende brug
af konstruktioner eller strukturelle elementer med lav dæmpning. For at opfylde kravene
til styrke og anvendelse af disse konstruktioner er efterspørgslen efter vibrationsdæmpning
stigende. Gennem de seneste årtier er mange forskellige muligheder for at tilføje ekstern
dæmpning til disse konstruktioner blevet udviklet og praktiseret. Heraf er den passive
dæmpningsanordning baseret på friktion blevet populær på grund af sine relativt lave
omkostninger og høj pålidelighed. Disse typer af dæmpere har dog nogle ulemper. På grund
af deres passive natur kan de kun optimeres i forhold til en enkelt frekvens og svingningsform,
eller med andre ord, de kan kun optimeres i forhold til at imødekomme ét vibrationsproblem.
For friktionsdæmperen er optimeringsmulighederne yderligere begrænset, da dæmperen kun
kan optimeres i forhold til et bestemt interval af ﬂytningsamplituder. Dette speciale er
et forskningsprojekt i den praktiske udformning af en friktionsdæmper, der er i stand til
mekanisk at ændre friktionskraften i forhold til ﬂytningsamplituden, og i undersøgelse af
mulighederne for forbedret dæmpning i forhold til den almindelige friktionsdæmper. Målet
er at opnå ﬂere niveauer i friktionskraften til at imødekomme forskellige vibrationsproblemer,
samt at udvikle en friktionsdæmper, der er mindre afhængig af ﬂytningsamplituder.
I praksis lykkedes det at udvikle en passiv friktionsdæmper, der mekanisk ændrer dæmpn-
ingskraften afhængigt af amplituden gennem et aﬂangt hul, som begrænser bevægelsen af den
lave friktionskraft. Den nye friktionsdæmper og den almindelige friktionsdæmper er testet
grundigt for at veriﬁcere ydeevnen under forskellige forhold, der repræsenterer virkelighedens
krav til dæmperne. Eksperimentelle resultater viser at begge friktionsdæmpere opfylder
alle normkrav og har den ønskede hysterese adfærd. Desuden blev en numerisk model af
begge dæmpere udviklet, opdateret og sammenholdt med eksperimentelle resultater. En
meget god overensstemmelse blev opnået, og den numeriske model kan bruges til design af
dæmpere, der opfylder fremtidige kunders krav.
Numeriske simulationer blev gennemført som en undersøgelse af den nye dæmper i
forhold til mulighederne for at imødekomme ﬂere vibrationsproblemer og for at opnå en
friktionsdæmpning, som er mindre amplitudeafhængighed. Disse simuleringer blev udført
med en ﬁktiv bygning udsat for vind- og jordskælvsbelastning, hvor en fremgangsmåde til
optimering af dæmperne blev foreslået. For at vurdere multifunktionaliteten af dæmperne
blev det testet, om det er muligt at reducere ﬂytninger ved jordskælv og reducere accelera-
tioner ved vindbelastning. Det konstateres, at friktionsdæmperen med ﬂere niveauer er i
stand til at afhjælpe begge vibrationsproblemer, hvor den almindelige friktionsdæmper kun
er i stand til at afhjælpe ét vibrationsproblem. Endvidere blev amplitudeafhængigheden
af den nye friktionsdæmper i forhold til den almindelige friktionsdæmper analyseret ved
at beregne graden af dissiperet inputenergi under varierende intensiteter af belastningen.
Fra denne undersøgelse kan det konstateres, at den nye friktionsdæmper er i stand til at
==PAGE:8==
vi
Resumé
overvinde amplitudeafhængigheden i optimeringen af friktionsdæmperen. Dybest set kan
det konstateres, at en passiv dæmper er udviklet med nogle af fordelene fra semi-aktiv
dæmpning.

==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
In civil engineering, there is an increasing use of structures or structural elements with low
internal damping in placement with dynamic loading, such as slender bridges, cables for
cable-stayed structures, towers, buildings and oﬀshore structures. The dynamic loading of
the structures cannot be removed, since it is a part of the natural environment we live in
with wind, waves, earthquakes and man and machine movement. With the research and
exploitation of high-quality materials with higher strength, engineers are able to construct
structures with larger spans and heights with decreased stiﬀness, which all improve civil
engineering possibilities and opportunities, but increases the demands for vibration control
to satisfy structural integrity and serviceability criteria. Therefore, through the past decades,
a vast variety of diﬀerent possibilities to add external damping to these structures have been
researched, developed and practised from active tuned vibrations absorbers with advanced
measurement and control electronics to simple devices employing friction between sliding
surfaces, all with diﬀerent pros and cons. The latter, the friction damper, has been developed
over the past three decades and has become popular due to its simple nature. These devises
provide a high energy dissipation potential at a relative low cost with high reliability, low
maintenance, no material yielding problems and no ﬂuid leaking problems. However, they
also have a few disadvantages. Because of their passive nature, they can only be designed in
an optimal manner with respect to a single frequency and vibration mode, as any passive
damper, e.i. they can only be optimized in relation to a single vibration control objective.
Furthermore, the friction damper, as it is generally known with one constant friction force,
can only be optimized in an optimal manner for a certain displacement amplitude. The use
and eﬃciency of the passive damper and the traditional friction damper can be expanded, if
the properties of the damper can change with respect to the oscillation amplitude without
the use of expensive electronics with less reliability. Today, with the increasing requirements
of multi-functional vibration control demands of these devices through increased structural
demands and developments with clients requiring low cost, simpleness and high reliability,
the incentives for research and development of these multi-functional passive devices are
high. Therefore, this thesis is a research project in the practical design of a friction damper
with mechanical change of the friction force depending on the oscillation amplitude and in
the possibilities of vibration control enhancement in comparison with the general friction
damper with just one constant friction force.
For the experimental part of the thesis, the work is involving development of the friction
dampers that uses a rotational mechanism, which are supplied by DAMPTECH. The
development is carried out in collaboration with DAMPTECH, by which the 20 years of
experience in development of friction dampers is beneﬁted from. Furthermore, the entire
project has beneﬁted from the collaboration with NIRAS in guidance and discussion of
ideas throughout all of the process of thesis. NIRAS possesses many years of experience
with engineering design of structures involving all kinds oﬀdynamic problems.
==PAGE:26==
2
1 Introduction
Objectives
The overall objectives of the thesis is to develop a practical design of a multi-level friction
damper that is mechanically changing the damping force depending on the displacement
magnitude, and to investigate the possible enhancement of vibration control compared to
the general friction damper with one constant friction force. The immediate objectives of
the thesis are:
• To create a mathematical model of the developed multi-level friction damper in order
to be able to understand the mechanics and governing parts of the design related to
the behaviour of the damper. For this part, a study of dry friction and friction in
general is necessary. The main application of the mathematical model is to design
multi-level friction dampers that fulﬁls client requests of speciﬁc force-displacement
relations, when the damper is supplied for civil engineering projects in the future.
• To experimentally test, investigate and document the mechanical behaviour of the
single-level and multi-level dampers during various conditions representing real-life
demands of the dampers. It is necessary to know and test the behaviour of the multi-
level mechanism in order to ensure that is working as intended, and to investigate if
unknown problems are existing with the mechanism. The current single-level design
is tested in order to fully understand the diﬀerent parts of the design before the
development is carried out. The testing of the dampers should fulﬁl applying design
codes.
• To numerically simulate and study the feasibility of the multi-level damper in order
to investigate the possibilities of vibration control enhancement. Based on client
requests for DAMPTECH, a test case is simulated, where the multi-level damper’s
ability to mitigate earthquake induced displacements and wind induced accelerations
is investigated and compared to the single-level damper. Furthermore, it is desired to
simulate the damping diﬀerence from having multi-levels of friction instead of one,
and relating the results to possible applications of the multi-level damper.
Structure of the Thesis
In this thesis, the ﬁrst part involves introduction of diﬀerent types of damping, passive
damping devices, research of existing passive multi-level dampers and theory of friction.
Then the mathematical model of the dampers are described followed by the experimental
work. Finally, the numerical simulation and feasibility study is described and discussed in
detail. The chronology of the thesis follows, to some extent, the chronology of the process
of the project.
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 2
Vibration Control
Vibration can often lead to undesirable circumstances like discomfort for people in oscillating
structures and structural or mechanical fatigue or failure, just to name a few. This chapter
describes the overall energy dissipation categories in vibration control of structural and
mechanical engineering. Then the more frequently used passive energy methods used in
structural engineering are outlined with emphasis on friction damping. Furthermore, three
interesting projects in relation to this thesis, found in a study of related multi-level passive
damping projects in the literature, are presented.
This chapter should help the reader to get a better understanding of the current damping
techniques, possibilities and limitation and provide a suitable contrast for the friction damper
in question in the present thesis.
Dissipation Condition and Cyclic Energy Loss
In order to get an understanding of the dissipation mechanism, the dissipation condition of
a dynamic system is investigated in accordance with Krenk (2013). The equation of motion
is considered with the damping force given as Fd = −h(x, ˙x):
m¨x + h(x, ˙x) + kx = F(t)
(2.1)
From the equation of motion the rate of work is formulated. This is done by multiplying the
equation of motion with ˙x and integrating the inertia and stiﬀness term in terms of time, t:
(m¨x + h(x, ˙x) + kx) ˙x = F ˙x
(2.2)
⇒
d
dt
1
2m ˙x2 + 1
2kx2

= F ˙x −h(x, ˙x) ˙x
(2.3)
The mechanical energy, E, of a system is deﬁned as the sum of kinetic energy, 1
2m ˙x2, and
potential energy, 1
2kx2. Thus, the rate of change of the mechanical energy can be described
in terms of the rate of external work, F ˙x, and the rate of dissipation, h(x, ˙x) ˙x:
d
dtE = F ˙x −h(x, ˙x) ˙x
(2.4)
Two important relations are associated with equation (2.4). Depending on the load and
response, the rate of external work can be both positive and negative. However, in order
to have valid passive damping, the energy dissipation must always extract energy, thus,
h(x, ˙x) ˙x ≥0. Based on this equality and only considering linear functions of h(x, ˙x), the
only valid form of damping is the linear viscous damping, h = c ˙x.
==PAGE:28==
4
2 Vibration Control
Energy Loss Factor and Equivalent Viscous Damping
For many years equivalent static loads have been used in building codes in order to take
dynamic eﬀects into consideration. In this way structures are designed by only focussing
on strength, which can lead to very heavy and costly structures. In the recent decades
the focus has changed to involve dynamic design of structures with emphasis on damping,
whereby energy is extracted from the system instead of constructing structures to resist the
input energy (Krenk, 2013; Soong and Dargush, 1997).
From equation (2.4), the energy balance of a free system can be formulated by omitting
the external work and considering the damping force as Fd = −h(x, ˙x):
d
dt
1
2m ˙x2 + 1
2kx2

= Fd ˙x
(2.5)
The dissipated energy or energy loss over one cycle can be obtained by integrating the rate
of energy dissipation over a full cycle, T = 2π/ω0:
∆E = −
Z
T
Fd
dx
dt dt = −
I
Fd dx
(2.6)
The energy loss per cycle can be used as a quantiﬁable reference for describing the magnitude
of damping, when investigating a variety of damping mechanisms.
Based on the energy loss per cycle, an important factor in relation to damping is deﬁned,
the so-called loss factor, ηd. The loss factor describes the dissipated energy per cycle relative
to the maximum energy in the cycle, and is given as

From the equivalent viscous damping of the friction damping, it is clear that friction damping
is dependent of frequency and also the amplitude of the displacement. The later dependency
implicates that friction damping, in the traditional sense, can only be optimised for a
speciﬁc amplitude. Furthermore, if the system forces are not large enough to overcome the
friction force, the oscillation will stop, e.i. the oscillation will stop when kXn < FC. It is
also important to mention that the equivalent viscous damping is only equivalent to the
dissipated energy for the amplitude of which it is determined, and is not in any way capable
of describing the behaviour of friction damping.
2.1
Categories of Vibration Control
The existing damping techniques for structural and mechanical vibration control are divided
into three main damping categories; Passive damping, semi-active damping and active
damping. These three categories are illustrated in Figure 2.1.
A passive damper or passive damping mechanism is a mechanical device or mechanism,
which is able to extract energy from a vibrating system in accordance with equation (2.4)
without involvement of electronic control or change. Thus, a passive damper does not require
an external power source, complicated software and control algorithms, they are much more
simple by nature. A passive damper develops the damping forces that dissipates vibration
energy when moved, accelerated, shortened and/or lengthened in relation to the vibration
of the system. However, the simpleness also naturally involves limitations in design and
application. On the marked today, a vast variety of passive dampers exists and some of
them are described in the following section.
The semi-active damper is similar to the passive damper, but has the ability to change
properties in real-time (Symans and Constantinou, 1999). The ability to change properties
in real-time enables the damper to change force depending on the vibration situation, and
thereby deliver the optimal damper force for the respective situation. The possibility of
changing damper properties comes from including an active resistance generator to the
damping mechanism, which has an external power supply managed by a control algorithm
running on information of the given vibration situation. The design and usage possibilities
==PAGE:30==
6
2 Vibration Control
of the semi-active damper are enhanced compared to the passive damper. However, they
come with a cost of the necessity of including an active resistance generator with an external
power supply and advanced control algorithms. The semi-active damper is illustrated in
Figure 2.1 and has an indication of the active resistance generator with an ’R’.
An active damper has, like the semi-active damper, a passive damping mechanism and an
active resistance generator, but also an active force generator. With this extra modiﬁcation,
the damper now deviates from equation (2.4), since it is able to provide energy into the
system. If the active damper is properly controlled, it can achieve the uppermost optimal
vibration control and eﬀectively damp several vibration modes simultaneously. However,
it requires an even larger power source and is highly complex due to design, control and
understanding, thus, the cost of this extra feature is high (Symans and Constantinou, 1999).
The active damper is illustrated in Figure 2.1 and has an indication of the active resistance
generator with an ’R’ and an indication of the active force generator with an ’A’.
(a) Passive damper
(b) Semi-active damper
(c) Active damper
R
A
R
Figure 2.1: The three categories of damping, (Wagner, 2014).
In the literature, the hybrid damper is often mentioned as another category of damping.
Hybrid systems have both a passive damping part and an active damping part. In order to
maximise the eﬃciency of the system it is quite common in practise to use active devices
that can redress drawbacks of some passive dampers and vice versa (Di Sarno and Elnashai,
2005).
Several diﬃculties and limitations are existing with present damping devices. Active
and semi-active damping devices requires electronic hardware and software, which are extra
features that introduces extra cost and are prone to errors. A passive damper on the other
hand is a mechanical device, which because of its passive nature can only be designed in
an optimal manner with respect to a single frequency and vibration mode (Krenk, 2013;
Inman, 2014). Therefore, the possibility of a passive device that can change properties by
mechanical modiﬁcations instead of electronic programming, the diﬃculties and limitations
can be reduced.
2.2
Passive Vibration Dissipation Methods
This project addresses the use of friction dampers for further development of a passive
vibration damper. When developing something within any ﬁeld, it is important to known
==PAGE:31==
2.2 Passive Vibration Dissipation Methods
7
the current available techniques and methods in order to understand the strengths and
weaknesses of the project or product at hand. Since this thesis is working within passive
energy dissipation, the more frequently used passive energy methods used in structural
engineering are outlined in this section.
2.2.1
Viscous Damper
The viscous damper is a mechanical device, which resistance follows the linear viscous
damping theory. The most common viscous dampers are using ﬂuid motion, typically
silicone-based ﬂuid, which is forced through oriﬁces in a closed cylinder. In this way, the
energy is dissipated due to friction between the ﬂuid and oriﬁce/cylinder walls (Chopra,
2012). The advantage of this method is that the contact involves no stiction (static friction)
or other phenomena that leads to undesirable non-linearities. Figure 2.2 illustrates a viscous
damper and its application.
Figure 2.2: (a) Schematic drawing of ﬂuid viscous damper. (b) Diagonal bracing with ﬂuid
viscous damper. (Chopra, 2012)
For viscous damping, Fd = −c ˙x, the force-displacement relation for a harmonic cycle,
x(t) = X cos(ωt), can be evaluated by:
Fd = kX cos(ωt) + cωX sin(ωt) = kx(t) ± cω
q
X2 −x(t)2
(2.14)
The force evaluated by equation (2.14) is the viscous-elastic damping, because of the inclusion
of an elastic stiﬀness, kx(t). Figure 2.3 illustrates the force-displacement relation without
the elastic part.
By rewriting equation (2.14) in quadratic form, the equation resembles the equation of an
ellipse:
F 2
d = (cω)2 h
X2 −x(t)2i
(2.15)
It is, therefore, clear that the viscous damping has a form of an ellipse with the maximum
value of X and cωX on the x- and y-axes, respectively.
The viscous damper can also be constructed without ﬂuid using a solid viscoelastic
material. The viscous damper is a so-called local damper, that uses the relative displacement
within a structure or the relative displacement to something stable to generate the damping
force.

2.2.2
Magneto-Rheological Damper
The magneto-rheological (MR) damper is the latest newcomer among vibration control
devices and have been used since the mid 1990’s.
This particular device is using the
properties of magnetism to create the dissipation behaviour. The MR damper looks like
a viscous damper and contains ﬂuid that typically consists of micron-sized, magnetically
polarisable particles in a carrier medium of mineral or silicone oil (Soong and Spencer-Jr,
2002). This MR ﬂuid has the capability of changing between free-ﬂowing viscous liquids
and semi-solids having a controllable yield strength within milliseconds (Sapiński, 2009).
This capability arises when the MR ﬂuid is exposed to a magnetic ﬁeld, which can be
controlled by electricity, allowing for modiﬁcation of the damping characteristics under
varying vibration conditions. With this property the MR damper, it is ideal for real-time
semi-active control, which has led to major interest from researchers and practitioners in
the past decade (Soong and Spencer-Jr, 2002). However, the same advantage is also a
disadvantage since the control of the MR damper requires a power supply and/or a control
algorithm. Figure 2.4 illustrates the structure of a MR damper and its application for
damping of stay cables. The MR damper can be used for vibration control of buildings in
the same fashion as the viscous dampers.
Figure 2.4: (a) Illustration of 20-ton magneto-rheological ﬂuid damper (Soong and Spencer-
Jr, 2002). (b) Magneto-rheological ﬂuid damper attached to stay cable (Krenk, 2013).
==PAGE:33==
2.2 Passive Vibration Dissipation Methods
9
The force-displacement behaviour of the MR damper is similar to coulomb friction,
with an almost constant force varying in direction with the sign of the velocity. Figure 2.5
illustrates the force-displacement relation for a 200 kN MR damper.
In the latest years, research of simultaneous energy harvesting and vibration damping
using the MR damper has been successfully carried through, see Shen et al. (2016). In this
particular case, a small generator as the one in a standard car has been used to generate the
electric power. This property creates possibilities for spectacular engineering applications in
the next decade.
Figure 2.5: Force-displacement relation for a 200 kN magneto rheological ﬂuid damper,
(Soong and Spencer-Jr, 2002).
2.2.3
Metallic Damper
Another popular and eﬀective method of passive seismic energy dissipation is to use the
inelastic deformation of metals. These dampers are normally constructed by using mild
steel plates with a triangular or X shape, installed with diagonal bracing in frame structures.
In this way, yielding of the metal plates will occur, when the shear force passes the yielding
force of the plates. Thus, seismic energy is dissipated through the yielding of the mild
steel plates, which is the hysteria eﬀect (Soong and Spencer-Jr, 2002; Soong and Dargush,
1997). Other types of metallic dampers can be found, such as types based on bending of
honey comb, slit dampers and shear panel types. Figure 2.6 illustrates an X shaped metallic
damper from ADAS and the corresponding force-displacement relation can be seen in Figure
2.7.
==PAGE:34==
10
2 Vibration Control
Figure 2.6: Schematic drawing of a metallic damper by ADAS, (Soong and Spencer-Jr,
2002).
These kinds of dampers have some particularly desirable features. They have a stable
hysteretic behaviour, long term reliability and are relative insensitive to temperature (Soong
and Spencer-Jr, 2002). However, there is one particular downside with the metallic dampers.
Every time an earthquake, with a large enough magnitude to cause yielding of the metallic
dampers, has occured, the dampers have to be replaced. Thus, maintenance operations and
costs are associated with use of metallic dampers.
Figure 2.7: Force-displacement relation of X-shaped metallic damper. Displacement Ampli-
tude; (a) 0.42in, (b) 1.04in and (c) 1.56in, (Soong and Dargush, 1997).
==PAGE:35==
2.2 Passive Vibration Dissipation Methods
11
2.2.4
Friction Damper
The friction damper, as the name indicates, is employing the mechanism of two solid
bodies in contact moving relative to each other. The idea of the friction damper comes
from the automotive industry, where it has been used for dissipation of kinetic energy
through friction brakes for ages. In 1979, Avtar S. Pall was inspired by the principle of the
friction brake, and he started to develop a friction damper for structures with his colleagues
(Di Sarno and Elnashai, 2005). Since then, friction dampers have been investigated by
many researchers, and they have successfully been used for vibration control of structures
subjected to earthquakes. Through the development in the last decades, many friction
dampers have been invented and tested experimentally e.g. Mualla and Belev (2002), Pall
and Marsh (1982), Aiken and Kelly (1990), Wang et al. (2012), Constantinou et al. (1991),
Nims et al. (1993) and Grigorian et al. (1993). The friction damper is one of the more
popular dissipation methods due to its advantages. These systems provide a high energy
dissipation potential at relative low cost with high reliability, no material yielding problems,
no ﬂuid leaking problems, and they are easy to install and maintain (Mualla and Belev,
2002; Lu et al., 2006).
One of the diﬀerences between a friction damper and some the other devices is that
the damper requires a certain force to overcome the static friction before the surfaces in
contact starts to slide relative to each other. This is called the slip force or slip load. A
standard single-level friction damper is designed to slip during an earthquake of a speciﬁed
magnitude, which according to the current design codes, is an earthquake with a return
period of 475 years. The resulting force-displacement behaviour for a traditional friction
damper is simply of rectangular shape. Besides enhancing the vibration control for severe
vibration, the dampers also changes the natural vibration frequencies, when it is not sliding
(Chopra, 2012). When designing the friction damper for a speciﬁc building, the ratio of the
slip force to storey yielding shear and ratio of bracing-to-storey stiﬀness also signiﬁcantly
eﬀect the vibration control enhancement (Di Sarno and Elnashai, 2005).
For the design of the friction damper itself, two very important subjects most be
considered carefully. One is the internal mechanical mechanism of the damper. How is
the friction force transformed into damping force, e.i. is the device using a rotational or
translational mechanism. The second subject is the materials for the solid bodies in contact
moving relative to each other. Since the development of friction dampers started in 1979,
various materials have been used for the contact surfaces such as brake pad material on steel,
steel on steel, steel on brass, graphite impregnated bronze on stainless steel and other metal
alloys (Di Sarno and Elnashai, 2005). Several experiences can be found in the literature,
see Di Sarno and Elnashai (2005), Mualla (2000) and Lee et al. (2016). In the following
subsections some of the more popular friction dampers are brieﬂy presented.
Slotted Bolted Connection Energy Dissipaters (SBC)
One of the more simple ways to create a friction damper is by using a slotted bolted
connection, see Figure 2.8. In the connection solid bodies are pre-tensioned in order to have
a desired normal force with a friction material in between. The slotted holes then allow
the bolts and the solid bodies to move relative to each other. The SBC can be placed in a
diagonal brace or chevron brace in a frame or similar.
==PAGE:36==
12
2 Vibration Control
Figure 2.8: (a) Schematic drawing of Quaketek friction damper with slotted bolted connec-
tion, (Quaketek.com, 2016). (b) Schematic drawing of slotted bolted connection, (Chopra,
2012).
Pall Type Friction Damper after Pall and Marsh (1982)
The Pall type damper is a slightly more complicated damper, but it is still using the
translational friction mechanism. Several variations of this friction damper have been
developed in past decades. In Figure 2.9, the most common of the Pall type dampers is
schematically illustrated. The damper concept consists of four pairs of long and narrow
plates connected by four corner bolts and a cross-shaped plate, which is connected to the
center part of the vertical and horizontal plates. The frictional material is then placed in
between the horizontal plates and the cross-shaped plate. The bolts at the two positions of
the friction material are pre-tensioned and placed in slotted holes. When the building is
subjected to severe seismic excitations the damper slips and the four outer plates of the
mechanism are moved and the damper deforms into a rhomboid shape, see Figure 2.9.
Figure 2.9: Schematic illustration of Pall type damper, (Wu et al., 2005).
==PAGE:37==
2.2 Passive Vibration Dissipation Methods
13
Rotational Friction Damper - Damptech A/S
Another way to create a friction damper is to use rotational technology. Rotational friction
joints have a poor utilisation of the generated friction force compared to slotted friction joints
due to the involvement of a moment arm of the friction joints. However, this drawback can
easily be compensated by applying higher clamping forces, material with higher coeﬃcient
of friction or multiple friction layers. The advantage is that all of the friction surfaces
are covered at all time and are therefore not prone to corrosion or any kind of dry or wet
lubrication.
The only, to the author known, friction damper manufacturer that uses rotational friction
technology is Damptech A/S, who has patented their technology. The present thesis is
based on this device and a thorough description can be found in Chapter 4.
Energy Dissipating Restraint by Nims et al. (1993)
The last single-level friction damper described in this thesis is the Energy Dissipating
Restraint (EDR) originally invented by Nims et al. (1993). The behaviour of the EDR is
very diﬀerent from the traditional friction damping with a rectangular force displacement
relation. The basic diﬀerence is that the EDR is self-centring, which it is also often called in
the literature, but this extra feature comes with the downside of dissipating less energy per
cycle. The self-centring mechanism is simply created by using a spring and by making the
normal force in the friction force depending on the spring force (Soong and Dargush, 1997).
The damping force of this device is relative small compared to other friction dampers.
The EDR is constructed of a cylinder, which on the inside has steel compression and
bronze friction wedges to transform the axial spring force into a normal pressure acting
outwards on the cylinder wall. In this way the frictional force is generated between the
bronze wedges and the steel cylinder. The normal force for the friction is generated by a
spring, which in one end is supported by an internal stopper and in the other end pressing
on the internal steel wedge. The length of the spring can be altered in order to change the
frictional slip force. If the device is set to have zero spring force on the internal wedges at
initial position, the force displacement relation will be formed by two triangles. However,
if the device is set to have a non-zero force on the internal wedges at initial position, the
force displacement relation will be formed by two so-called ﬂags (Zhou and Peng, 2009). In
Figure 2.10, a schematic illustration of the EDR can be found with typical experimental
force-displacement behaviour.
2.2.5
Tuned Vibration Absorbers
The tuned vibration absorbers are reducing structural vibration by creating an opposing
oscillation, which will counteract the oscillation of the structure. The tuned vibration
absorbers are constructed by attaching a secondary mass to the structure through a spring.
The optimal vibration absorption is then obtained by ’tuning’ the mass and the spring
of the damper. The addition of the extra mass and spring to a structure can be done in
various ways. The most simple way is by using steel springs and steel plates for the mass,
the so-called tuned mass damper, or a mass hanging in a wire, the so-called pendulum tuned
mass damper. For vibration control of the oscillation of the extra mass a local damper
can be added, typically a viscous damper. Another way is to have a basin with water, the
==PAGE:38==
14
2 Vibration Control
so-called tuned liquid damper. The tuned vibration absorber can be constructed in various
ways. In Figure 2.11 examples of the tuned mass damper and pendulum tuned mass damper
are illustrated.
Figure 2.10: Schematic illustration of the energy dissipating restraint and typical exper-
imental force-displacement behaviour with zero and non-zero spring pre-load (triangular
hysteresis and double ﬂag hysteresis), (Zhou and Peng, 2009).
Figure 2.11: (a) Tuned mass damper for vertical vibration mitigation, (SOHansen.com, nd).
(b) Illustration of the pendulum tuned mass in Taipei101, (Boobsimge.com, nd)
Early applications of the tuned vibration absorber was directed towards damping of wind
induced vibrations. However, in the past decades numerical and experimental studies have
been carried out to examine the eﬀectiveness of tuned vibration absorbers for mitigation of
seismic response of structures, which has led to plenty use of the method for passive and
==PAGE:39==
2.3 Multi-Level Damping Research
15
especially active and hybrid damping of high-rise buildings (Hoang et al., 2008; Soong and
Spencer-Jr, 2002). It is important to mention that a passive vibration absorber only can
be optimal tuned for damping of a single structural frequency. If the vibration absorber is
tuned for reduction of the ﬁrst mode response of a multi-degree-of-freedom structure, the
response of the higher modes may increase with the increase of the number of storeys. For
ground excitation, the reduction of the response is large for resonant ground motion, but
the vibration reduction diminishes as the dominant frequency of the excitation is moved
away from the structural natural frequency to which the vibration absorber is tuned (Soong
and Spencer-Jr, 2002).
2.2.6
Base Isolation
The base isolation idea is not an actual damping method, but a way to reduce the input
energy to the structure, instead of mitigating or transforming it, when it is in the structure.
A base isolation system or seismic isolation system is placed at the foundation of a structure.
In practice, the building is placed on elastomer bearings, lead rubber bearings or sliding
friction pendulum (Soong and Dargush, 1997). The base isolator then reduces the stiﬀness
of the structure and thereby lowers its natural frequencies as it acts as a spring between the
soil and the structure. Since the energy of earthquakes are generally contained in higher
frequencies, the structure will be less eﬀected by the earthquake. The principle of the
base isolation method and the eﬀect of reducing the natural frequencies are illustrated in
Figure 2.12. The base isolation system is often combined with a passive damper in order to
dissipate some of the remaining input energy, see Nielsen et al. (2004).
Figure 2.12: Illustration of the base isolation concept, (Eﬁloglu, 2013).
2.3
Multi-Level Damping Research
Before carrying out the multi-level modiﬁcation of the friction damper in question in the
present thesis, a study of related projects in the literature has been carried through. In
this study, two relevant projects were found. One experimental investigating of a friction
damper combined with a metallic damper and one numerical investigation of the vibration
==PAGE:40==
16
2 Vibration Control
enhancement of a friction damper with two-stage slip forces. These two project are brieﬂy
presented in the present section.
The idea of the combined friction damper and metallic damper by Lee et al. (2016)
is, as the fundamental idea of this thesis, to satisfy multiple functional demands of the
damper. This particular device is desired to be designed in a fashion, where the friction
damping part of the device is activated during small amplitude vibration, which could
be caused by a moderate earthquake or wind induced vibrations. When the structure is
subjected to large amplitude vibrations, caused by a severe earthquake, the metallic damper
is activated by a stopper generating a larger damping force. Lee et al. (2016) experienced
some problems with the speciﬁc set-up of the friction damper and metallic damper. However,
they successfully managed to create a device with two-phase behaviour in order to satisfy
multi-functional demands. The device developed by Lee et al. (2016) and its two-phase
behaviour is illustrated in Figure 2.13. The idea of the present thesis is very similar to the
one in Lee et al. (2016), but the developed device in the present thesis is using rotational
friction damping for both phases.
Figure 2.13: Conceptual conﬁguration of the multi-level damper, behavioural phases and
idealised hysteresis curve, (Lee et al., 2016).
The second interesting project in relation to this thesis found in the literature study is a
numerical simulation of a friction damper with a two-stage slip force, carried through by
Chien et al. (2014). The multi-level friction force in the simulation is created by having
==PAGE:41==
2.3 Multi-Level Damping Research
17
two passive friction elements, that possess diﬀerent slip loads, connected in series. The
ﬁrst friction element has a small slip force and is activated when subjected to a moderate
earthquake, while the second element has a high slip force and is activated when the damper
stroke and damper force is exerted by a severe earthquake. The simulation is carried out
for a single-degree-of-freedom system, which is illustrated in Figure 2.14 with the results of
the analysis. The results obtained from the simulation of the multi-level friction damper
is compared with simulations of just having one of the two friction elements respectively.
From the comparison, it is clear that the multi-level damper is able to eﬀectively dissipate
energy during moderate earthquakes, while almost still dissipating energy with the same
eﬀectiveness during severe earthquakes. However, they only obtained reductions around 40
%. It is believed, that greater reductions can be achieved. But these results obtained by
Chien et al. (2014) still provides even more reason for this thesis and the development of a
multi-level friction damper. It should be mentioned that the analysis in Chien et al. (2014)
is purely numerical, and no proposals have been made for construction of a damper that
has these damping characteristics.
Figure 2.14: Analytic model of a single-degree-of-freedom structure installed with the
investigated two-stage slip force friction damper and a graphical presentation of the vibration
control enhancement, (Chien et al., 2014).
Traditional friction damper (TFD), multi-
functional friction damper (MFD), friction damper (FD).
Furthermore, numerical simulations have been carried out for investigation of vibration
control by connecting adjacent buildings by friction dampers, see Bhaskararao and Jangid
(2006). Their results indicated, that the use of friction dampers to connect adjacent buildings
with diﬀerent natural frequencies can eﬀectively reduce earthquake hazards. Vibration
reductions of 25-50% were obtained.

CHAPTER 3
Tribology and Friction
Modelling
Friction is a complex, non-linear phenomenon and the desire to understand friction is old.
The ﬁrst concepts on the matter date as far back as to Leonardo da Vinci around the
15th-16th century (Olsson, 1996). In order to understand the friction behaviour of the
rotational friction damper investigated in this thesis, a minor research on the subject has
been carried through. In this chapter, important friction phenomena and static and dynamic
friction models are clariﬁed and discussed. The static and dynamic friction models are used
for modelling of the expected behaviour of the friction damper. The friction phenomena are
related to the frictional behaviour of the dampers, when analysing the experimental results,
and kept in mind when developing the friction dampers.
3.1
Friction Phenomena
Friction Interface
The phenomena of friction occurs between two surfaces in contact. The contact is not
continuously over the entire surface, but at a number of asperities as illustrated in Figure
3.1. When the surfaces are subjected to normal and tangential loads, the asperities will
deform and the surfaces will move relative to each other. In reality, the surfaces will always,
to some extend, be rough and covered by oxides or some other material (Olsson, 1996). If
the surfaces are lubricated, the friction behaviour will depend on the hydrodynamics of the
lubrication.
N
N
F
Figure 3.1: The microscopical contact between two surfaces, (Olsson, 1996).
An idealistic interpretation of the friction interface is illustrated in Figure 3.2, where
the behaviour of the contact of the surfaces are described by a number of springs. For the
asperity contact and the idealised springs, the tangential deformation is elastic to a certain
point. When the applied shear pressure exceeds the shear strength, the asperity contacts
will experience a plastic deformation and the idealised springs will snap (Olsson et al., 1998).
==PAGE:44==
20
3 Tribology and Friction Modelling
F
F
Figure 3.2: Interpretation of the friction interface.
In the sticking regime, an elastic
displacement occurs, and when the force becomes too large sliding occurs. (Olsson, 1996).
When examining the sliding process of the friction behaviour, two friction regimes are
revealed, the pre-sliding or so-called stick regime and the gross sliding regime. In the stick
regime the friction force is dominated by the asperity contacts and consequently the friction
force is primarily a function of displacement rather than velocity (Al-Bender, 2010). In the
sliding regime, more lubricant is brought into the interface depending on the velocity, when
the surfaces moves relative to each other. Thus, in the sliding regime the force is primarily
a function of velocity. This is further described in the following section.
Friction Force and Velocity
The mechanisms of friction in the interface can be divided into four so-called lubrication
regimes with diﬀerent behaviour (Olsson, 1996; Armstrong-Hélouvry, 1991). Figure 3.3
illustrates the four regimes for which the mechanisms of friction depends on the constant
relative velocity between the surfaces in contact. The ﬁrst regime is named sticking and
covers the mechanism, when the surfaces stick, i.e. no gross motion. In the sticking regime,
the friction force is dominated by the elastic and plastic deformation of the asperities. In
the second regime, boundary lubrication, motion with relatively low velocity is occurring. In
this regime the friction force is dominated by the shearing resistance of the asperity contacts.
The shearing resistance is dominated by the shearing resistance of the covering oxides or
other compound, which is weaker than the shearing resistance of the asperities leading to a
decrease in friction force. When the relative velocity of the surfaces in contact increases,
more lubricant is brought into the interface separating the surfaces. When the surfaces are
fully separated by the lubricant, the fourth regime, full ﬂuid lubrication, is reached. The
transition to full ﬂuid lubrication is the third regime, mixed lubrication. In the third regime
the friction force is partially due to asperity contacts and the viscosity of the lubricant.
In the fourth regime, the thickness of the ﬁlm is large enough to completely separate the
bodies in contact. The friction force is depending on the hydrodynamics of the lubricant,
which may lead to an increase in force (Olsson et al., 1998).
˙x
F
Sticking
Boundary lubrication
Mixed lubrication
Full ﬂuid lubrication
Figure 3.3: A typical relation between constant velocity and friction force. The so-called
Stribeck curve/eﬀect.
==PAGE:45==
3.1 Friction Phenomena
21
The mentioned relation between velocity and friction force with the deﬁned regimes were
described for the ﬁrst time by Stribeck in 1902. Stribeck performed his experiments with
constants velocity, hence, the friction force as a function of constant velocity is called the
Stribeck curve. In particular his work was focused around the relations in the boundary and
mixed lubrication regimes (Olsson, 1996). According to Al-Bender (2010) and Al-Bender
et al. (2004) the same behaviour seems to hold true for dry friction as well, even though
the involvement of hydrodynamics in this case is out of the question. Al-Bender et al.
(2004) states, that in the case of dry friction, the relation between velocity and friction
force is similar to the relation shown in Figure 3.3, but is governed by the deformation of
the asperities, the adhesion forces and the asperity masses. The two ﬁrst contributions
are increasing the force with increasing velocity, where the contribution from the asperity
masses has the opposite eﬀect. This description of the friction behaviour stipulates, that
the same relation will apply for the friction between the friction pads and steel plates in the
friction damper. However, experimental testing of similar devices using frictional material
with relative high friction coeﬃcients have been concluded to be independent of velocity
(Mualla, 2000; Soong and Dargush, 1997; Pall and Marsh, 1982).
Frictional Lag and Non-reversibility
The next relevant friction phenomena is the so-called friction lag also known as frictional
memory and hysteresis in the velocity. Basically, this phenomenon is a lag in friction force
relative to the sliding velocity, which is illustrated in Figure 3.4 (a). The reason for the
frictional lag for lubricated friction is related to the required time to modify the lubricant
ﬁlm thickness, which is called the squeeze eﬀect. However, friction lag is also observed in
dry friction experiments (Al-Bender, 2010). The friction lag mechanism in dry friction is
quite similar to lubricated friction. In dry friction, the local adhesion coeﬃcient increases
with the time of contact of two opposing asperities, which is caused by creep normal to the
surfaces. Due to this change, time is required before the friction changes, when the velocity
changes in magnitude causing a time lag. The end result of the frictional lag is that a higher
dynamic friction force can be observed for acceleration than for deceleration (Al-Bender,
2010).
˙x
F
˙x
F
(b)
(a)
Acceleration
Deceleration
Figure 3.4: (a) Illustration of frictional lag that may cause a higher dynamic friction force for
acceleration than for deceleration. (b) Illustration of non-reversible friction characteristic.
==PAGE:46==
22
3 Tribology and Friction Modelling
During so-called regressive or two-way oscillations with friction, another phenomenon
similar to frictional lag is described in the literature (Wojewoda et al., 2008).
Both
experimental and analytical studies have conﬁrmed that diﬀerent slopes of the friction
force may be obtained for acceleration and deceleration respectively. The phenomenon is
illustrated in Figure 3.4 (b) and is called the non-reversibility of friction force.
Friction Force and Displacement
Ernest Rabinowicz researched the transition phase from sticking to sliding, see Rabinowicz
(1951). Instead of investigating friction as a function of velocity, he argued that friction
should be studied as a function of displacement for the transition phase. For this, he carried
through simple experimental tests, where a block were place on an inclined plane and a ball
were rolled on the same plane impacting the block. From these experiments the relation
illustrated in Figure 3.5 was observed with the transition from sticking to sliding at the peak
of the friction force, which is called the break-away force. The observations of diﬀerence
in friction force for the sticking and sliding regime let to deﬁnitions of a static friction
coeﬃcient, describing the maximum friction force before break-away, and a kinematic friction
coeﬃcient, describing the friction force during relative motion of the interacting surfaces.
The reason for the diﬀerence in friction force in sticking and sliding regimes is found in
the behaviour of the microscopical contact. When displacement increases, junctions break
with less time to create new junctions resulting in gross sliding. Therefore, the friction
force in the sliding regime is dominated by the continuous process of asperity junction
formation and breaking, thus, resulting in a smaller force, when the strength of the contact
is depending on time, as described in the previous section (Al-Bender, 2010). With this
behaviour the dynamic friction force naturally becomes eﬀected by the relative velocity.
x
F
Figure 3.5: The relation between friction and displacement as described by Rabinowicz
(1951).
Previous work with the rotational friction mechanism with relative high friction coeﬃcient
by Mualla (2000) clearly indicates displacement dependent behaviour. The diﬀerence in
static and kinematic friction is however not observed. It can be argued that the velocity
eﬀect diminish for dry friction with relative high friction coeﬃcients. When the diﬀerence
in friction force from static to kinematic friction, due to the eﬀect of velocity, diminish, the
velocity eﬀect will also diminish.
==PAGE:47==
3.1 Friction Phenomena
23
Dwell-time and Break-away Force
In addition to the friction and displacement relation Ernest Rabinowicz investigated static
friction in relation to the time spent sticking, the so-called dwell-time (Olsson, 1996). He
concluded that the static friction increased with the dwell-time as illustrated in Figure 3.6
(a). However, later Johannes et al. (1973) concluded that the relation of the friction force
and dwell-time cannot be investigated without also investigating the relation to the rate of
the applied force. In order to take the second relation into consideration, they redesigned
the experiments so the rate of the force and the dwell-time could be varied separately. The
conclusion of the redesigned experimental tests was that the break-away force is depending
on the rate of the force as illustrated in Figure 3.6 (b), but not the dwell-time as concluded
by Rabinowicz (1958).
Dwell time
F
Force rate
Break-away-force
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.6: (a) Relation between dwell-time and friction suggested by Rabinowicz (1958).
(b) Relation between break-away force and force rate according to Johannes et al. (1973)
Pre-sliding Displacement
Experimental results investigating static friction have shown that some microscopic motion
is occurring even when the surfaces in contact are in the sticking regime (Olsson, 1996). This
is the so-called pre-sliding displacement or zero-slip displacement. The phenomenon was
found by Courtney-Pratt and Eisner in 1957, when they studied the spring-like behaviour in
the sticking regime. For small displacements the results agree well with Figure 3.7. When
the force is removed, a permanent displacement is present even though sliding has not
occurred, which is indicated by demolished lines in Figure 3.7.
x

Figure 3.7: Illustration of the pre-sliding displacement, (Olsson, 1996).

3 Tribology and Friction Modelling
3.2
Static Friction Models
As earlier mentioned, the study of dry friction goes back centuries, which naturally also
includes development of friction models.
These friction models developed in the past
centuries all have two things in common. They describe friction as a static function of
velocity, thus, they are called static friction models (Olsson, 1996). Secondly they are all
based on the same hypotheses developed from experiments with planar sliding of rectilinear
blocks (Soong and Dargush, 1997):
1. The friction force that can be developed is independent of the apparent surface area
in contact.
2. The friction force that can be developed is proportional to the normal force of the
surfaces contact.
3. The friction force that can be developed is independent of velocity for relative low
velocities.
Based on the above assumptions, Coulomb developed his basic friction model in the
18th century. The idea was that the friction force opposes motion and that the magnitude
can be described by a friction coeﬃcient multiplied with the normal force. These ideas are
stated by the following two equations and visualized by Figure 3.8 (a).
FC = µ N
(3.1)
F = FC sgn( ˙x)
(3.2)
Today, it is still vary common to describe the friction force by equation (3.1). Since the
Coulomb model can be used to describe both static friction and friction during slippage,
respective static, µs, and kinetic, µk, coeﬃcients are often introduced. This description
can handle variations of the friction force caused by material properties and geometric
properties, but the dependence on the normal force is more complicated (Olsson, 1996).The
eﬀect of rapid change in the normal force, which can cause large deviations from this model,
is not considered. However, this eﬀect is not relevant when dealing with friction dampers.
Furthermore, a relevant shortcoming of the Coulomb model is the inability to describe the
friction force at zero velocity. The friction force can simply be any value between −FC and
FC.
As earlier described for lubricated surfaces, the hydrodynamics of the lubricant have a
huge inﬂuence on the friction force. When the theory of hydrodynamics were developed in
the 19th century, it also lead to a development of a friction model depending on the viscosity
of the lubricant (Olsson, 1996). The viscous friction model together with the Coulomb
model can in general terms be described by the following equation and by Figure 3.8 (b).
F = F ˙x | ˙x|δ ˙x sgn( ˙x)
(3.3)
where δ ˙x depends on the geometry of the application and allows for non-linear variation
with velocity.
As earlier described, a higher friction force can be observed in the sticking regime.
Taking this phenomena into consideration, the friction force cannot be described by the
velocity alone. When the interacting surfaces are in the sticking regime, the static friction
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Static Friction Models
25
counteracts the external force. When the external force overcomes the static friction motion
will occur and the friction force drops to the deﬁned level for the friction force, when in
motion. This relation of the friction force in the sticking regime can be described by the
following equation and by Figure 3.8 (c).

force can however be any given function in the sliding regime, and still be combined with
the higher friction force at rest as given in equation (3.4).
Another phenomenon to include in a model is the mentioned Stribeck eﬀect with non-
linear variation of the friction force with the velocity. By modelling this eﬀect, the jump in
friction force when movement occurs can be changed to a more smooth decrease over low

3 Tribology and Friction Modelling
3.3
Dynamic Friction Models
By changing the static models, dynamic friction models can be obtained. In relation to this
thesis many diﬀerent dynamic models have been investigated (Olsson, 1996; Geﬀen, 2009;
Olsson et al., 1998; Al-Bender, 2010; Wojewoda et al., 2008; Dr. Korondi et al., 2014; Åström
and Canudas De Wit, 2008; Soong and Dargush, 1997). From an analysis of previously
conducted experimental work with friction dampers and demands of the functionality of the
friction model, it should at least be frequency independence, able to model hysteria, able to
model pre-sliding stiﬀness and have fair simulation eﬃciency. These demands exclude use of
models like the mentioned static models, the Armstrong Seven Parameter model, the LuGre
model, the Karnopp model and the Bristle model. For this thesis and for the modelling of
the friction force in the friction damper, the Dahl model and the models by Bliman and
Sorine have been considered. These models are described in the following subsections. Both
models are given and described as diﬀerential equations. In Section 5.2, a description of
how to implement such models in practical computation is given.
The Dahl Model
In 1968, P. R. Dahl published a friction model for the purpose of simulating control systems
with friction. The ﬁrst part of the model is based on the stress-strain curve in classical
solid mechanics. The surfaces in contact are modelled as a connection subjected to shearing.
The friction force force is, thus, depending on the strain generated by the shearing forces
corresponding to pre-sliding displacement. When the shearing reaches the rapture point,
the connection breaks and motion occurs with a constant friction force. The stress-strain
curve for the model is given as a diﬀerential equation. In 1975-1976, Dahl developed his
model further into the format that is generally known as the Dahl model today, which yields
the following relation in space domain with the direction of the force relative to the velocity
(Olsson, 1996).
dF
dx = σ0

sgn( ˙x)

sgn

sgn( ˙x)

where the exponent i is a model parameter, σ0 is the initial stiﬀness for F = 0 and Fc is
the Coulomb friction.
In order to obtain a time domain model, Dahl observed the following relation.
dF

(3.7)
The above deﬁnition implies that the model is only position dependent, e.i. the model
is rate independent. This property of the model is very important, because it allows for
modelling of pre-sliding displacement and use of hysteresis theory and operators, which is used
for dynamic calculations using friction dampers. Furthermore, the model is a generalization
of Coulomb friction. It does not capture the rate dependent Stribeck phenomenon nor does
it capture stiction. When analysing experimental results of the friction damper, none of
these two phenomenons are present in the frictional behaviour of the damper. However,
these shortcomings have led to many extensions of the Dahl model, e.g. the LuGre model.

The Dahl friction model is one of the more popular models due to its simplicity and
ability to capture much of the observed friction phenomena. In Figure 3.9, the Dahl model
is illustrated with the eﬀect of the parameters i and σ0 in relation to the force-displacement
behaviour of a typical friction damping mechanism.

The Models by Bliman and Sorine
From 1991 to 1995, Bliman and Sorine developed and published a family of dynamic
models. In the following, their ﬁrst-order model that corresponds to the Dahl model, and
a second-order model, which consists of two ﬁrst-order models in parallel, are presented.
The foundation of these model is the earlier described experimental observation made by
Rabinowicz (1951), where a higher static friction was observed compared to the friction
force during motion. The second-order model is, thus, developed in order to model the
frictional behaviour as illustrated for the Dahl model with the inclusion of the higher static
friction (Olsson, 1996).
Bliman and Sorine focussed on creating a rate independent model in order for the models
to describe friction in terms of displacement instead of time. This was done by replacing
the time variable with a space variable by the following transformation (Olsson, 1996).

CHAPTER 4
Design of 4-joint Rotational
Friction Damper
The designs of the passive friction dampers investigated in this thesis are described in detail
in this chapter. At ﬁrst, the design of the single-level damper is outlined. This damper
is manufactured by Damptech A/S and used in several projects all over the world for
earthquake protection of buildings. It is used in Abenobashi Terminal Building, the tallest
building in Japan, among others. The multi-level damper on the other hand is a brand new
concept. As described in Chapter 2, this concept of change in damper force is novel and the
research on the concept is limited, see Section 2.3. For this thesis, a simple change of the
single-level damper is made, where one joint is made with slotted holes in order to make the
damper force non-linearly dependent of the damper displacement. The mechanics and the
respective eﬀects related to vibration control are described in the following sections.
Long plate (LP)
Short plate (SP)

Figure 4.1: Picture with description of single-level multi-unit rotational friction damper
with 4 friction joints.
4.1
Single-Level Damper
The passive single-level damper is based on a rotational friction concept. Two sets of short
plates (SP) connects two sets of long plates (LP) and the long plates are connected to the
==PAGE:54==
30
4 Design of 4-joint Rotational Friction Damper
structure by a pinned connection at each end of the damper, see Figure 4.1, 4.2 and 4.3.
The number of plates in the damper are based on the desired capacity. One entire set of
plates equals a slip load of 100 kN and three sets of longitudinal plates equals 300 kN slip
load, etc. The most frequently sold dampers are in the range of 100 to 500 kN, but dampers
with capacities of up to 5000 kN have been specially fabricated, see Mualla et al. (2010).
This thesis mainly focusses on the 500 kN damper with 5 sets of long plates and 6 sets of
short plates. In the following, this damper is referenced to as the single-level damper. The
damper and the diﬀerent parts can be seen in Figure 4.1.
In the rotational joints of the damper, friction pads of frictional material with the
desired frictional behaviour is placed between the long and short plates. By a pre-tensioned
bolt through the joint, the necessary normal force on the friction pads is obtained. The
pre-tension force of the bolts are set to create the desired slip force of the damper.
When the damper is subjected to a load larger than the slip force, the friction pads
in the joints slips, and a relative constant damper force are exhibited, while the damper
lengthens or shortens. This mechanism of the damper is illustrated in Figure 4.2. If the
slip force is not exceeded by the external load, the damper will not move and add an extra
stiﬀness to the structure instead.
1
2
3
LP
SP
Joint
Pin
Figure 4.2: Mechanism of damper. 1) Original position. 2) Damper lengthened. 3) Damper
shortened. (Damptech.com, nda).
The friction damper is a local damper, and it is installed in structures as any other
local damper. The local damper term is describing a category of dampers all activated by
diﬀerential displacements within the structure or between the structure and a rigid external
part. Examples of use is damping of cables, damping of the base isolation connection of
a building or frame structures in general. Possible applications of the damper is further
discussed in Section 7.6.
The majority of the projects the 4 joint rotational friction damper has been fabricated
for are buildings with a frame structure. For this type of structure, the damper is installed
in a frame by a diagonal or chevron bracing. When the structure is laterally exited the
frame is displaced elastically and the damper is activated as illustrated in Figure 4.3. When
the damper is lengthened or shortened it dissipates potential energy by the simple relation
from equation (2.6):
∆E = −

(4.1)
During oscillations of the structure the dampers will be lengthened and shortened several
times transforming the input energy into thermal energy. It is important to stress that
even though the damper is not activated, the bracing structure adds extra stiﬀness to
the structure, which increases the natural frequency. The added stiﬀness can have both
==PAGE:55==
4.1 Single-Level Damper
31
positive and negative inﬂuence on the input energy depending on the natural frequency
without the bracing structure. However, if the input energy is unchanged with the change
in frequency, the response will decrease, since the system forces resisting the earthquake
forces are increased.
Figure 4.3: Mechanism of damper installed in a frame structure, (Damptech.com, nda).
The standard procedure for the force level of the single-level friction dampers in frame
structures is to set the friction force within a lower and upper limit based on functional
demands. The lower bound is about 130 % of wind storey-shear and the upper bound is
75 % of the storey-shear, at which the structural members will yield (PallDynamics.com,
nd). Thus, it is not normal procedure to use friction dampers for damping of wind induced
vibration of buildings. The use of two levels of slip force, where the ﬁrst level is designed to
damp wind induced vibrations, cannot be a success without considering issues, that may be
involved with activation of the friction dampers during wind storms. This matter is further
discussed in Chapter 7. In Figure 4.4, the principle of the force-displacement relation for
the single-level damper is illustrated. In this thesis the focus is on the situation where the
friction damper is placed in a frame as illustrated in Figure 4.4. The damper is a local
damper and can be used for any oscillating structure or mechanism, which has diﬀerential
movement.
x
Fd
Figure 4.4: Illustration of the principle force-displacement relation for the single-level
damper.
The bolts for the joints and clamping force are a critical object in the design. The right
bolts with the correct properties must be chosen. The ﬁrst important property is that the
bolts should be applicable for tensioning. Normal construction bolts like DIN 931/933 or
DIN 6914/15 cannot be used for tensioning. They will strip the thread around 70 % of the
proof load, which is approximately 90 % of the yield strength (Wallace, 2004). The problem
==PAGE:56==
32
4 Design of 4-joint Rotational Friction Damper
with the stripping of the tread is not only the lack of strength, but the way is breaks. Failure
by stripping of thread will not be discovered by the site worker and the broken bolt will
be left in the connection. Had the failure instead happened by bolt breakage, the failure
would be very obvious and the bolt would be replaced. This issue is thoroughly tested
by Wallace (2004), and it is concluded that the following bolts can be used for tensioning:
ASTM A325/A490, BS 4395, BS 4395, JIS B1186, AU 1252 or EN 14399-3/4 (so-called HR
or HV type bolts). Furthermore, the bolts must be of steel grade 8.8 or higher and cannot
be of stainless steel. For corrosion protection hot dip galvanization can be used.
For the experimental work involved in this thesis, DIN 931 grade 10.9 bolts were initially
used. These bolts were accepted due to the availability, ﬁtting thread length and the fact
that they would only be tensioned to 55 % of the proof load. According to the knowledge at
that point in time, these bolts would be ﬁne. However, it came about, that this was not
the case. During and in between testing, something occurred causing the damper force to
increase 40-50 %, and an unstable behaviour began, where the force could suddenly decrease
or increase. This issue is concluded to be caused by either wearing of the interfaces between
the bolts and bolt holes, or changes of mechanical properties of the bolt with repeated
change in section forces of the bolt. From discussions with experts on the subject, it came
up that this crazy behaviour has been observed for stainless steel bolts. It is concluded that
the best solution is using hot dip galvanized EN 14399-3/4 bolts.
When the correct bolts are chosen, other issues must also be taken into consideration.
These issues are spontaneous loosening of the bolts and the so-called relaxation, also called
slackening, caused by bolt creep over a long period of time, which causes loss of force in the
bolt. In order to reduce the amount of tension loss caused by relaxation, spring discs are
used, see Figure 4.1. When spring discs are placed in the connection, the total spring of
the bolt tensioning mechanism is softened, thus, less force is lost when the bolt creeps a
certain amount. For the spontaneous loosening, so-called lock washers with a wedge locking
mechanism was initially used. These washers are placed underneath the nut and the bolt
head. This set-up is not perfect, because spontaneous bolt loosening can still occur between
the spring washer and spring discs or between individual spring discs. However, it is a
lot more unlikely to happen in these sections, since the moment arm from the contacting
surfaces and the bolt center is a lot larger and no grease is present. The perfect set-up would
be using spring discs with a locking mechanism like Nord-Lock x-series washers, Rip-Lock
washers or similar, but none of these are made for bolts larger than M24. However, is was
discovered that the nut still came loose in some cases, when the torque was relatively low.
In the end, it is concluded that to avoid any spontaneous loosening, a second nut simply
has to be glued or welded on.
In order to investigate the actual forces in the bolts of the damper, one bolt is mounted
with strain gauges. Previous designs were investigated when determining how this bolt
should be designed. On this basis, it was chosen to use 2 two-element rosette strain gauges.
The strain gauge set-up on the bolt can be seen in Figure 4.5. This set-up is very eﬃcient
and robust in determining axial forces in a bolt, when there is no torsion or shear force
present. Unfortunately, this was not the case. When the damper was in motion huge
deviations around ±15-20 % were observed. The deviations made it more diﬃcult to obtain
a precise estimate of the forces in the bolts, and a somewhat average value was obtain by
manual assessment of the data. In order to have better measurements in the future, so-called
through hole (donut) load cells or three-element rosette strain gauges with a diﬀerent bridge
set-up could be used.
==PAGE:57==
4.2 Multi-Level Modiﬁcation
33
Bolt head lowered in spring washer.

4.2
Multi-Level Modiﬁcation
The single-level rotational friction damper design is modiﬁed into the multi-level design,
which is one of the main objectives of the present thesis. This modiﬁcation is made by a
simple change of one of the joints of the damper. The multi-level damper can be seen in
Figure 4.6 with a description of the respective joints and the slotted hole in the lower short
plates. In this joint, the friction pads are removed, allowing the plates in the joint to move
relative to each other without frictional resistance. The hole in the short plates is changed
into a slotted hole, and a chrome-nickel pin is put through without any pre-tension force.
The hole in the long plates is kept unchanged in order for the pin to follow the movement of
the long plates. The idea of this design is simply that, when the damper is displaced within
a small interval, the pin in the modiﬁed joint will not reach the end of the slotted holes
in the short plates. This means, that within this interval one joint of the damper is not
activated, and a smaller damper force is obtained. When the displacement is increased and
the pin reaches the end of the slotted holes, this joint is activated and the damper force is
increased. As indicated in Figure 4.6, two joints are activated the entire time generating
the constant ﬁrst level damper force, and only one joint is generating the second level. It is
desired that the damper force of the second level is at least twice the damper force for the
ﬁrst level. Thus, the desired diﬀerence is created by pre-tensioning the joint for the second
level at least twice of the joints for the ﬁrst level.
==PAGE:58==
34
4 Design of 4-joint Rotational Friction Damper
The main advantages of the presented multi-level design for multi-functionality is of
cause the two levels, but also the movement of the slot, when the second level is activated.
If the second level is activated at a displacement of 15 mm and the displacement continues
to 80 mm, then the damper only needs a displacement of 30 mm before the second level
is activated again when the motion changes direction. This advantage is however also an
extra non-linear dependence of the displacement history that needs to be taken care of in
the dynamic calculation of any structure damped by this device.
The main idea with the present multi-level design is rather to test the idea than to
create the perfect design, which is also project and application dependent. If the mechanism
of the slotted joint works without any complications, the damper design can be re-evaluated
(number of units, number of joints, plate and pad sizes etc.), in order to ﬁt almost any
desired ﬁrst and second level forces and ﬁrst level interval.
Joint 1 (1st level)
Joint 2 (1st level)
Joint 3 (no friction)
Joint 4 (2nd level)
Figure 4.6: Picture with description of the modiﬁcation of the single-level damper into the
multi-level damper.
The design of the slotted hole in the short plates was investigated in order to ﬁnd
the best solution. For this, a series of mathematical calculations were carried out, where
the short plates were kept in their initial position and the bolt movement is tracked and
plotted on top. The results of the investigation can be found in Figure 4.7. It is clear
that the movement has a radius, which is the distance between the bolts in the short plate.
Furthermore, when the results are studied more closely it is also observed, that the length
of the movement right and left from the center is not equal. However, if the curve length
of the slot is small this diﬀerence can be neglected. The diﬀerence in movement is though
incorporated in the numerical model.

is not reaching the end of the slotted holes), and where the amplitude of the second cycle is
well within the activation of second level. The area of the force-displacement relation is the
dissipated energy. It is clear that in principle the damper has a large area with activation
of the second level even though a small area at the diagonal corners are removed in order
to create the smaller ﬁrst level. The displacement length and interval are deﬁned with the

CHAPTER 5
Numerical Model of the
Friction Damper
In this chapter, the numerical models of the single-level and the multi-level damper are
described in detail. The diﬀerent behavioural characteristics inﬂuencing the damper are
analytically described and calculated numerically using the programming software Matlab.
When formulating the numerical model, previously conducted experimental tests of the
single-level damper were analysed in order to determine, which phenomena to include in the
model. The following considerations were made:
1. Non-linear variation of the damper force at varying displacements of the damper.
2. Pre-sliding displacement and elastic behaviour before slippage. In the stick state of
the friction pads, a clear displacement of the damper is observed before the joints of
the damper slips.
3. When the damper switches from stick state to slip state, a lower damper force and
diﬀerent behaviour is observed for a small distance.
4. The model should also be able to describe the second level for the multi-level behaviour
depending on a desired ﬁrst level displacement interval.
Figure 5.1 illustrates the observed phenomena for the computational model. Each
phenomenon is further described in the following sections.
