5 Numerical Model of the Friction Damper
5.1
Trigonometry, Forces and Static Friction Model
In order to describe the internal forces of the damper and the damper force at any given
displacement, an analytical model of the damper is derived. Such a model has already
been derived for the same 4 joint damper by Tarasaitis (2016). This model is however not
applicable for this project, because it is only able to describe the forces in the exact position,
where the short plates are perpendicular to the long plates. Thus, a new model is derived in
this chapter. The new model is developed in a way that allows diﬀerent frictional moments
in each joint, which makes it possible to describe the damper force and the internal forces
of the multi-level damper.
The new damper model is based on the diﬀerent lengths of the respective plates, l1−4,
the frictional moment of the joints, M1−4, the angle of the long plates, θ, and the angle of
the short plates from a position perpendicular to the damper force, κ. An illustration of
the model is given in Figure 5.2. The angle κ is a very important parameter of the damper
force. When κ = 0 the damper force reaches its minimum, because the moment arm for
transforming the frictional moment in the joint into damper force are the largest possible
at this position. In the following subsections the derivation of the damper force and the
internal forces is given.

for right-angled triangles:

5.1.3
Frictional Moment of a Rotational Joint
The frictional moment for one pad considering Coulomb friction, Mp, is deﬁned as the
moment developed by the friction in the hinge, when the friction material is sliding. The
magnitude of Mp is depending on the friction coeﬃcient, µ, the clamping force of the friction
interface, Pb, the friction area, A, and the moment arm of the friction area, r. If µPb is the
friction force in the friction interface, then µPb/A is the friction stress uniformly distributed
over the area. The frictional moment can be integrated by considering an elemental area,
dr · dθ, and the moment arm, r, see Figure 5.10. Thus, the frictional moment of one pad is
calculated by:
Mp = µPb

The friction moment of one joint, Mj, is simply found by multiplying the friction moment
of one pad, Mp, by the number of friction pads in one joint, mpads.
Mj = Mp · mpads
(5.27)
5.1.4
Test of the Numerical Damper Model
The damper model derived in this chapter is thoroughly tested by comparing results of the
damper force and the section forces with results from a ﬁnite element analysis in the ﬁnite
element program LUSAS Modeller. The ﬁnite element model is modelled using beam
elements and non-linear connections at the joints with constant moment at any rotation.
The beam elements are given the same dimensions as the standard 4 joint damper and
an isotropic material with a linear elastic stiﬀness of 210 GPa and a Poison’s ratio of 0.3.
The damper force and the section forces of the numerical model are tested at two diﬀerent
positions with frictional moment in all joints and only two joints, respectively. A very good
agreement was found between the numerical model and the FE-model. The FE-results at
initial position with frictional moments in all joints are shown in Figure 5.11 with indication
of the damper force at the ends of the damper.
Figure 5.11: FE-results at initial position with frictional moments in all joints and with
indication of the damper force at the ends of the damper.
From the previously conducted experiments, some elastic behaviour before slippage is
observed. In order to determine how much of the elastic behaviour is caused by the elasticity
of the steel, and how much is caused by the pre-sliding displacement friction phenomena,
a simple test is carried out using the FE-model. In this case, all joints of the damper are
clamped and the model is given a load of 500 kN. The FE-analysis resulted in a displacement
of only 0.37 mm. The displacement before slippage in the previously conducted tests are
observed to be 3-3.5 mm. With an elastic elongation of the steel parts of only 0.37 mm,
the elastic part of the force-displacement relation is concluded to be mainly caused by the
==PAGE:69==
5.2 Dynamic Friction Model
45
pre-sliding displacement friction phenomenon. Thus, no elastic stiﬀness is included in the
numerical model, but a pre-sliding displacement is incorporated by a dynamic friction model.
The results of the displacement test of the FE-model is shown in Figure 5.12.
Figure 5.12: FE-results of the displacement test.
5.2
Dynamic Friction Model
As described in the beginning of this chapter, the numerical model of the damper should
be able to describe the pre-sliding displacement behaviour. In order to do so, a dynamic
friction model is implemented in the numerical model of the damper. For this thesis, two
dynamic models have been considered, see Section 3.3. The Dahl model has been found to
be the best ﬁt due to its simplicity and because no increased static friction is observed in
the experimental data of the damper. In this section, a description of how the Dahl model
is implemented is given. The model by Bliman and Sorine can be implemented in a similar
fashion.
As described in Section 3.3, the ﬁrst order spacial derivative of the friction force in
relation to the friction force is given as:

The Dahl model is implemented using the ﬁnite diﬀerence approach. Other methods like
using the ode45 solver in matlab have been assessed. This solver is, however, diﬃcult to
control and a lot of numerical work had to be carried out in order to ﬁt the results from the
solver with the damper. The ﬁnite diﬀerence approach requires a higher resolution, but is
still a lot more eﬃcient than the solver, and the implementation is simple.

Based on the described and argued dominating asperity behaviour of the dry friction
interface in Chapter 3, the stiﬀness parameter σ0 of the Dahl model is changed to ﬁt
the present situation. If the change from stick to slip phase is depending on the relative
deﬂection and position of the asperities, the maximum friction force will be reached at
a certain distance independent of the magnitude of the friction force. In order for this
phenomenon to be considered in the model, the stiﬀness is simply considered as a function
of the pre-tension force, which inevitably changes the unit of the stiﬀness parameter σ0.
σ0(j) = σ0 · Pj
(5.34)

friction force, µP, in equation (5.26) is simply replaced by the Dahl friction force Fn+1.

discrete manner, becomes:

5.3
Multi-level Implementation
Before the numerical model is developed further, the behaviour of the short plate with the
slotted hole is investigated. With the curved design of the slotted hole, the moment arm, l3,
and the angle, κ, in the calculation of the damper force, becomes independent of position of
the pin in the slotted hole. For the independence of position to be valid, l3 and κ is deﬁned
from the centre of the pin and bolt. Thus, no change in calculation of damper force from
the frictional moment is necessary. A zero force interval for the joint activated, when the
pin reaches the end of the slotted hole, is simply implemented. The relation is illustrated in
Figure 5.14.

As described in Section 4.2, a length s is deﬁned for the total length of the damper stroke
without activation of the second level. When the damper has reached a stroke large enough
for the second level to be activated it returns to the ﬁrst level, when the force changes
direction. The eﬀect of the parameter s on the force-displacement relation is illustrated in
Figure 5.15.
==PAGE:72==
48
5 Numerical Model of the Friction Damper
x
Fd
s
s
s
Level = 1
Level = 2
Figure 5.15: Illustration of the eﬀect of the parameter s on the force-displacement relation.
As the displacement changes, the angle κ changes. Therefore, it is convenient to introduce
an angle change η based on the change in displacement through κ, and a change in angle
from the previous calculation step, ∆ηn+1. It is also convenient to formulate the ﬁrst level
displacement interval, s, also in terms of angle change interval, ηs. These formulations are
given as:
ηn+1 = κn+1 −κn,
∆ηn+1 = ηn+1 −ηn
and
ηs = κs/2 −κ−s/2
(5.37)
In order to make the calculation procedure simple and more understandable, the force
calculation is divided into two contributions; A constant part (subscript Con) and a varying
part (subscript V ar). The constant part is the contribution from the two joints without a
slotted hole. As the name indicates, this contribution is constant, e.i. no interval where
the contribution is zero. The constant contribution can be calculated as for the single-
level damper model. However, equation (5.36) must be changed since only two joints will
contribute to the constant part:
FdCon(n+1) = M3(n+1) + M4(n+1)
l3 cos(κn+1)
(5.38)
The varying contribution of the damper force is implemented by two if -statements and a
reduced damper angle change, η∗, both implemented in an algorithm, see Algorithm 1. The
change of the reduced damper angle is the angle change of the pin in the slotted hole. From
the change of the reduced damper angle, the ﬁrst if -statement is formulated. If the angle
change exceeds the angle change interval ηs of the ﬁrst level, the second level is reached and
a contribution from the varying part is added to the damper force, and the reduced angle
does not increase until motion changes direction. The second if -statement is formulated
in order indicate when the damper goes back to the ﬁrst level. This change is formulated
based on the change of sign of the force. The algorithm is stated in Algorithm 1 and the
deﬁnitions of level 1 and 2 is illustrated in Figure 5.15.
When the second level is activated and the varying part of the damper force is added,
equation (5.39) to (5.41) are applied. For equation (5.39) to (5.41) i is a counter that starts
==PAGE:73==
5.3 Multi-level Implementation
49
from 1 each time the second level is reached in order to restart the dynamic friction model.
Only one of the remaining joints contributes to the varying part of the force. The joint with
the slotted connection has not been given a friction material and pre-tension force.

The calculation procedure is stated and described for the tested 4 joint damper design.
If the damper design is changed to have more sets of short plates as the 5000 kN damper
described and tested by Mualla et al. (2010), the formulas can easily be corrected by adding
contributions from the additional joints.

==PAGE:75==
5.4 Updating of the Numerical model
51
5.4
Updating of the Numerical model
When comparing the results obtained from the numerical model and the experimentally
obtained results, perfect compliance is not found. The diﬀerence is concluded to be caused by
the tolerances between the bolts and bolts holes. These tolerances mainly have 3 noticeable
eﬀects on the force-displacement relation of the dampers. A smaller force is observed over a
distance of 15-20 mm, when the motion of the damper changes direction. This observation
is indicated with the number 3 in Figure 5.1. Secondly, the slope of the damper force,
when the direction of the motion of the damper is constant, is not entirely equal to the
slope of the numerical model. Lastly, the length of the ﬁrst level interval is not entirely
equal in the numerical model and the experimental results. As stated, these diﬀerences
are concluded to be caused by the tolerances between the bolts and the bolt holes, which
ﬁts well with results and conclusions by Mualla et al. (2010). In this case, a full numerical
model of a 5000 kN rotational friction damper was established using 3D-continuum solid
elements, and calculations were carried out with varying tolerances. The mentioned eﬀect of
having tolerances between the bolts and bolt holes can all be mitigated by removing these
tolerances by using bushes. However, this will increase production costs and since the eﬀect
is quite small, it is more proﬁtable to have the very simple design.
The ﬁrst issue with a smaller force, after change in the direction of the motion, is not
implemented in the numerical model. This issue is simply accepted as a consequence of the
simplicity of the damper design. The second issue is dealt with by applying a correction
to the angle κ for the calculation of the damper force. Basically, it is observed that over
the distance of 15-20 mm, the displacement is only related to movement of the bolts in the
holes, and the short plates are not rotating. This behaviour is implemented through the
displacement for the calculation of κ. The implemented correction is based on the length of
decrease in force as the only parameter and can be changed if the tolerances are changed.
The eﬀect of the correction can be seen in Figure 5.18 and 5.19.

damper.
==PAGE:76==

5 Numerical Model of the Friction Damper
The third issue with the ﬁrst level interval being diﬀerent from experimental results is
also corrected in the numerical model. This issue depends not only on the length of the
decrease in force, but also heavily on the angle of the short plates when motion changes
direction. Thus, the correction is implemented with the length of decrease in force and a
non-linear factor based on the position of the plates. The eﬀect of the correction can be
seen in Figure 5.19. The comparison of the numerical model with the experimental results
can be seen in Section 6.5.

==PAGE:77==
CHAPTER 6
Experimental Testing
The current design of the single-level friction damper with the rotational technology and
the modiﬁed multi-level friction damper are tested in order to investigate their performance
and behaviour in diﬀerent conditions. For a friction damper, in general, it is desirable to
know its frictional behaviour in relation to the discussed friction phenomena in Chapter 3.
It is also necessary to know the general performance of the damper and the performance of
the rotational mechanism in order to verify and document that the damper is usable for
damping of structures, in the extent it is intended. Furthermore, the modiﬁed multi-level
damper is tested in order to investigate the multi-level mechanism and ﬁnd the optimal
set-up of the damper to obtain the desirable multi-level behaviour. In the present chapter
the experimental program, set-up and the results of the experimental work is presented and
discussed. All of the experimental work has been carried out in the laboratory in building
119 at the Technical University of Denmark in Kongens Lyngby.
6.1
Experimental Program & Steps
In this section, the experimental program is described. In general terms the bullet points
below describe the relations and dependencies that are investigated in the experimental
work for the single-level and the multi-level damper.
• Relation and dependency of bolt torque and force in relation to damper force with
determination of the kinematic friction coeﬃcient of the frictional material.
• Frictional behaviour of the frictional material in relation to the discussed friction
phenomena in Chapter 3.
• Dependency of cyclic frequency (velocity) on the damper force and behaviour.
• Dependency of damper displacement on the damper force, energy dissipation and
behaviour.
• Dependency of excitation duration on the damper force and behaviour. Referred to as
stability in the following.
• Dependency of temperature on the damper force and behaviour.
• Multi-level behaviour of the multi-level damper.
The single-level damper investigated in this thesis has an initial capacity around 500
kN, thus, a larger 1 MN testing machine is used for testing. The limitation of this size
of machines are the velocity of the piston in the actuator, since a lot of oil needs to be
moved in order to keep up the force. Due to this limitation, it is more diﬃcult to carry out
dynamic tests, and quasi-static cyclic tests are therefore conducted in some extent, except
==PAGE:78==
54
6 Experimental Testing
for the investigation of the dependency of the cyclic frequency on the damper force and
behaviour. For the frequency dependency investigation, dynamics tests have been carried
out both in the 1 MN machine and in a smaller machine in order to reach higher frequencies
and velocities. This subject is further described in Section 6.3 and 6.2.
Before the experimental program was planned, a research of the pertinent codes for
friction dampers or so-called displacement depending devices was carried out. In relation to
the acceptance criteria of the damper force, both FEMA speciﬁcations (american standards)
and Eurocode speciﬁcations (EN 15129:2009) allow variations of ± 15 % of the damper
force compared to target capacity at the time of manufacturing. In relation to testing, EN
15129 states the following on type tests for displacement depending devices:
• Type tests of devices are to be performed whenever new devices with an internal or
external geometry, materials or kind of constrains diﬀerent from those already qualiﬁed
are designed.
• In general, type tests shall be carried out on full-scale specimens.
• In general, dynamic tests shall be carried out to reproduce the actual working conditions
of the devices. If it can be demonstrated, that the velocity has negligible inﬂuence,
quasi-static tests can be carried out.
• Evaluation of the force-displacement cycle.
Increasing amplitude cycles shall be
imposed, at 25 %, 50 % and 100 % of the maximum displacement, which shall be at
least equal to the design amplitude. Five cycles for each intermediate amplitude and
at least ten cycles for the maximum amplitude shall be applied. The device shall not
break and shall keep its characteristics unchanged during tests.
• Eﬀects of ageing, temperature and cycling frequency shall be evaluated on the proto-
type by repeating the evaluation of the force-displacement cycle in diﬀerent conditions
speciﬁed by the Structural Engineer, or on the core mechanism, elements or mate-
rials. In the latter case, the eﬀects on the overall behaviour of the device shall be
quantitatively evaluated.
The performance criteria of a damper in relation to excitation duration and number of
excitations are often discussed in terms of number of cycles the damper should be able to
endure without degradation of performance. In order to have a reference for the stability test
the EN 15129 guidelines are used, but with 20 cycles instead of 10 with a cyclic amplitude
of 100% of the maximum displacement. The maximum displacement during these tests are
considered to be 70 mm, since this value also is used in present projects in Damptech A/S
and for CE-marking of the current single-level damper.
In table 6.1, the experimental program for testing of the single-level and multi-level
friction dampers are described in terms of procedure and steps. Two specimens of the
single-level damper and one specimen of the multi-level damper are tested. Alongside the
thesis, the author has carried through minor tests of around twenty other specimens of the
single-level damper. All of these dampers behaved similarly to the results in the present
chapter.
==PAGE:79==
6.1 Experimental Program & Steps
55
Table 6.1: Experimental program for testing of the single-level and the multi-level friction
dampers.
1.
Torque, bolt force and friction coeﬃcient test
The torque of the bolts are set to a low value by use of a torque wrench. A 3
cycle quasi-static test is run, where the force in the bolts are measured and
a representative value is noted. From the measured values, the kinematic
friction coeﬃcient is found by using the numerical model. The procedure
is repeated until the design force of the damper is reached (500 kN at zero
displacement). During the tests, the temperature of the damper is kept as
constant as possible.
2.
Displacement test
The damper is tested quasi-static by a sinusoidal displacement load with an
increasing amplitude for every cycle. The used load can be seen in Figure 6.1
(a). During the tests, the temperature of the damper is kept as constant as
possible.
3.
Frequency test
The damper is tested by a sinusoidal displacement load with an increasing
frequency for every cycle. The used load can be seen in Figure 6.1 (b).
Furthermore, a smaller single-level friction damper model with larger friction
pads is tested in a smaller machine in order to reach higher relative velocities
between the sliding surfaces. The work has been carried out by Tarasaitis
(2016) with a slightly diﬀerent procedure. The obtained results are re-analysed
in the present thesis.
4.
Stability test
The type test according to EN 15129 is used. A 30 cycle quasi-static test
is run, where the ﬁrst 5 cycles are with an amplitude of 17.5 mm, the next
5 cycles are with an amplitude of 35 mm and the last 20 cycles are with a
amplitude of 70 mm.
5.
Temperature test
After the stability test, the damper will be as hot as it will ever get during
working conditions. A 3 cycle quasi-static test is run with the high tempera-
ture, and the temperature is noted. When the damper is cold again a new 3
cycle quasi-static test is run and the temperature is noted.

6 Experimental Testing
6.2
Experimental Setup & Performance
All tests of the 4 joint single-level and multi-level friction damper are conducted in the
same 1 MN Instron 4495 machine. Another smaller and more suitable machine is used
for calibration of the bolt with strain gauges, described in Chapter 4. For testing of the
mentioned frequency tests of the smaller V-shape friction damper, a smaller 250 kN Instron
8502 machine is used, where a higher frequency is achievable. The experimental set-up in
these three machines and their performances are described in the following subsections.
6.2.1
Measurement of Bolt Force
In order to calibrate the bolt with strain gauges and obtain the exact force to a given
resistance through the strain gauges, the bolt is tested by a simple uni-axial tension test.
For this test, a 60 ton uni-axial servo-hydraulic Mohr+Federhaﬀ+Losenhausen machine is
used. This machine is used due to the suitable mounting grips of the machine, where the
bolt can be tensioned without clamping the tread or having a unreliable clamping of the
bolt head. The experimental set-up of the bolt in the testing machine can be seen in Figure
6.2.
Bolt nut
Bolt head
Bolt mounted in test machine
Figure 6.2: Picture with description of the experimental set-up for calibration of the bolt
with strain gauges.
The bolt with strain gauges is tested by pulling it twice in steps from 0 to 200 kN. In
each step, the force is increased just a bit, and when the machine has stabilised, the force
shown on the machine is noted. The voltage through the strain gauges is measured by a
separate computer controlled data logger. For each force step, a value of the voltage is
noted as well. When both calibration tests have been carried out the obtained results of
force over voltage are plotted, and a linear relation of the data is made. The experimental
results and the linear ﬁt are plotted in Figure 6.3. In both tests, the exact same results are
==PAGE:81==
6.2 Experimental Setup & Performance
57
obtained with an exact linear relation. Based on this observation, it is concluded that the
measurement of force through this set-up with use of strain gauges is very precise. However,
when the bolt is mounted in the damper, larger variations of the force are observed over
the cyclic displacement of the damper. These variations are concluded to be caused by
torsional clouding of the measurements and temperature inﬂuence. Due to variations and
the necessity of using a mean value for the bolt force over each displacement cycle, the
precision of the bolt force is degraded. It is assessed that the precision is still within ± 5 %.

As discussed in Chapter 4, the DIN931 bolts were found not to be suitable for pre-
tensioning application even though the force is low in relation to the strength of the bolt.
Due to time and ﬁnancial issues, a new bolt of the correct kind with strain gauges were not
made, and the DIN931 bolt with strain gauges were only used for investigation of the relation
between torque, damper force and friction coeﬃcient. For the remaining experimental work,
recently acquired hot dip galvanised EN 14399-3 (HR) bolts were used.
6.2.2
Main Damper Testing Set-up
As previously mentioned, the main testing machine, for the 4 joint rotational friction
damper, is the 1 MN computer controlled uni-axial servo-hydraulic Instron 4495 machine.
The dampers are simply mounted in the testing machine by use of pin-bolts and specially
made connectors, which are made to ﬁt the top and bottom part of the machine. In order
to control the tests and handle the data, one computer is used to control the machine, one
computer is used to log data of the voltage in the strain gauge bolt and one computer is used
to control the numerical model in order to ensure that everything is working as intended.
The experimental set-up of the 4 joint damper in the Instron machine can be seen in Figure
6.4.
The ﬁrst tests in this machine had precarious results with a very low damper force. The
reason was found to be the use of a wrong electronic calibration ﬁle loaded after restart of
the machine in conjunction with rearrangement of machines in the laboratory. Thus, the
machine was calibrated using a load cell for calibration and the experimental program was
==PAGE:82==
58
6 Experimental Testing
started over. The calibration showed a deviation of up to 0.26 % at a load of 500 kN, which
is excellent. The calibration can be seen in Appendix A.3.
The stroke of the actuator is 250 mm. The damper was mounted at center position
enabling a cyclic amplitude of up to 125 mm. As earlier mentioned, this size of testing
machine has quite low velocities of the actuator and relative low velocities were used. This
matter is further discussed in section 6.3. The overall performance of the machine is assessed
to be excellent.

6.3
Experimental Results and Analysis
In this section, the obtained experimental results are presented, discussed and analysed.
First, the experimental program is carried out for the single-level damper. This is done in
order to get knowledge about the behaviour of the damper under more simple circumstances,
before developing the design, and to investigate if any optimisation of the design could
be made and used for the design of the multi-level damper. Furthermore, the current
single-level damper has not been tested, investigated and documented as extensively as
done with the present experimental program. In the following the experimental results
are presented, discussed and analysed for single-level damper and then for the multi-level
damper. All tests have been carried out on two single-level dampers and one multi-level
damper. For the two single-level dampers, the results have been identical, thus, in most
cases only results of one damper is given. All experimental results are presented in form of
graphical presentation.
6.3.1
Testing of Single-Level Damper

Coeﬃcient and Damper Force
The relation between the damper behaviour and the torque of the bolts was investigated as
described in Table 6.1. Figure 6.6 shows the obtained results and the relation of the damper
force for every increase in applied torque.

6.3 Experimental Results and Analysis
61
For the last two tests with torques of 500 Nm and 550 Nm, a small decrease in force
appeared at the top right corner of the hysteria plots. This small decrease is caused by the
behaviour of the DIN 931 bolts discussed in Chapter 4. It can also be observed that the
torque is relative low in relation to damper force, which is also caused by the behaviour
of the DIN 931 bolts. The goal of the present test was to investigate the relation between
torque and force in bolt, investigate linearity of involved parameters and to get the friction
coeﬃcient of the friction pads. Due to the problems with the bolts, it was not possible to
establish a representative friction coeﬃcient from the conducted tests with the single-level
damper. However, the friction coeﬃcient of the friction pads was successfully obtained in
the identical tests with the multi-level damper and is described in Section 6.3.2.
From Figure 6.7 it can be concluded that a linear relation is existing between the force
in bolt and torque, damper force and force in bolt and damper force and torque. It can also
be observed from the b values of the linear regressions, y = ax + b, that the force in the
bolts may be a bit underestimated and torque may a bit overestimated. The linearity of the
involved parameters are an important feature to establish in order to design new dampers
with diﬀerent characteristics, and to have a reliable and stable device. In Section 6.3.2 the
relation between the torque and the force in the bolts is discussed further.

described in Table 6.1. Figure 6.8 shows the obtained results and the relation of the damper
force for every increase in damper displacement.

f(x) = 2.15x −10.8

Displacement amplitude [mm]
Energy dissipation [kJ]
Figure 6.9: Experimental results of the relation between energy dissipation and displacement
amplitude from the displacement test of the single-level damper.
From Figure 6.9, it is very clear, that the energy dissipation is linearly dependent on the
displacement amplitude. The fact that the b value of the linear regressions, y = ax + b, is a
negative value is due to the insigniﬁcantly non-linearity caused by rotational behaviour of
the damper and the reduced area of hysteria caused by tolerances, which are not changed
with the magnitude of the amplitude. This relation is one of the more important properties
in order to classify the damper as a displacement depending device. It is concluded that the
single-level 4 joint damper is linearly dependent of the displacement.
Investigation of the Relation Between Frequency and Damper Force
The relation between the cyclic frequency and the damper force was investigated as described
in Table 6.1. Figure 6.10 shows the obtained results and the relation of the damper force
for every increase in cyclic frequency. As earlier mentioned, the testing machine is quite
large and therefore not able to move oil fast enough to control higher velocities and run
with higher frequencies. This issue is clearly observed in Figure 6.10. When the frequency
exceeds 0.05 hz the machine starts to overshoot the target displacement, and when the
frequency exceeds 0.175 hz the machine starts to undershoot the target displacement. Due
to this issue, only frequencies of up to and including 0.05 hz can be used with an amplitude
displacement of 40 mm, and the velocity is only increased for frequencies up to and including
0.2 hz. Figure 6.11 shows the hysteria plots for the ﬁve frequencies, where the machine is
able to maintain the target displacement amplitude of 40 mm.

Figure 6.12: Experimental results of the relation between damper force and cyclic frequency

When analysing the relation described by Figure 6.12, it becomes quite clear that the
damper force increases a bit, when the frequency is increased from 0.01 hz to 0.05 hz. From
0.05 hz the change in damper force is almost non-existing. Based on this observation, it was
concluded that all future capacity tests of the 4 joint damper, with the same size of friction
pads, should be run with a frequency of 0.05 hz and a displacement amplitude of 40 mm.
The relation between damper force and cyclic frequency is further discussed in Section 6.3.2.
Investigation of the Damper Performance when Tested According to EN
15129:2009
The stability of the damper force was investigated as described in Table 6.1, when subjected
to many load cycles. Figure 6.13 shows the obtained hysteria plots, when tested according
to EN 15129:2009.
From the results, no degradation in the capacity of the damper is found for the 5 cycles
at 25 % and 50 % of design displacement, respectively. During the 20 cycles at 100 % design
displacement, a small degradation in force is observed as the damper gets warmer. The
change in temperature is indicated by a darker line for higher temperatures and the matter
is further discussed in the following section. Several 20 cycle tests have been carried out
with the single-level damper and degradations of the energy dissipation in all cases were
found between 2 to 9 %, where all cases but one is found to be less than 6 %. The demand
for this test is that the device shall not break and shall keep its characteristics unchanged.
The demand is clearly fulﬁlled even though the amount of cycles are increased compared to
Eurocode demands.

Investigation of the Relation Between Temperature and Damper Force
The relation between the damper temperature and the damper force was investigated as
described in Table 6.1. Figure 6.14 shows the obtained results and the relation of the damper
force, when it is working at room temperature, and when it is working at a temperature as
high as it will get during a very long load series.
As mentioned in the previous section, a small decrease in force was observed during
the 20 cycles with an amplitude of 70 mm in the stability test shown in Figure 6.13. For
each of these 20 cycles, the energy dissipated in the respective cycles are calculated and
plotted in relation to the cycle number and a representative temperature of the damper,
see Figure 6.15. The temperature is measured on the outside of the long plates, and is
therefore a lot less than the temperature of the sliding interfaces of the friction pads, which
is rather impossible to measure. The idea of the measured temperature is to have a relative
temperature range, where the damper is as hot as it will get, when a temperature of 85-90o
is measured at the respective position of the long plates.

Temperature [o]
Energy dissipation [kJ]
Figure 6.15: Experimental results of the relation between energy dissipation and number of
cycles, and the relation between energy dissipation and temperature from the stability test
of the single-level damper.
The ﬁrst test of the two tests plotted in Figure 6.14 is carried out with the damper being
warm, which is where the force is less. After the test of the warm damper it is cooled down
and the exact same test is conducted again. With this sequence of the two tests it is clear,
that the diﬀerence in force is mainly caused by the diﬀerence in temperature. The reason
for the change could be found in the interface of the sliding surfaces, where the temperature
is a lot larger. When steel gets warm the hardness and strength is reduced. If the stiﬀness
and strength of the asperities in contact are reduced, the force will decrease. From the
results shown in Figure 6.15, a clear non-linear relation between the energy dissipation and
the temperature is observed. The decrease in the illustrated case is 8.6 %, which is the
maximum decrease observed in all of the conducted tests. Normally the decrease is between
==PAGE:91==
6.3 Experimental Results and Analysis
67
2 to 6 % as earlier stated. Furthermore, not all of the decrease is caused by temperature
alone. The 2.6 % of the 8.6 % is concluded to be caused by some wear of the pads and the
damper. The relation between force degradation and temperature is discussed further in
the following section. It should be kept in mind that the degrease is very small and the
demands of the Eurocode for a test like the present is that the damper shall not break and
shall keep its characteristics unchanged.

of the bolts for the ﬁrst level. This diﬀerence is chosen in order to create a diﬀerence more
suitable for the applications of the damper. The subject is further discussed in Chapter 7.

Torque [Nm]

Figure 6.17: Experimental results with linear ﬁts for the multi-level damper of relations
between bolt force and torque, damper force and bolt force and damper force and torque.
From Figure 6.17 it can be concluded that a linear relation is existing between the force
in bolt and torque, damper force and force in bolt and damper force and torque, as for
the single-level damper. It can also be observed from the b values of the linear regressions,
y = ax + b, that the force in the damper is obtained from a position that is inﬂuenced by
the tolerances between bolts and plates.
From the results, the kinematic friction coeﬃcient was found to be 0.38-0.40, using the
numerical model of the damper. Some uncertainty is associated with this result, since it
is based on only one set of pads. To have more certainty of the friction coeﬃcient more
tests could be run. The manufacturer of the friction pads states or guaranties a friction
coeﬃcient of 0.35. Thus, the results seems to be reasonable.
For the relation between the force in the bolts and the torque, a suitable relation was
desired for design of new dampers with diﬀerent characteristics. In order to ﬁnd a suitable
relation diﬀerent options were considered, like the relation stated on Figure 6.17 and the
relation by Kellerman and Klein from the 1950’s, see Arvidnilsson.dk (nd). In order to make
the best decision, the parameters of inﬂuence were investigated; The diameter of the bolt,
thread pitch, friction relation of surfaces in contact and the method for pre-tensioning. The
relation is desired to cover bolts in the range of M30 to M42, where the change in thread
pitch is negligible compared to uncertainties. The friction and the method for pre-tensioning
will also be the same. Thus, a simple relation of just one coeﬃcient multiplied with the
diameter of the bolt and the torque is proposed. The one coeﬃcient is then covering all
parameters in play.
==PAGE:93==
6.3 Experimental Results and Analysis
69
Pb = CT · dbolt · MT
(6.1)
where CT is a coeﬃcient, dbolt is the diameter of the bolt and MT is the applied moment to
the bolt nut.
In Table 6.2, experimentally obtained coeﬃcients, CT , are given. Again, these constants
are associated with some uncertainty due to the sample size and should be updated as the
sample size and experience increases with more tests. Furthermore, the greasing procedure
and amount of grease had a relative large impact on the bolt force. Thus, the greasing
procedure should be thorough and kept constant in order to have a high predictability in
damper capacities.
Table 6.2: Experimentally obtained coeﬃcients for the relation between torque and force in
bolts.
CT [-]
DIN 931 black with Nordlock washer
0.18
EN 14399-3 (HR) hot dip galvanised without washer
0.19-0.20
EN 14399-3 (HR) hot dip galvanised with Nordlock washer
0.181
Another important phenomenon in relation to bolt torque and force was observed during
the experimental work. In the time shortly after the bolts had been pre-tensioned, the
force dropped markedly. This observation is shown in Figure 6.18. The shown results are
obtained using DIN 931 bolts, and the drop might be less for other bolt types. In another
test, the pre-tension force of the bolts was investigated several minutes after the torque was
applied and after 2 weeks. No loss in pretension force was found. However, when designing
new dampers and testing dampers, this phenomenon should be taken into consideration.

When Figure 6.20 is analysed it becomes very clear that a large increase in the energy
dissipation occurs, when the second level of the friction damper is activated. For each part
of Figure 6.20, before and after the increase, a very good linear relation of displacement
is found. However, in order to satisfactory establish the linear relation of the ﬁrst part,
only based on present results, at least one more test should have been conducted in the
ﬁrst level range. The linearity of these two values are concluded based on the results of the
single-level damper. When the single-level damper is linearly dependent of the amplitude it
directly implies that the ﬁrst level part of multi-level damper is linearly dependent of the
displacement. Thus, it is concluded that the multi-level 4 joint friction damper is bi-linearly
dependent of the displacement.

starts to undershoot the target displacement. Figure 6.22 shows the hysteria plots for the
ﬁve frequencies, where the machine is able to maintain the target displacement amplitude
of 40 mm.

Cyclic frequency [hz]
Force [kN]
Figure 6.25: Experimental results of the relation between the damper force and cyclic
frequency from the frequency test of the V-shape damper.
When analysing the data presented in Figure 6.12, 6.23 and 6.25 it is clear that when
the cyclic frequency is increased from frequencies low enough to be considered as quasi
static, to frequencies high enough to be considered as dynamic, the force increases. For all
three dampers this increase in force happens from 0.01 hz to 0.05 hz. Beyond this point
the force still increases but the increase is so small that it is almost non-existing. The
reason for the small increase in force should be found either in the dynamics of the testing
machine and load cell or in the microscopic frictional behaviour between the two interacting
==PAGE:98==
74
6 Experimental Testing
surfaces discussed in Chapter 3. The described process parameters inﬂuencing the friction
behaviour in relation to velocity do not ﬁt with the observed behaviour. The behaviour
of the friction force in relation to velocity is described for constant velocity, which is far
from the testing conditions in the present tests. However, no friction lag is observed, e.i.
no change in force due to acceleration and deceleration. The reason for the behaviour of
the force with the cyclic frequency may be found with the degradation process of the pads.
When the pads are in use they produce soot, which may enter in the friction process as dry
lubrication. The microscopical behaviour of the interacting surfaces is then similar to the
behaviour described for the full ﬂuid lubrication regime, and the change in force should
be found in this mechanism of the two surfaces fully separated by the dry lubrication. It
is very important to state that the variation of the friction force and thereby the damper
force with the cyclic frequency is as small as 1-5 %. A variation of this size is without
inﬂuence and importance, and the frictional material and the dampers are concluded to be
velocity independent. Furthermore, none of the relations described by Figure 6.12, 6.23 and
6.25 shows any variations in force at higher frequencies, which indicates that the force will
remain at the same level for frequencies outside the possible test range. Even if a variation
could be found, nothing suggests, that the variation within applicable range of the friction
damper would be larger than the variation seen at low frequencies.
Investigation of the Damper Performance when Tested According to EN
15129:2009
The stability of the damper force was investigated as described in Table 6.1, when subjected
to many load cycles. Figure 6.26 shows the obtained hysteria plots, when tested according
to EN 15129:2009.
As for the single-level damper, no degradation in the capacity of the damper is found
for the 5 cycles at 25 % and 50 % of design displacement, respectively. During the 20 cycles
at 100 % design displacement, a small degradation in force is observed as the damper get
warmer. The change in temperature is indicated by a darker line for higher temperatures
and the matter is further discussed in the following section. Three 20 cycle tests have been
carried out with varying frequency, and the degradation of the energy dissipation in all
cases have been found between 2 to 7 %. The demand for this test is that the device shall
not break and shall keep its characteristics unchanged. The demand is clearly fulﬁlled even
though the amount of cycles are increased compared to Eurocode demands.
Investigation of the Relation Between Temperature and Damper Force
The relation between the damper temperature and the damper force was investigated as
described in Table 6.1. Figure 6.27 shows the obtained results of the damper force, when
it is working at room temperature and when it is working at a temperature as high as it
will get during a very long load series. As for the single-level damper, the temperature is
measured on the outside of the long plates and is therefore a lot less than the temperature
of the sliding interface of the friction pads.
Again a small decrease of the force was observed during the 20 cycles with an amplitude
of 70 mm of the stability test shown in Figure 6.26. For the multi-level damper the same
non-linear relation between force and temperature is found during the 20 cycles of the
stability test. However, in this test the temperature diﬀerence is only 20o, where it was
==PAGE:99==
6.3 Experimental Results and Analysis
75
35o for the single-level damper, and the loss in force during the 20 cycles is only 2.4 %.
Furthermore, the friction pads used in the present test had been used a lot, which stipulates
that the decrease in force due to wear will also decrease in time ensuring a long lifetime of
the energy dissipation device.

Figure 6.26: Experimental results of the stability test of the multi-level damper.
Due to the good results obtained from the stability tests of all dampers, an extra test
was conducted. Based on the curiosity to see what will happen after 20 cycles, it was chosen
to run a 200 cycle test with an amplitude of 70 mm and with a frequency of 0.01 hz. The
results of approximately every tenth cycle of the 200 are shown in Figure 6.28. During this
test the temperature on the outside of the damper changed from around 51o to 79o. For
each of these 21 cycles, the energy dissipated in the respective cycles are calculated and
plotted in relation to the cycle number, see Figure 6.29.
From the results plotted in Figure 6.28 it is clear that even though the damper is run
through 200 cycles, the force is still stable and the characteristics of the damper is unchanged.
During the 200 cycles, the dissipated energy per cycle is decreased only 6.4 %. By observing
Figure 6.29, it is found that the decrease in force and energy dissipation are beginning to
stagnate after approximate 100 cycles. This change could indicate that the temperature
also began to stagnate. The warmer the damper gets, the more energy will dissipate from
==PAGE:100==
76
6 Experimental Testing
the damper and into the air through heat radiation. At some point, the output energy will
equal the input energy, and the temperature will stagnate. Had a higher frequency been
used for the test, the temperature stagnation point would have been higher. However, no
vibration event with activation of the second level will be anywhere close to 200 cycles.
The ﬁrst level on the other hand could come close to 200 cycles if used for damping of
wind induced vibrations, but the amplitude will be very small compared to the present test
decreasing the input energy markedly. Based on these reﬂections and the present results, it
is concluded that no issues are found for using the damper for vibration control of wind
induced vibrations.

6.4
Conclusions of the Experimental Results
The design of the single-level friction damper with the rotational technology and the
modiﬁed multi-level friction damper were tested, in order to investigate their performance
and behaviour in relation to the dependency of normal force in bolts, displacement, frequency,
temperature and stability during continuous use. Furthermore, the multi-level damper was
tested extensively to ensure that the generation of a large jump in force was successfully
obtained without any complication or issues regarding the behaviour of the damper. From
the experimental program, it is concluded that the idea of generating the jump in force
through a slotted joint was successfully accomplished without any discovered complications.
Even the eﬀect of the tolerances between the bolt and plates are almost non-existing. In
regards to the behaviour and functionality of the tested friction dampers, the following
conclusions are made:
• The single-level damper is linearly dependent on displacement and the multi-level
damper is bi-linearly dependent on displacement.
• Both the single-level and the multi-level damper has a linear relation between the
normal forces of the bolts and the damper force.
• Both the single-level and the multi-level damper are independent of frequency within
applicable range.
• Both the single-level and the multi-level damper are stable in force, have no signiﬁcant
adverse impact due to heating and have no change of behavioural characteristics
during continuous use.
• None of the friction phenomena described in Section 3 have been found to have a
signiﬁcant adverse eﬀect on the characteristics of the dampers.
==PAGE:102==
78
6 Experimental Testing
6.5
Comparison of the Numerical Model with
Experimental Results
In order to verify and check that the numerical model is able to describe the behaviour of the
damper, the numerical model is compared to the experimental results. The comparisons can
be seen in Figure 6.30, 6.31 and 6.32. From the comparisons, it is clear that the numerical
model is able to describe the behaviour of the damper, except for the small decrease in
force, when the motion of the damper changes direction. As earlier mentioned this issue is
simply accepted as an eﬀect of the simplicity of the damper design. The force level in the
comparisons are matching perfectly. This is because there is always at least one unknown in
the torque, pre-tension force and friction coeﬃcient relation, that are found by matching
the force level. If the mean estimated values mentioned in this report are used to create
new damper models, it is expected that the resulting damper force will be within ± 10 %
of the experimental results when testing the damper. Most of this deviation is related to
deviations between torque and pre-tension force.

==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 7
Numerical Simulation of
Vibration Control
Enhancement
This chapter of the thesis is a feasibility study of the multi-level friction damper in comparison
with the single-level friction damper. For this study, the behaviour of a ﬁctive building with a
traditional frame structure, with the two diﬀerent friction dampers, is numerically simulated
using non-linear time history analyses. The idea of the multi-level friction damper is to
accommodate multi-functional demands of the damper. In this feasibility study, it is chosen
to focus on and simulate if the multi-level damper can be used to ensure the structural
integrity during earthquakes by reducing displacements, and to mitigate comfort criteria
during wind storms by reducing accelerations. The idea of this particular multi-functionality
is requested by Damptech’s clients, and thus focussed on in this study. Furthermore, in
order to clearly assess the diﬀerences in the amplitude and intensity dependence of the
single-level damper and the novel multi-level damper, and to assess the possible applications
of the novel multi-level damper, simulations based on relative energy dissipation in relation
to vibrations intensities are also carried out.
In the following sections, the non-linear time history calculation approaches are set up
and described with following clariﬁcation of the parameters of the feasibility study in terms
of structure and loads. Then methods for optimisation of the single-level and the multi-level
damper are proposed and investigated, in order to obtain damper settings that can achieve
optimal vibration control of the structure in relation to the respective performance criteria.
Finally, results of the study are represented with assessment of applications of the multi-level
damper.
7.1
Time History Analysis with Implementation of
Non-linear Damper Force
In order to investigate the possibilities of the multi-level damper, it is necessary to carry out
time history analyses with implementation of the damper. In order to do so, several diﬀerent
options have been assessed, such as non-linear ﬁnite element calculation using a commercial
program and Matlab programming using modern algorithms for the non-linear dynamic
calculations. Since the position of the boundaries of the ﬁrst level interval of displacement
changes, it is not possible to use commercial ﬁnite element programs, because they simple
do not have an element that possesses this feature. However, it has been assesses that an
experienced user of the single-level friction element will be able to put two of these elements
in series, and then limit the stroke of the ﬁrst element by using inﬁnitely stiﬀelements and
==PAGE:106==
82
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
contact modelling. In the end, Matlab programming is chosen in order to make sure that all
calculations can be carried out with the desired features.
For the solution of Matlab programming using modern algorithms, various non-linear time
integration methods have been assessed (Lu et al., 2006; Krenk, 2014; Chien et al., 2014;
Wang et al., 1998; Chopra, 2012). For traditional friction damping with single-level friction,
it is found most eﬃcient to use a discrete-time state-space algorithm with iteration of damper
forces based on simple assumptions like the method by Lu et al. (2006). Thus, it is used for
the time history analyses with the single-level friction damper. This method is described in
Section 7.1.4. For the novel multi-level damper, it was investigated if this algorithm could
be extended to cover the second-level behaviour. However, it was found that the extension
would ruin the simpleness and eﬃciency of the algorithm and require a massive extension
in order to cover the respective multi-level behaviour for multi-degree-of-freedom systems.
Therefore, a more general approach using the non-linear formulation of Newmark’s time
integration method with Newton-Raphson iteration is chosen. This method is described in
Section 7.1.3 and is found to be very robust, but less eﬃcient. If the presented multi-level
damper element becomes popular, it is recommended that a new algorithm is developed
based on a formulation in discrete-time state-space like Lu et al. (2006) or Krenk (2014) with
a simple and eﬃcient iteration algorithm based on appropriate assumptions. In the following
section, the dynamic system with implementation of the damper element is presented. When
formulating the non-linear time integration method to cover the system described in the
following section, the required knowledge is to some extent found in Chopra (2012), Krenk
(2013) and Lu et al. (2006).
7.1.1
Equation of Motion with Damper Implementation
When formulating the system of second-order diﬀerential equations to be solved, the
traditional linear system with the inertia term, viscous damping term, stiﬀness term and
load is left unchanged. The non-linear damping is then simply implemented through an
additional term, B fd(x(t)).
M ¨x(t) + C ˙x(t) + K x(t) + B fd(x(t)) = E w(t) ∨p(t)
(7.1)
where, M, C and K are the system matrices of the linear system, B is the damper placement
matrix, fd(x(t)) is the non-linear damper force, E is the excitation matrix, w(t) is the
ground motion and p(t) indicates that the system can be subjected to any given load.
When the damper is installed in a structure, it is placed with a supporting structure like
a chevron bracing as illustrated in Figure 4.3. When the damper is in its stick state, it adds
stiﬀness to the dynamic system. When the force on the damper element exceeds the slip
force (sf) the friction element starts to slide and energy is dissipated. This behaviour of the
single-level friction element is illustrated in Figure 7.1 (a) and (b). The novel multi-level
friction element behaves in the same way, except the movement of the ﬁrst-level friction
element is limited within an interval. When the boundaries of the ﬁrst-level friction interval
is reached, the damper goes back to its stick state and a higher force is required to overcome
the slip force of the second-level. Thus, it requires that sf1(i) < sf2(i). The novel multi-level
friction element is illustrated in 7.1 (c).
==PAGE:107==

With the described equation of motion, it implies that when the eﬀect of the implemented
dampers are compared to vibration levels without damping devices, it is compared to
vibration levels for a structure that stays in the linear elastic regime. This procedure is
usually used by manufacturers of friction dampers when illustrating the eﬀect of the damper.
However, when designing structures for earthquakes today with a return period of 475 years,
energy dissipation through plastic deformation is allowed. When designing structures with
friction dampers, the structures are designed to stay within the elastic regime. Therefore,
it can be argued that when illustrating the diﬀerence of having friction dampers or not, it
may not be fair to compare the results of having friction dampers with results from linear
elastic calculations. In this case the investigated subject involves the diﬀerence of having
single- or multi-level friction dampers, thus, this discussion becomes irrelevant for the work
of this thesis.
7.1.2
Newton-Raphson Iteration
In order for the chosen time integration method, Newmark’s method, to be extended to
non-linear systems, an iteration scheme is required. The iteration scheme is implemented
in order to iterate the damper force within each time step, which is an implicit non-linear
function of the unknown displacement. For the present time integration method, it is chosen
to use the Newton-Raphson Iteration scheme. This iteration scheme is used in most modern
commercial ﬁnite element programs and is very robust and easy to implement for most
non-linear problems. Had any other non-linearities been introduced to the model, like
non-linear materials, the same iteration scheme could have been applied. In order to present
the basics of the Newton-Raphson Iteration scheme in a simple way, the method is described
for a static analysis of a non-linear single-degree-of-freedom system.
==PAGE:109==
7.1 Time History Analysis with Implementation of Non-linear Damper Force
85
For static analysis of a non-linear single-degree-of-freedom system, the inertia and the
damping term of the equation of motion are dropped. Therefore, the non-linear equation to
be solved is:
fs(x) = p
(7.2)
The objective of the iteration scheme is to ﬁnd the displacement, x, of equation (7.2). The
iteration scheme is developed in such a way that after j cycles of iteration of the unknown
displacement, x(j), provides an improved estimate of the displacement, x(j+1). In order to
do so, the resisting force, f(j+1)

, is expanded by a Taylor series about the known estimate
of the displacement, x(j).

From the improved estimate of the displacement, x(j+1), a new residual force is found and
the iteration is continued until convergence is achieved. The Newton-Raphson iteration
method is illustrated in Figure 7.4.
In order to determine when the displacement, x(j+1), is found with high enough precision,
a convergence criteria is needed. The convergence criteria can be formulated based on the
residual force, the displacement change or by incremental work. With the elasto-double-
plastic behaviour of the damper, the residual force is found to be the most appropriate
criteria, since the displacement change can be rather large without having any residual force.
In this way the elasto-double-plastic behaviour is utilised in order to increase calculation
eﬃciency. The following convergence criteria is used.

In the literature, a modiﬁed Newton-Raphson Iteration method can be found, where a
new tangent stiﬀness is not calculated within each iteration step. The same tangent stiﬀness
is simply used throughout the iteration in order to increase calculation eﬃciency. With the
elasto-double-plastic behaviour it is more beneﬁcial to calculate a new tangent stiﬀness for
each iteration, thus, the original formulation stated in this section is used.
7.1.3
Time Integration Method
As mentioned in the introduction of this section a non-linear expansion of the Newmark
time stepping method is used for time history analyses of the simulation of the vibration
control enhancement. The derivation and an elaborate description of the linear Newmark
time stepping method is not given in this thesis, and it is assumed that the reader has some
knowledge on the subject. For more see Krenk (2013).
In 1959, N. M. Newmark developed a family of time stepping methods all based on the
following equations:
˙xn+1 = ˙xn + (1 −γ) ∆t ¨xn + γ ∆t ¨xn+1
(7.9)
xn+1 = xn + ∆t ˙xn + (1
2 −β) ∆t2 ¨xn + β ∆t2 ¨xn+1
(7.10)
where ∆t is the time increment within each time step and the parameters 0 < γ < 1 and
0 < β < 1
2 are parameters of the algorithm determining the degree of forward weighting. If
the parameters are set to γ = β = 0 it correspond to full backward weighting, while γ = 1 and
β = 1
2 correspond to full forward weighting. For all calculations in relation to the simulations
in the present thesis, γ = 1
2 and β = 1
4 are used, which correspond to the assumption of
constant average acceleration within each time step. This formulation of the Newmark
procedure leads to bounded solutions meaning that the procedure is unconditionally stable
regardless of the time step length. Furthermore, it involves a normalised time delay within
each period of
1
12(ω∆t)2. Another option is setting γ = 1
2 and β = 1
6, which correspond to
the assumption of linear variation of the acceleration within each time step. However, this
==PAGE:111==
7.1 Time History Analysis with Implementation of Non-linear Damper Force
87
formulation is only conditionally stable, meaning it requires a small enough time step length,
but the normalised time delay within each period is halved compared to the assumption of
constant average acceleration. For more on the derivation of Newmark’s method and an
elaborate description of stability and computational error, see Krenk (2013).
For determination of the necessary time step length, the knowledge about the numerical
error is used and the rule of thumb implying that a maximum length of 1
20Tmin should be used.
However, in this case with non-linear hysteretic damping, the structural frequencies changes
throughout the time history analysis. If the dampers are not activated, the frequencies
are increased relative to the added stiﬀness from the bracing structure supporting the
dampers. If the dampers are activated the frequencies are not increased and energy is
dissipated through work of the dampers. When determining the time step length, the
increased frequencies of the structure is used.
The original formulation of the Newmark procedure is organised with a prediction
of xn+1 and ˙xn+1 from which the acceleration ¨xn+1 is obtained, and a correction of the
predicted xn+1 and ˙xn+1 values are made with the obtained value of the acceleration ¨xn+1.
For the non-linear calculation, a diﬀerent approach is taken, since the damper force is a
non-linear function of xn+1. Thus, in short terms, the displacement xn+1 is found through
iteration and then the velocity and acceleration is obtained by the following rearrangement
of equation (7.9) and (7.10).

where ||·|| denotes the Euclidean norm of the vector. For an n-dimensional Euclidean space

(7.26)
The non-linear damper force has two non-linearities; the elasto-double-plastic behaviour
and the history dependency of the position of the boundaries of the interval, where the
ﬁrst level friction element can operate, e.i. history dependence of when the damper element
has to overcome the second slip force. The ﬁrst non-linearity is simply solved through
the iteration, where the damper force is corrected if it increases the respective slip force.
The exact implementation of the force correction can be found in Algorithm 3. When a
damper slips in the ﬁrst level or second level, the stiﬀness goes to zero, thus, the damper
stiﬀness must be updated. Kb+d is introduced in the calculations as a matrix containing the
damper stiﬀnesses in the diagonal. If all dampers are in stick state the tangential stiﬀness
contribution of the dampers and bracing structures to the system is:
KT = B Kb+d BT
(7.27)
If all dampers are in slip state the tangential stiﬀness contribution of the dampers and
bracing structures to the system is:
KT = 0
(7.28)
If the dampers are in mixed states of slip and stick, the respective damper and bracing
stiﬀness of the dampers in slip state is set to zero in equation (7.27). The implementation
of the change in stiﬀness can be found in Algorithm 3.
The second non-linearity is dealt with by calculating the position of the ﬁrst friction
element for each iteration within each time step. If a boundary is reached, the second slip
force has to be exceeded in order for the damper to reach the slip state again. In order
to know the position of the ﬁrst friction element, the total relative displacements of the
damper elements have to be obtained by:
y(j)
n+1 = BT x(j)
n+1
(7.29)
The relative displacements of the friction part of the damper elements (right part of Figure
7.1 (c)) are then found by:
v(j)
n+1 = y(j)
n+1 −(Kb+d)−1 f (j)
d(n+1)
(7.30)

If a ﬁrst level friction element reaches a boundary the increase in relative displacement of

In order to determine when the damper is in the stick state and the slip state of level
1 and 2, an indicator is build into the calculation procedure. This indicator is simply
determining, when the damper is in the deﬁned level 1 or 2 according to Figure 7.3. The
calculation procedure should be able to run on random vibrations without any errors, which
mixed together with small numerical errors increases the diﬃculties of ﬁnding a suﬃciently
robust formulation of the indicator. From testing of several formulations based on damper
displacement, velocity and force, an airtight solution was found. The indicator is based on
two if-statements determining the change from one level to the other. If the displacement
of the ﬁrst friction element is equal the respective boundary, the indicator goes to level 2.
If the damper force up-crosses or down-crosses the slip load of level 1, the indicator goes
to level 1, see Figure 7.3. The algorithmic formulation of the indicator is given in Algorithm 2.
It should be noted that the solution for each time step is not exact, because equilibrium
is only satisﬁed at the beginning and end of each time, and not within the respective time
step. Therefore, the so-called energy balance equation is violated and a minor energy leak
is present. This have led to formulation of new time stepping methods like Krenk (2014),
which is an energy-momentum based time integration in discrete state-space. However, with
a relative small time step, the numerical error becomes negligible.

7.1.4
Time Integration Method for Traditional Single-level
Friction Damping
As earlier mentioned for the time history analysis with single-level friction damping, the
discrete-time state-space algorithm with iteration of damper forces based on simple assump-
tions by Lu et al. (2006) is used. Since this method is just employed for the calculations and
not developed further by the author, and the single-level friction is not the main subject of
the present thesis, this method is only brieﬂy presented.
The time integration method is employing equation (7.1) in state-space form. The advantage
of the state-space form is that the second-order diﬀerential equation is transformed into
a ﬁrst-order diﬀerential equation. The equation of motion in state-space form can be
represented as
˙z(t) = Ac z(t) + Bc fd(t) + Ec w(t)
(7.33)
==PAGE:117==
7.1 Time History Analysis with Implementation of Non-linear Damper Force
93
where z(t) represents the vector of state variables, Ac is the system matrix and Bc and Ec
are the counterparts of the damper placement matrix B and the excitation distribution
matrix E. These parts are deﬁned as
z(t) =

(7.35)
where Raxb denotes that the matrix is real-numbered with dimension of (axb). m denotes
the number of modes, q denotes the number of dampers and l denotes the number of
independent loads.
The standard analytical solution of the time-invariant dynamic system in equation (7.33),
which is also a state-space equation, is given as
z(t) = eAc(t2−t1)z(t1) +
Z t2

eAc(t2−τ)[Bc fd(τ) + Ec w(τ)]dτ
(7.36)
where t1 and t1 is the initial and end time.
It can then be proven that the analytical solution of the time invariant dynamic system can
be transformed into the following diﬀerence equation
zn+1 = A zn + B0 un + B1 un+1 + E0 wn + E1 wn+1
(7.37)
where A = eAc∆t is the discrete-time system matrix. B0 and B1 are the discrete-time
friction force matrix of the previous and current time step. E0 and E1 are the discrete-time
earthquake loading matrix of the previous and current time step. The time integration
method can be used for any loading by setting w(t) = p(t) and E = I. The matrices of the
diﬀerence equation are deﬁned as

The non-linear damper force is then dealt with through a three folded algorithm. Initially,
for all time steps, the dampers are assumed to be in their stick state. Then the damper
states are re-evaluated and one of three approached is taken; All dampers are correctly
in stick state, all dampers are in slip state or the dampers are in mixed states. If all the
dampers are in slip state, the force in the dampers are simply set to their respective slip
forces. If the dampers are in mixed states, an iterative procedure is taken until the correct
states of all dampers are found. For a full description of the algorithm and the iterative
procedure see Lu et al. (2006).
==PAGE:118==

7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
7.2
Fictive Structure and Dynamic Model for
Simulation
The feasibility study of the multi-level damper is carried out using a ﬁctive building. It is
desired for the study, that the governing parameters and conditions are as representative
for real life conditions as possible. Therefore, wind tunnel test data is used for simulation of
the wind load, and in this case, it is chosen to use existing results from wind tunnel test of
a 10 cm cube, see Figure 7.12. When using these wind tunnel test data, the outer geometry
of the building for the simulation is ﬁrmly set based on the size of the wind tunnel model
and the length of the eddies in the wind tunnel wind ﬂow. In this speciﬁc situation it is
required that the ﬁctive building is a 10 ± 5 m cube. It is therefore chosen to have a four
storey 13 m tall cubic building for the simulations.
The structural system of a very simple structure, as used for full scale experimental
benchmark studies of dampers at the National Center for Research on Earthquake Engineer-
ing (NCREE) in Taipei, Taiwan, is used (Loh et al., 2008). It is stressed that this structure
is very simple and building features like stairs, elevators and utility shafts are not taken
into consideration. Since it is a ﬁctive building for simulation purposes only, some liberty is
employed when designing the building in order to scale its natural frequencies. The purpose
of the feasibility study is not to design buildings, but to assess the possible vibration control
enhancement of the multi-level damper. Thus, the emphasis is on creating a structure
with frequencies in applicable range of multi-level damper with a realistic behaviour and a
realistic main structural system.
In the following list, the main design and dimensions of the structural system of the
ﬁctive building are described.
• Outer columns consist of HE360B proﬁles.
• Center column consists of HE400B proﬁles and is rotated 90o around center axis
compared to outer columns.
• Beams consist of HE280B proﬁles.
• Composite slabs of 150 mm reinforced concrete and HE120A proﬁles.
• All joints between columns and beams are considered clamped.
• Façade is assumed to be made of glass supported by a minor steel structure.
In order to give the reader a better understanding of the ﬁctive structure used for the
simulations, a CAD 3D model is made, see Figure 7.5. The fundamental frequency of the
structure will be much lower for one direction than the other, due to diﬀerence in moment of
inertia between axes of the HEB-proﬁles. For the feasibility study, oscillation with bending
of the proﬁles purely about their weak axis is only considered. Wind induced vibrations are
most severe for low frequencies, thus, this direction will mainly be of interest.
==PAGE:119==
7.2 Fictive Structure and Dynamic Model for Simulation
95
Figure 7.5: 3D rendered view and section of the main structural system of the ﬁctive
building for the simulation of possible vibration control enhancement.
Loads for inertia eﬀects are set equal in the case of simulation of earthquake eﬀects
and wind induced vibrations even though, they are not ascribed to the same limit state.
This is chosen in order to have similar conditions throughout the comparisons and not to
have too many parameters changing from one simulation to the next. It is however assessed
that the diﬀerence is quite small and therefore insigniﬁcant. It is chosen to use the deﬁned
combination scheme from EN 1998-1:
X
j≤1
Gk,j ” + ”
X
i≤1
ψE,i · Qk,i
(7.40)
where Gk,j is the characteristic dead-load of the structure and ﬁxtures, Qk,i is the charac-
teristic live-load and
ψE,i = ϕ · ψ2,i
(7.41)
where ϕ = 0.8 for live-load on storeys and ϕ = 1.0 for live-load on roof. Values of ψ2,i can
be found in EN 1990. The magnitude of the loads are found based on the assumptions of
the building being an oﬃce building.
When only considering vibrations with bending of the proﬁles purely about their
weak axis, the 3D structure reduces to a 2D plane frame. The frame structure without
consideration of the friction dampers are considered as an idealised linear elastic structure.
By neglecting member axial elongation eﬀects, the idealised structure has 16 degrees-of-
freedom, see Figure 7.6. Each element is modelled as a Bernoulli-Euler beam element, which
means that plane sections remain plane and normal to the deformed longitudinal axis, e.i.
no shear distortion is considered. Furthermore, all elements are considered to have uniform
stiﬀness.

Before creating the total stiﬀness matrix, a local 4x4 stiﬀness matrix is made for each
member of the 2D frame. The matrix is created by calculating the stiﬀness equal to the
forces developed, when a unit displacement is introduced to a speciﬁc degree-of-freedom.
The result of this procedure is then put into the respective position, kij, in the matrix, where
i is the force developed, when a unit displacement of degree-of-freedom j is introduced. The
stiﬀness is directly obtained, when a unit displacement is introduced without considering
axial forces, as the constitutive stiﬀness. The geometric stiﬀness is the change in stiﬀness
in relation to the magnitude and direction of the axial force. The geometric stiﬀness is
obtained by considering the change in stiﬀness, when a unit axial force is considered at a unit
displacement. It is important to mention that the forces and masses in the local matrices
are obtained through the shape functions, which are based on the previously mentioned
assumptions for the Bernoulli-Euler beam element. The following local stiﬀness matrix is
obtained and employed in the calculations (Gavin, 2016).

By investigation of the results, it is found that the inclusion of the geometric stiﬀness
only decreases the frequencies about 2 %. It indicates that the geometric stiﬀness could
have been omitted and that the columns are far from a buckling state.
The global matrices are established by using the Direct Stiﬀness Method based on
the system illustrated in Figure 7.6. Basically, when using this method each position in
the global 16x16 system matrices are found manually as described for the local matrices.
For the global stiﬀness matrix a unit displacement is introduced to a degree-of-freedom.
Then the respective contributing elements to the respective developed forces are identiﬁed
and the contributions are added together for each force. When the procedure has been
carried out for each degree-of-freedom, the global stiﬀness matrix is complete. The same
procedure applies for the formulation of the mass matrix, but instead of applying a unit
displacement, a unit acceleration is introduced. Additionally, the horizontal inertia forces
from the horizontal members (beams, slabs, ﬂoor load etc.) are added as a lumped mass to
the degree-of-freedom corresponding to translational acceleration, when applying the unit
acceleration to the respective translation.
When the system matrices for the inertia and stiﬀness terms are established, the
eigenvalue problem is solved in order to obtain eigenfrequencies and eigenvectors. Figure
7.7 illustrates the ﬁrst four mode shapes of the building.

7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
When the possible vibration control enhancement is investigated, a lot of non-linear time
history analyses are conducted. In order to make the calculations more eﬃcient, the system
matrices are reduced using the quasi-static condensation technique. When employing this
technique, direct loading of the omitted degrees-of-freedom are not possible, as it would
have been, if the master-slave reduction technique had been used. However, it is desired to
reduce the system to only four modes and the four translational degrees-of-freedom, and
the master-slave reduction technique would only reduce the system to eight modes. When
omitting direct loading of the rotational degrees-of-freedom, it involves omitting of the wind
load on the roof. The critical vibration will be translational movement of the building, and
the contribution from the loads on the roof will be minimal. Therefore, it is considered to
be fair to reduce the system to only the four translational degrees-of-freedom.
When using the quasi-static condensation technique, partitioning of the matrices and
vectors are carried out in relation to the degrees-of-freedom, that are retained in the reduced
model, xd, and the degrees-of-freedom, that are eliminated, xs. With the partitioning, the
equation of motion with an external force can be set up for a frequency dependent problem
in the following way:
 "
Kdd
Kds
Ksd
Kss
#
−iω
"
Cdd

−ω2
"

Mss
#! "

"

#
(7.44)
By neglecting the damping and inertia eﬀect (hence the name of the method) and the
external load on the omitted degrees-of-freedom the equations are reduced to
Ksdxd + Kssxs = 0
(7.45)
where the quasi-static degrees-of-freedom can be isolated to
xs = −K−1
ss Ksdxd
(7.46)
From equation (7.46), it is given that the relation between the omitted and retained degrees-
of-freedom is a static set of constraints that are based on force equilibrium. Furthermore,
it is clear that the direct loading of the degrees-of-freedom in xs is not included. The
homogeneous quasi-static relation of equation (7.46) can be described with the constraint
matrix:
S = −K−1
ss Ksd
(7.47)
With the partitioned matrices, it can be proven through the elastic and kinetic energy that
the reduced mass and stiﬀness matrix can be obtained in the following way:
¯K = Kdd + ST Ksd + KdsS + ST KssS
(7.48)
¯M = Mdd + ST Msd + MdsS + ST MssS
(7.49)
For more on the quasi-static condensation technique and its limitations see Krenk (2013).
When the system matrices for the inertia and stiﬀness terms have been reduced, the
eigenvalue problem is solved again in order to obtain eigenfrequencies and eigenvectors.

frequencies of the reduced system are stated. The frequencies of the reduced system and
the ﬁrst four frequencies of the full system are identical to a precision of 10−5.

The eﬀective modal mass is the mass eﬀective in producing the earthquake forces of the
mode, e.i. the mass of an equivalent single-degree-of-freedom system. By pre-multiplying
both sides of equation (7.50) with ϕT
j the eﬀective modal mass can be derived by the modal
mass:
meff
