= ϕT
j M ι Γj
(7.52)
The majority of codes concerning dynamic calculations of seismic eﬀects states that in order
for the dynamic model to be valid, at least 90 % of the total mass of the building must be
contained in the sum of the eﬀective modal masses of the included modes. In the present
case, 99.4 % of the total mass is contained in the sum of the eﬀective modal masses of the
four modes of the reduced system, where 89.5 %-point are allocated to mode 1, 8.3 %-point
to mode 2, 1.5 %-point to mode 3 and 0.2 % point to mode 4.
When constructing the damping matrix for the system, two options have been considered.
At ﬁrst Rayleigh damping with mass and stiﬀness proportional damping has been considered.
==PAGE:124==
100
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
When using this procedure, the amount of stiﬀness and mass proportional damping are
determined on two natural frequencies and a target damping ratio. The damping ratio of the
remaining modes will then deviate from the target damping ratio. The advantage of Rayleigh
damping is that higher modes can have high damping ratios and thereby have less eﬀect on
the calculations. However, mass proportional damping cannot be physically justiﬁed for
shear buildings (Clough and Penzien, 1995). The second method, which is chosen for this
project, is to evaluate the damping matrix directly from four desired damping ratios for
the respective modes. The procedure is carried out by considering the complete diagonal
matrix of generalised damping coeﬃcients, which is done by pre- and post-multiplying the
damping matrix with the mode shape matrix (Clough and Penzien, 1995):
c = ΦT CΦ = 2


ζ1ω1m1
ζ2ω2m2
...
ζjωjmj


(7.53)
By using the orthogonality properties of the mode shapes relative to the mass, the modal
mass mj = ϕT
j Mϕj and the modal viscous damping coeﬃcient cj = 2ζjmjωj, the damping
matrix can be constructed from the damping ratios by the following equation:
C =
N
X
j=1
(MΦ) cj
m2
j
(MΦ)T
(7.54)
Based on Table 1.4 in Krenk (2013), the damping ratio of all four modes are chosen to 2.5
%. The same damping ratio is used in the case of earthquake load and wind load.
7.3
Loads for Simulation
In this section, the basis for the earthquake load and the wind load is described in detail.
The dampers in question can be used for mitigation of other dynamics loads also, but in
relation to the scope of the thesis, only earthquake load and wind load are considered.
7.3.1
Earthquake Accelerograms
For the time history calculations with earthquake load, accelerograms are needed. For these
accelerograms two possibilities have been considered; Artiﬁcial accelerograms, simulated
based on a target spectrum and Random Vibration Theory, and real measured accelerograms
matched to a spectrum. The later is chosen, since the general opinion in the ﬁeld seems to be
that these are better, and the artiﬁcial accelerograms seems to have an unreal large amount
of energy contained in higher frequencies. Before selecting and scaling accelerograms, EN
1998-1 demands for accelerograms are investigated. The following demands are found:
• A minimum of 3 accelerograms should be used.
• The peak ground acceleration (PGA) for any of the accelerograms should not be less
than for the site in question including soil ampliﬁcation.
==PAGE:125==
7.3 Loads for Simulation
101
• In the range of periods between 0.2T1 and 2T1, where T1 is the fundamental period of
the structure in the direction, where the accelerogram will be applied; no value of the
elastic spectrum, calculated from all time histories, should be less than 90 % of the
corresponding value of the elastic response spectrum.
The target spectrum for the accelerograms are as stated the elastic response spectrum
from EN 1998-1. The spectrum can be seen in Figure 7.9. Several diﬀerent elastic response
spectra are available depending on the soil conditions and the surface-wave magnitude, Ms.
For the simulations, it is desired to have as wide a plateau of the response spectrum as
possible. When all the friction dampers are in their slip state, the frequencies of the building
are identical to the frequencies found without considering the dampers. But when all the
friction dampers are in their stick state, the frequencies are markedly increased with the
stiﬀness from the bracing structure. It is desired for the simulations that the fundamental
frequency with the dampers in their slip state is at the right edge of the plateau or just to
the right, and to have the fundamental frequency with all dampers in their stick state still
on the plateau. In this way, the eﬃciency of the dampers are not based on just removing
the total base shear by increasing the frequency markedly, but based on similar input for
both outer limit states of damper states. In order to have the widest plateau, soil type D
and type 1 spectrum (surface-wave magnitude > 5.5) are chosen.
For evaluation of the dampers and determination of the magnitude of the earthquake,
the no-collapse requirement is chosen. For this requirement, the seismic action should be
associated with a 10 % probability of exceedance in 50 years, which equals a return period of
475 years. For determination of the PGA with this reference period, the European Seismic
Hazard Map by the European Commission is used. The map can be found in Appendix A.4.
From this map, a peak ground acceleration of 0.38 g is chosen, which is the average value of
the deﬁnition of a severe earthquake. The chosen peak bedrock acceleration indicates that
the building could be placed in areas in Greece or Italy with severe seismic hazard. The
Eurocode values for these areas are around 0.36 g. Furthermore, importance class II with
γII = 1.0 is chosen. Importance class II is for ordinary buildings.
The real recorded accelerograms are supplied by Evangelos Katsanos, who is a Postdoc
at the Technical University of Denmark. From the supplied accelerograms the following
three spectrums, with similar characteristics as the chosen spectrum, are selected; Cape
Mendocino 1992, Kobe 1995 and Northridge 1994. The accelerograms should, according to
EN 1998-1, be scaled to the value of ag · S for the zone of consideration. The applied scaling
factors of the accelerograms to satisfy this demand range from 1.33-1.70, which is not the
case for these scaling factors. Too large scaling factors can result in inappropriate alternation
of the acceleragrams. From the accelerograms the respective spectra are calculated using
the Duhamel Integration Method. This method is described in appendix A.5. From the
obtained spectra, a mean spectrum is found. In Figure 7.9, the respective spectrum of each
accelerogram and the mean spectrum are compared to the Eurocode target spectrum with
indication of the 90 % boundaries.
It is clear from Figure 7.9 that scaling the accelerograms with a single factor would require
that the accelerograms all overshoot the target spectrum quite heavily, in order for all mean
values in the interval to be above the 90 % boundary. It can, however, be helped by using
more accelerograms, but another approach is taken. By using the program SeismoMatch,
from SeismoSoft the accelerograms are matched to the target response spectrum, using the
==PAGE:126==
102
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
wavelets algorithm proposed by Abrahamson (1992) and Hancock et al. (2006). The results
of the matching of the accelerograms can be seen in Figure 7.10.

7.3.2
Wind Loads
As earlier mentioned, the wind loads for the simulation are based on wind tunnel tests.
These tests have been carried out by Julian Christ in the close-circuit wind tunnel at the
Civil Engineering department of the Technical University of Denmark for his master’s thesis.
The raw data obtained from the test is scaled, transformed and analysed in the present
thesis. In Figure 7.12, the cubic wind tunnel model is illustrated with indication of points
of pressure measurements on the front side, the rear side and the top side. A still picture
with illustration of the wind ﬂow around the model is also given.
==PAGE:128==
104
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
Figure 7.12: (a) Illustration of cube with points of measurement. Rear side identical to
front side. (b) Simulation of wind ﬂow on building with laser and smoke. Courtesy of H.
Holger Hundborg Koss, Kaare Ask Skotte and Mads B. Bloch-Sørensen.
When scaling and transforming the wind data to ﬁt the full-scale model, the references
EN 1991-1-4 and Koss (2015b) are generally used. No particular statistical analysis of
the wind data has been carried out, since the main focus is the simulation of the possible
vibration control enhancement of the multi-level damper.
The ﬁrst step when scaling and transforming the wind tunnel data to ﬁt the full scale
building, is to calculate an appropriate wind velocity. In general for structural analysis, EN
1991-1-4 dictates that the characteristic value of the wind velocity is a 10 minutes mean
velocity exceeded once in 50 years (98 % fractile), and the characteristic loading eﬀect within
the 10 minutes storm is deﬁned as the 78 %-fractile of all extremes. The 10 minutes mean
velocity is measured at a height of 10 m and in terrain category II. For the present project
a 10 minutes mean wind velocity exceeded once in 50 years of 32 m/s is chosen. In general,
the 10 minutes mean wind velocity exceeded once in 50 years for real life projects is found
in the national annex of EN 1991-1-4 for the respective country. This particular wind speed
is found at the more windy coasts in Europe. For the dynamic simulation, it is the comfort
criteria in the serviceability limit state that are of interest. It is desired to investigate if
the accelerations can be mitigated by the damper in order to satisfy the comfort criteria.
These criteria are based on using a wind velocity with a return period of 10 years. The
probability factor method given by EN 1991-1-4 is used for calculating wind velocities for
diﬀerent return periods. The probability factor method in EN 1991-1-4 is given as
ub,prob = cprob · ub
(7.55)
where ub is the characteristic 10 minutes mean wind velocity for a return period of 50 years
and
cprob =
1 −K · ln(−ln(1 −p))
1 −K · ln(−ln(0.98))
n
(7.56)
where p is the probability of occurrence and K = 0.2 and n = 0.5 if not otherwise deﬁned
in the National Annex. The 10 minutes mean wind velocity exceeded once in 10 years is
calculated to 28.9 m/s.
==PAGE:129==
7.3 Loads for Simulation
105
The 10 minutes mean wind velocity is then corrected in relation to season, direction,
orography, terrain category and reference height of the full scale building. The corrections
are carried out in the following way.
um(z) = Cr(z) · Co(z) · ub
(7.57)
where Cr(z) is the roughness factor and C0(z) is the orography factor. ub is the characteristic
10 minutes mean wind speed for the return period in question corrected in relation to direction
and season:
ub = ub,0 · Cdir · Cseason
(7.58)
where Cdir is the directional factor, set to 1.0, and Cseason is the seasonal factor, set to 1.0.
The roughness factor is calculated by
Cr(z) = 0.19

where z is the height above terrain, z0 is the roughness length and z0,II is the roughness

category II.
The orography factor takes into consideration the upwind slope and is generally described as
Co =

larger than 0.3 and s can be obtained from Figure A.2 and Figure A.3 in EN 1991-1-4.
Since it is a ﬁctive project s is simply chosen to 0.5, which results in an orography factor of
Co = 1.3.
With the described calculations the characteristic 10 minutes mean wind speed at the top
of the building becomes 39.7 m/s for a return period of 10 years.
For ﬂuid mechanical similarity between test and real life, the fundamental assumption
is that the distribution of the acting forces on the model and in the ﬂow are the same
and only diﬀers in magnitude. Based on this assumption, the measured pressure on the

stagnation point on the front at around 60 % of the height, separation at the leading edge
and negative pressure on top and rear surfaces. As argued in Section 7.2, the dynamic load
on the top surface is neglected. The load on the front and rear surfaces are added together
and distributed to the four degrees-of-freedom. When distributing the load at each time
step of the load series, the façade, beams and columns are assumed to have inﬁnite stiﬀness,
in order to have a direct and easy force distribution. The mean values of the load on the
four degrees-of-freedom are illustrated in Figure 7.13 (b).

Figure 7.13: (a) Illustration of the mean values of the load on each horizontal row of tabs

The dynamic content of the load series is assessed by analysing the power spectrum
of the load on the third degree-of-freedom. The power spectrum is calculated according
to Koss (2015a), where the load spectrum is calculated by using the pwelch function in
MATLAB. In short terms, the load spectrum is a Fast Fourier transformation of the time series
of the force, which in principle transforms the time series by superposition into numerous
harmonic waves of diﬀerent frequency, amplitude and relative phase shifts to each other.
The ﬂuctuation of the load represents the dynamic part or power of the process, and when
calculating the load spectrum the power is distributed over frequency. Since the ﬂuctuation
is equivalent to the power, the area underneath the power spectral density function is set
equal to the statistical variance. Thereby, the power spectral density function reﬂects the
distribution of the variance over frequency. Furthermore, in order to satisfy time step
demands of the time stepping algorithms, the wind load series is splined using a cubic spline
interpolation by the spline function in Matlab, which also increases the frequency range of
the load series. The power spectrum is calculated and plotted in Figure 7.14.
The splining of the load series generated some minor amount of power on frequencies just
above 100 hz. This energy can occur due to the multi-sampling-factor from the lower
sampling frequency which contain power of higher frequencies. However, this minor power
was simply removed by a low-pass ﬁlter.
From the power spectral density, it is clear that the power is located in lower frequencies,
therefore a lower frequency of the building will results in a larger response. It is also clear
that the power drops markedly around 9 hz. This drop is due the sampling frequency of the
measuring equipment. The fact that the drop is a bit before the 11.4 hz stated earlier, is
assessed to be due to ﬁlter transitioning, since it is not possible to make a clean cut on one
frequency.

The measured wind load series from the wind tunnel is 32.8 minutes in full-scale, which
is too long to practically run through the non-linear calculations. Therefore, the load series
is initially run through without dampers and with the dampers in their stick state, from
which the 5 most severe segments of 66 s for both cases are chosen for the feasibility study.
Each of these segments starts and ends with full intensity resulting in two main issues,
when running the segments uncorrected through the time history analyses. Starting the
calculations directly with full intensity is, due to the mean load, like starting with a huge
impact load, causing a large unrealistic response. When ending the wind load series it is
desirable to investigate how the structure oscillation decays again, and how close it ends to
the initial position. Therefore, it is chosen to use a so-called trapezoidal intensity envelope
function. In this way, the intensity of the wind load is linearly increased to full intensity
over the ﬁrst 15 s, and then after 30 s of full intensity, the intensity is linearly decreased to
zero over 20 s. At the end, one second without load is included. It is stressed that the use of
this trapezoidal intensity envelope function is only a numerical trick in order to take care of
the mentioned behaviour of the model with the given load series. The trapezoidal intensity
envelope function do not have anything to do with meteorology or the actual behaviour of
the wind load. It can be argued that based on the assumption of ﬂuid mechanical similarity,
that if the wind speed had been increased and decreased, instead of the intensity, the time
should have been scaled accordingly. An alternative method would be to apply a ﬁctitious
load linearly in the same fashion from zero to the mean wind load before applying the wind,
but it would only solve the ﬁrst issue. The method is chosen in order to disturb the wind
load as little as possible and still be able to see how the dampers behave, when they have
been activated. The trapezoidal intensity envelope function and the eﬀect on a segment of
the wind load series can be seen in Figure 7.15.

Comfort Criteria During Wind Loads
The human comfort at diﬀerent levels of acceleration can be described in the following way
according to Koss (2015c):
• Below 1 milli-g most people cannot perceive motion.
• Around 3 milli-g over 50% of people physically sense motion.
• Around 6 milli-g even people at work sense motion.
• Between 10 and 20 milli-g people begin to feel diﬀerences in walking and action.
• Above 20 milli-g people begin to complain of motion sickness.
Melbourne and Cheung gave the following guideline for maximum acceleration in relation
to comfort in 1988 (Koss, 2015c):
¨xpeak =



p
2 ln(νT)

0.68 + ln(R)
5

e−3.65−0.41 ln(ν)
for ν ≤1 hz
0.063 + 0.019 ln(R)
for ν > 1 hz
(7.67)
where ν is the response frequency, T = 600 is the averaging time for mean wind velocity
and R = 10 is the return period in years. Since the fundamental frequency of the building is
around 1 hz and higher if the friction dampers are in their stick state, the response frequency
will be around 1 hz or higher, thus, the comfort criteria is calculated to 10.9 milli-g.

110
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
7.4
Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Damper
Slip Forces
Two of the more critical subjects of the feasibility study, in relation to the intended behaviour
of the friction dampers and the obtained reductions of displacements and accelerations, are
the bracing stiﬀness of the supporting structure and the damper slip forces. Therefore, this
section investigates the eﬀects of the bracing stiﬀness and the damper slip forces in relation
to achievable vibration reductions for the investigated loads. Furthermore, procedures for
determination of bracing stiﬀness and damper slip load are proposed.
Before studying the eﬀects of the bracing stiﬀness and the damper slip forces, the
approaches for single-level friction dampers are looked into. The American National Earth-
quake Hazards Reduction Program (NEHRP) guidelines demand that single-level friction
dampers are designed for 130 % of Maximum Capable Earthquake (MCE) displacements
and the bracing structure is designed for 130% of the damper slip force. The traditional
approach is to design the frictions dampers not to slip during during wind load. In this way,
the slip force is traditionally set in the interval of 130 % of wind shear and 75% of the shear,
at which the bracing structure will yield (PallDynamics.com, nd). For the present study, it
is desired that the second level of the multi-level has a behaviour as close to the single-level
damper for earthquake mitigation as possible. Thus, the same guidelines are applied for
second level of the multi-level damper.
For the present study, the friction dampers are placed in pairs in a chevron bracing,
with one set in each façade on each ﬂoor. The placement of the dampers in the ﬁctive
building is illustrated by a purple colour in Figure 7.16 (c). This particular set-up is one of
the more frequently used set-ups, when employing Damptech rotational friction dampers,
which have been investigated extensively in this thesis. However, identical results can be
achieved by using any kind of friction damper implemented with the same slip forces and
bracing stiﬀness. The set-up is illustrated in Figure 7.16 (a) and (b).
Figure 7.16: (a) Example of Damptech friction dampers and bracing structure from a project
in Iran (Damptech.com, ndb). (b) 3D rendered illustration of Damptech friction dampers
and bracing structure (Damptech.com, nda). (c) 3D rendered illustration of placement of
friction dampers in the ﬁctive building.
Based on experience from the present feasibility study of the multi-level damper, the
knowledge of local viscous dampers in shear buildings and the knowledge of the behaviour
of the friction damper for mitigation of earthquake forces, the following two step procedure
==PAGE:135==
7.4 Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Damper Slip Forces
111
is proposed for determination of slip forces and the necessary bracing stiﬀness. For this
procedure only single-level friction damping is employed in the calculations, which implies
that traditional calculation methods for single-level friction damping can be used for this
part of the investigation. It is important to mention that the proposed procedure is directly
based on the test case, where the ﬁrst level is intended to reduce accelerations and the
second is intended to reduce displacements. At the end of this section, the optimal set-up
and procedure is described for energy dissipation of general random vibrations of diﬀerent
intensity, which is applicable for many other situations of optimisation and design of the
multi-level damper. The two-step procedure is described in the following way:
1. Determine slip forces for all dampers and the minimum bracing stiﬀness in case of
earthquake loading by considering all dampers as single-level friction dampers.
• The slip force should not be set higher than 75% of the shear force, at which the
bracing structure will yield.
• When a reduction of around 85 % of the displacement is achieved, it will not be
economical to keep increasing slip forces and bracing stiﬀness.
• The individual slip forces of the dampers should be chosen so that they will slip
at approximately the same time, when the structure is subjected to an earthquake
with the expected frequency band for the present location.
2. Determine slip forces and ﬁrst level interval length for all dampers in case of wind
loading by considering all dampers as single-level friction dampers.
• The ﬁrst level interval length, s, should be distributed relatively in a way that
disturbs the damped mode shape as little as possible. The increase in displacement
for the multi-level damper during earthquake loads is approximately equal to the
accumulation of s.
Step 1 - Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Slip Forces of the Second
Level
As described for the ﬁrst step of the proposed procedure, the slip forces and bracing stiﬀness
are investigated in the case of earthquake loading, by considering all dampers as single-level
friction dampers. For this investigation, several considerations must be done. First of all,
this investigation involves ﬁnding i sets of unknowns, kb+d(i) and sfi, for the system of
second order diﬀerential equations. This optimisation issue of bracing stiﬀness and slip forces
for optimal friction damping has been around since the implementation of friction damping
for buildings, therefore, the research and literature on this subject are quite extensive.
Among the many options, several diﬀerent approaches have been considered. One option is
to use a classical algorithm like fminsearch in Matlab. This algorithm ﬁnds the minimum
of a unconstrained multi-variable function by using the derivative-free method by Lagarias
et al. (1998). The algorithm is implemented using a least-squared-cost function on a success
criteria, which the algorithm ﬁnds the minimum of. Another option is to use the same
approach, but employing a genetic algorithm instead, which is a bit more robust optimisation
technique in terms of converging to a global minima. This kind of algorithm is based on the
principles of natural biological evolution, where stronger individuals (design solutions) are
more likely to ’survive’. By means of random ’genetic’ changes, which produce successively
==PAGE:136==
112
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
better approximations to a design solution, the algorithm simulate the biological evolution.
This method has been successfully used by Moreschi and Singh (2003) for optimisation of
friction damping of structures and by Cheng and Li (1998) for multi-objective optimisation
of structures. These methods seems, however, to introduce some randomness to the results
and the design engineer more or less looses the physical explanation of the obtained bracing
stiﬀness and slip forces. A more simple approach has been taken by Cherry and Filiatrault
(1993), where one bracing stiﬀness was chosen for all ﬂoors and the slip force of all dampers
were set equal. With these assumptions the optimisation issue reduced to only one variable.
However, the problem is less optimised and less vibration control is achieved. Other methods
have been found in the literature, like using the Fuzzy decision making method to ﬁnd a
desirable solution among a Pareto set (Fazeli, 2017). In the end, a diﬀerent approach is
proposed, where the optimisation problem is reduced to two variables based on a target
behaviour and spectral modal response. With only two variables, an economical optimum
can be found by assessing the eﬀect of each variable.
Before introducing the proposed approach, the optimisation criteria is described. Again,
several diﬀerent approaches are found in the literature, some investigate diﬀerent kinds of
energy change while others investigate reductions of both displacements and accelerations.
For this project, the second level of the multi-level damper should ensure structural integrity
during severe earthquakes. Therefore, it is desired to reduce sectional forces, which are
heavily related to displacements. Alternatively, maximum inter-storey drifts could have
been used for the performance evaluation, since these are directly related to section forces
of the building elements. However, displacements of the top ﬂoor of the building is easier to
relate to, and it is expected that the inter-storey drifts will not diﬀer much between the
three compared situations, if the maximum displacement of the top ﬂoor is equal. The
optimisation performance is thus based on reduction of displacements of the top ﬂoor of the
building.
In order to reduce the number of variables related to the bracing stiﬀness, it is simply
expressed in relation to the individual ﬂoor stiﬀness:
sri = kb+d(i)
ks(i)
(7.68)
where ks(i) is the stiﬀness of storey i and sri is the bracing stiﬀness ratio. Due to the
inclusion of the geometric stiﬀness, the storey stiﬀness of storey 2 to 4 varies just a bit, even
though they are geometrically identical. The bracing structure of these three storeys will
however be identical, therefore an average storey stiﬀness of these three ﬂoors is used. By
employing the same stiﬀness ratio for all ﬂoors, the variables related to the bracing stiﬀness
reduces to just sr.
The relative distribution of slip forces between dampers is determined in order to reduce
the variables of slip forces to just one; the magnitude. In general terms, the slip forces are
initially set equal to the forces developed in the bracing structures, which are found by a
spectral modal analysis based on the target spectrum, an initial estimate of the stiﬀness
ratio of the bracing structure and assuming all dampers are in their stick state. Secondly,
the magnitude of the slip forces are all reduced with the same factor, and vibration control
optimum is reached. With this approach a distribution of slip forces is obtained, where
all dampers enter the slip state approximately at the same time. Optimal damping is
obtained when the distribution of damper forces are balanced in a way that sustains the
mode and avoids coupling to other modes, e.i. when the dampers work together. Main
==PAGE:137==
7.4 Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Damper Slip Forces
113
and Krenk (2005) proved this to be the case for viscous dampers in shear buildings, where
the optimal damping is analytically analysed through the positive imaginary part of the
frequency change in relation to the positive real valued part of the frequency change. When
the dampers slip approximately at the same time, the closest possible slip force distribution
in order to balance the dampers in the optimal manner is achieved, thus, this approach
will lead to optimal damping. Furthermore, the structural dynamic behaviour is reduced to
two states, with slip and stick dampers, which helps the design engineer to understand the
behaviour of the structure, when it is subjected to earthquake loading.
In the literature, the ratio between the stiﬀness of the bracing structure and the storey
stiﬀness, sr, is found 2 to 3. For the present project and proposed procedure, sr is initially
chosen to 2.5. The stiﬀness from the bracing structures are then added to the stiﬀness
matrix, the mass of the bracing structure and dampers are assessed to be negligible and
the eigenvalue problem is calculated again. In Table 7.2, the natural frequencies with all
dampers in either stick or slip state are listed, and in Figure 7.17 the mode shapes with
all dampers in either slip or stick state are illustrated. When all dampers are in their slip
state, damping is introduced, and when all dampers are in their stick state, the stiﬀness of
the structure is increased, which increases the natural frequencies. The mode shapes are
normalised with the largest value set to unity. With this normalisation it is noted that the
largest value of mode 2 and 4 changes position, however, the changes in mode shapes are
minor.

stick state.

Based on the modal parameters of the system with all dampers in their stick state, the
spectral response is found by using the target spectrum. The modal response from the
spectral acceleration Sa(j) is simply found by the following equation (Chopra, 2012).
xj,peak = Γj
Sa(j)
ω2
j
ϕj
(7.69)
As proven in Chapter 2, damping can only by optimised in relation to one mode, thus, only
the modal response of the mode which the dampers are aimed to damp is necessary. Use
of modal combination rules as the square-root-of-sum-of-squares (SRSS) and the complete
quadratic combination (CQC) are not needed.
When the response of the structure has been estimated, the initial value of the slip forces
of the dampers are simply determined as the force in the bracing structure, which is simply
found by
sf 2 = Kb+d BT xj,peak
(7.70)
If it is desired to skip the spectral modal analysis, the mode shape of the fundamental mode
can be used instead of the response. In this way, a similar distribution of slip forces can
be obtained faster, but the magnitude of the forces are not known. Without the initial
magnitude, the search for optimum will have a wider span.
By expressing the slip forces as a factor, Csf, multiplied on the forces found by equation
(7.70), the optimisation problem is reduced to only two variables, the bracing stiﬀness
ratio, sr, and the damper slip force factor, Csf. When Csf = 0, the damper forces are 0
and when Csf = 1, the dampers will not activate if the response only has a contribution
from the damped mode and the spectrum of the earthquake follows the target spectrum
perfectly. In reality, the slip force factor, which will keep the dampers locked, varies between
earthquakes, but it does not vary much from 1. The eﬀect of the two variables in relation to
the displacement reduction is investigated by calculating the average displacement reduction
of the three accelerograms by non-linear time history calculation with varying bracing
stiﬀness ratio and slip force factor. The results are shown in Figure 7.18.
From Figure 7.18, it is clear that a high displacement reduction is covering the majority
of the horizontal surface. This indicates that suﬃcient suppression of displacements can
be achieved by a relatively small bracing stiﬀness ratio and damper slip force factor. It is
desirable to have an as low bracing stiﬀness ratio and damper slip force factor as possible.
If the damper slip force factor is to high, the dampers will stay locked, which will decrease
the fundamental period of the structure resulting in larger base shear, given that the
fundamental period is higher than 0.8 s with dampers in slip state. If the bracing stiﬀness
ratio is much higher than necessary, material is wasted by having larger dimensions of the
bracing structure than necessary. Before concluding anything from the results in Figure
7.18, some issues must be mentioned. The plot does not include buckling or yielding of the
bracing structure, which without a doubt will change the displacement reduction at higher
damper slip force factors. When a suitable stiﬀness ratio and slip force factor is chosen, the
capacity of the bracing structure must be investigated to see if the stiﬀness is suﬃcient.
Another important relation to mention from the plot is the vertical plane at a damper slip
force factor of 1.2. At this point the forces in the dampers, at any point during the three
earthquakes, are too low to overcome the slip forces. Furthermore, it should be mentioned
that the stiﬀness ratio should exceed 10 before the left corner of the plateau of the target
spectrum at T = 0.2 s is reached and the response is reduced markedly. For the investigated
==PAGE:139==
7.4 Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Damper Slip Forces
115
stiﬀness ratios, the spectral acceleration are approximately equal, because all fundamental
periods are ranging between 0.28 and 1 s.

Figure 7.18: Plot of the average displacement reduction with varying bracing stiﬀness ratio
and slip force factor for the three accelerograms.
From Figure 7.18, it is observed that the eﬀect of adding stiﬀness compared to dissipating
energy is very considerable. This eﬀect leads to two important remarks. Just adding stiﬀness
to a structure can mitigate the seismic response of structures. Researching the literature,
several examples of earthquake protection by employing frame systems are found, see
Safarizki et al. (2013) and Maheri and Akbari (2003), especially for seismic retroﬁtting.
However, this is only a valid option in areas with low seismicity, because the frame will not
be able to resist the seismic forces in areas with moderate to high seismic hazard. But when
the friction damper is introduced, it is set to slip before the bracing structure collapses and
then energy is dissipated. In this way, the friction damper can be considered as a structural
property more than an actual damper for vibration control. This observation is consistent
with conclusions from Chandra et al. (2000). Finally, it can be observed from Figure 7.18
that displacement reductions of around 90 % can be achieved with the single-level friction
damper.
Secondly, the graph also indicates that as long as a stiﬀness ratio higher than 2 is
chosen, the single-level damper set-up will suﬃciently mitigate seismic loads with the design
PGA and for all earthquakes with lower PGA, even though the dampers are not activated.
Therefore, the multi-level damper is not interesting for damping of earthquakes with diﬀerent
PGAs if the performance evaluation is only based on displacement reductions. With the
chosen damper set-up, see the following paragraphs, calculations have been carried out with
the PGA of the three earthquakes reduced to 50 %. In all cases, the acceleration response of
the building was increased compared to the situation without any dampers. In other words,
==PAGE:140==
116
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
the multi-level damper is more of interest in cases, where the dampers need to be activated
in an optimal manner for loads of diﬀerent magnitudes.
From the calculated results in Figure 7.18, a stiﬀness ratio of sr = 2.5 and a slip force
factor of Csf2 = 0.35 are chosen. The slip force factor of 0.35 is equivalent to damper
capacities of 510, 480, 310 and 150 kN on the respective ﬂoors from ﬁrst to fourth, where four
dampers are placed at each ﬂoor. With the proposed method, the intensity of the loading
and the shape of the damped mode are included when normalising the slip forces. This
means, that the optimal Csf2 of 0.35 is also optimum for other shear buildings subjected to
diﬀerent earthquakes in relation to both frequency content and PGA. Using the proposed
method, a stiﬀness ratio of sr = 2.5 and a slip force factor of Csf2 = 0.35, optimal damping
within acceptable precision is obtained in a simple way without having to vary the two
variables. For structures with diﬀerent characteristics as the shear building, the same
approach can be used, but the optimum of the stiﬀness ratio and slip force factor may vary.
Before calculations can be continued, the strength of the bracing structure with the
chosen stiﬀness has to be validated in order to ensure that unrealistic values are not chosen.
For this investigation, focus is on the bracing structure on the ﬁrst ﬂoor. In order to ﬁnd
steel proﬁles that correspond to chosen stiﬀness ratio with consideration of the stiﬀness of
the damper, the necessary area of the steel proﬁles is found. The necessary area is found by
setting up the system of springs in the bracing structure with the dampers, and transforming
the system of four springs into only two horizontal ones. The system of springs and the
transformation is illustrated in Figure 7.19.

From the obtained steel area, SHS 250x250x8 proﬁles are chosen for the ﬁrst ﬂoor. A

the slip forces of the dampers, where the allowable production variation of 15 % should
be included. The utilisation of the proﬁles is 60 %, which is fulﬁlling the demand of a
maximum utilisation of 75 %. The capacity calculations can be found in appendix A.6. The
calculations of the remaining bracing structures can be calculated in identical fashion. All
necessary parameters of the bracing stiﬀness and slip forces for the mitigation of earthquake
loads are now obtained.
Step 2 - Determination of Slip Forces for the First Level
The slip forces for the ﬁrst level of the friction dampers are found in a similar fashion as for
the second level, where a distribution of slip forces is found, and the magnitude is varied in
order to ﬁnd the optimum and to assess the robustness of the obtained optimum. In this
case, the slip forces are investigated in case of wind loading, and by considering all dampers
as single-level friction dampers. For the second level of the friction dampers, the slip forces
were initially set to match the forces in the bracing structure with all dampers in stick state
based on the peak spectral response of building. The initial settings of the slip forces for the
ﬁrst level is based on the behaviour of the building, when it is subjected to wind loading,
where the loading involves ﬂuctuation around a mean wind load. Therefore, the initial slip
forces are set to the force in the bracing structure during the static loading of the structure
with the mean wind load. The static response is simply found by omitting the inertia and
damping term
( ¯K + B Kb+d BT )x = pmean
⇒
x = ( ¯K + B Kb+d BT )−1pmean
(7.75)
With the static response of the building subjected to the mean wind load, the forces in the
bracing structure is simply found in the same fashion as for the second level
sf 1 = Kb+d BT x
(7.76)
As discussed earlier in this thesis, the optimal damper set-up is found when the dampers
are balanced in relation to the shape of the damped mode, which is not implemented in
equation (7.75) and (7.76). However, it is found from the simulations that the response
shape, when not in resonance, follows the shape of the mean static response almost perfectly
and, thus, not the mode shape of the dominating vibration mode. Hence, the shape of the
static response is employed when balancing the damper set-up. The diﬀerence from the
shape of the static response and the mode shape of the ﬁrst mode is, however, very small.
==PAGE:142==
118
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
From the obtained slip forces, it is noticed that the slip force on the top ﬂoor is very low
compared to what seems realistic to construct in practise. Therefore, the dampers on the
top ﬂoor are all considered as single-level dampers. When the top ﬂoor is not damped, it
increases the damping demands for the ﬂoors below. In order to cope with this issue, the
found slip force for the top ﬂoor is simply added to the ﬂoor below.
With the initial set-up of the slip forces, which gives the relative distribution between the
dampers on the respective ﬂoors, the optimum is again found by varying the magnitude with
a factor, Csf1. For the wind load, the performance is evaluated based on ability to reduce
accelerations, since the ﬁrst level of the multi-level dampers in this case is designed in order
to fulﬁll the comfort criteria. All ﬁve wind loads are then investigated with varying damper
slip force factor in relation to acceleration reduction compared to the situation where no
dampers are placed in the building. For the ﬁve sets of calculations, the stiﬀness found for
earthquake mitigation is initially used. The results of the analysis is shown in Figure 7.20.

In the analysis shown in Figure 7.20, the damper slip force factor is increased until the
dampers are all in stick regime for all wind loads, which gives the same results as if the
set-up for the single-level damper designed for earthquake mitigation was used. In this way,
Figure 7.20 compares the acceleration reduction to the building without dampers, which
is the obtained acceleration reduction, but also to the case where all dampers are in stick
state, which is the diﬀerence from the peak acceleration reduction to the straight part of
the graph, when a damper slip force factor of 2 is exceeded.
For the case where all dampers are in stick regime, the acceleration is not reduced for all
wind loads, but increased in one case. Which is caused by that wind load containing more
energy around the higher fundamental frequency of the building. The peak accelerations of
the remaining wind loads are reduced to some extend by just adding the extra system forces
and moving the fundamental frequency away from the lower critical frequency range. Of
all the seventeen wind loads, only 1-3 wind loads, depending on the bracing stiﬀness ratio,
had increase in accelerations. However, in the cases where the acceleration is increased by
the added stiﬀness, the peak acceleration is less compared to the remaining cases. By just
==PAGE:143==
7.4 Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Damper Slip Forces
119
adding the stiﬀness to the structure, the acceleration is reduced 30 % in average for the
ﬁve investigated wind loads. Considering the chosen sample of wind loads in relation to the
entire wind series, an average reduction of 40 % is more realistic for the present test case. It
is assessed that the majority of the reductions of accelerations with the dampers in stuck
state is related to the frequency change, where the fundamental frequency is moved away
from the frequencies of the wind load with higher energy content.
The optimum in relation to acceleration reduction is found with a damper slip force factor
of around 0.2. Reductions are, with these slip forces, found from 50 to 75 % with an average
around 65 %, which is considerable more than the 40 % reduction obtained by only adding
stiﬀness.
Since the results presented in Figure 7.20 are only based on the stiﬀness found in step 1,
another set of calculations are carried out. For these calculations, the stiﬀness ratio is again
varied with varying damper slip force factor. The results of the mean reductions of the ﬁve
wind loads are shown in Figure 7.21.

only 0.9-0.8 % of the slip forces for the second level found in step 1. A diﬀerence of this

magnitude involves several design issues and the proposed damper design will have to be

The displacement interval for the ﬁrst level, s, is then simply obtained by ﬁnding the
largest displacements of the single-level damper, when subjected to wind loading with
the chosen parameters. The force-displacement relation for the four dampers and two
bracing structures at each ﬂoor is shown in Figure 7.22 for the wind load causing the largest
displacements. From these results, it can be seen that the minimum displacement interval,
which is the optimum, is 8, 6 and 3 mm respectively for the three ﬂoors with multi-level
dampers. In practice, it is quite diﬃcult to generate the desired multi-level behaviour with
ﬁrst level intervals that small. Thus, ﬁrst level intervals of 12, 10 and 8 mm are chosen for the
three storeys. The diﬀerence between the chosen intervals is to reduce the disturbance of the
damped mode. The optimum balance of the s values is to use the inter-storey diﬀerences of
the mode shape for the damped mode. However, in this case it would lead to an undesirable
large value at the ﬁrst ﬂoor and an undesirable small value at the third ﬂoor. Furthermore,
it would be interesting to see the eﬀect of having a relative diﬀerence of s values diﬀerent
from the perfect set-up.

From tests of the chosen multi-level set-up during earthquake loads, it is observed that
the huge diﬀerence in slip forces from the ﬁrst level to the second level introduces extra
kinematic forces before the second level is reached compared to the single-level damper. In
order to cope with this issue, the slip forces of the second level is simply increased. The
==PAGE:145==
7.4 Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Damper Slip Forces
121
additional slip forces are estimated as the forces in the bracing structure developed with
displacements of the structure equal to half of the s values. These extra forces are found by
sf increase =

1
2 Kb+d BT s

(7.77)
This relation is not an exact estimate or relation that determines the increased dynamic
eﬀect. It simply bases the increase on the inter-storey diﬀerences of the s values, which
estimates a fair increase in relation to the size and distribution of the ﬁrst level intervals.
This relation of the increase should be used with great care, and if the diﬀerence of the ﬁrst
and second level is not large, it is recommended not to increase the forces of the second
level. The damper slip forces for the second level are then 600, 520, 350 and 280 kN and
the utilisation of the bracing structure on the ﬁrst ﬂoor is 73 %, which is still less than the
75 % maximum.
When analysing the vibration control abilities of the described set-up, by changing the
relative magnitudes between the friction dampers, it is found that the proposed method
results in the almost perfectly optimum set-up. Thus, it is concluded that the proposed
method is a very good and practical way for optimisation of friction damping of buildings
in general, and the use of advanced algorithms for this task is not necessary.
Slip Forces and Bracing Stiﬀness for Energy Dissipation as the Performance
Evaluation During Oscillations of Diﬀerent Magnitude
During the project and the simulation of the possible vibration enhancement for the test
case, several other ideas of application of the multi-level damper have come to life. In order
to assess the feasibility of these ideas, and in order to assess the general diﬀerence between
the single-level and multi-level damper in relation to damping, the ability to dissipate energy
in relation to the magnitude of the oscillation for the dampers in question is investigated.
For the pending calculations, it is chosen to use the same test building and the earthquake
loads. It would be easier to assess the damping abilities of the dampers using sinusoidal
loads or free decay of the building. However, sinusoidal loads rarely represents real-life
situations and single-level friction damping of buildings during free decays can remove
the oscillation entirely, if the force is set high enough. The earthquakes loads are chosen
because they, to some degree, can represent general random loading, and with these loads
the magnitude of the oscillations for linear systems is scaled linearly, when scaling the PGA
of the accelerograms linearly. The scaling of the magnitude of the oscillations is especially
of interest, since it is known that the slip force of the single-level friction damper only
can be optimised for an interval of oscillation amplitudes. Therefore, the main idea of
the multi-level friction damper is to extent the interval of oscillation magnitudes, where
the damper can operate with optimum, and thereby have greater vibration control and
multi-functional use of the damper.
As for the previous determinations of the stiﬀness ratio of the bracing structure and the
damper slip forces, it is required to have a criteria for the performance evaluation in order
decide what and where optimum is. In this case, the energy dissipation is of interest. Since
the oscillation and behaviour of the structure and the energy input is a highly non-linear
process depending on the time-variant dynamic properties, it is assessed that the amount of
the dissipated energy by the dampers is most accurately assessed in relation to the input
energy. Another option could have been to use damping ratios. However, damping ratios are
==PAGE:146==
122
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
determined on modal basis from the relation of equivalent viscous damping and is amplitude
dependent of each cycle, thus, the damping ratio changes throughout the oscillation time
history. The only thing the equivalent viscous damping is able to describe, is the amount
of energy relative to the maximum energy in the respective cycle, and not the non-linear
behaviour of the damper. It is concluded that using a peak or average damping ratio for
performance evaluation would give the reader a wrongful idea of the situation. However, the
chosen performance evaluation is closely related to the damping ratio without introducing a
wrongful impression of the damping ability of the dampers.
Furthermore, the three accelerograms are shortened only to cover the signiﬁcant duration
deﬁned as the 5 - 95 % quantile interval of the cumulative integration of the acceleration
squared. In this way, low slip forces are less favoured with their ability to dissipate energy
during the small oscillations after the signiﬁcant part of the earthquakes, which are not of
interest. The performance of the respective damping systems are thus based on the ratio of
the signiﬁcant input energy that it is able to dissipate during the signiﬁcant duration of the
oscillations.
The dissipated energy by the dampers is calculated based on equation (2.6) and from the
energy balance equation, the input energy to the dynamic system is found by
Einput = −
Z t
0
˙x(t)T p(t) E w(t) dt
(7.78)
As a ﬁnal comment on the load subject, it should be mentioned that the frequency content
of the matched accelerograms all primarily activate the fundamental mode. Therefore, the
comparison of the energy dissipation ability of the two diﬀerent dampers are based on
damping of only one mode.
Again, the determination and preliminary investigation of the multi-level friction damper
performance is investigated by considering it as two single-level friction dampers. The
relative distribution of slip forces between the dampers on the respective ﬂoor is found as
described for step 1 of the test case. The optimum of the bracing stiﬀness and the damper
slip force in relation to the mean ratio of dissipated input energy for the three accelerograms
is like previously investigated by varying the bracing stiﬀness ratio and the damper slip
force factor, see Figure 7.23.
When analysing the results from Figure 7.23, it is clear that the stiﬀness of the bracing
structure has some eﬀect, but not as much as when using displacement reductions for
performance evaluation. Also, when the friction dampers are all in stick state and the only
diﬀerence to the system is the increased stiﬀness, the ratio of the dissipated input energy
naturally goes to zero, since no energy is dissipated by the dampers, when they are in stick
state. The maximum ratio of dissipated input energy is around 90 %. The remaining 10
% is mostly still in the system when the signiﬁcant duration of the load series is over, and
some is dissipated by the viscous damping term of the structural damping. Finally, it is
observed that the optimum of the damper slip force factor is around half of the optimum
slip force factor with displacement reductions for performance evaluation. It is concluded
that the bracing stiﬀness ratio of 2.5 is still applicable, and a damper slip force factor of
0.18 in relation to the response with the PGA in question and the respective forces found
by equation (7.70) is optimal.

The next step is to determine which PGAs the diﬀerent slip force levels of the multi-level
damper should be optimised for. For this part of the simulation it is chosen to scale the
PGAs of the accelerograms from 0.1 g to 0.6 g. This should be interpreted as random
vibrations with an intensity diﬀerence of 6 from lowest to highest. In order to investigate
the performance of a single-level friction element optimised for one magnitude of the PGA
interval, the set-up with that respective set of slip forces is evaluated in relation to the mean
ratio of dissipated input energy for the three accelerograms scaled in the PGA interval. This
investigation has been carried out, where the single-level friction damper has been optimised
for several diﬀerent PGAs. It was found that when using two diﬀerent single-level friction
damper set-ups to cover the interval from 0.1 g to 0.6 g, the best overall coverage was found
by optimising one damper to a PGA of 0.18 g, which is the ﬁrst third of the ﬁrst half of
the interval, and by optimising one damper to a PGA of 0.48 g, which is the middle of the
second half of the interval. The performance of these two single-level friction dampers in
relation to the mean ratio of dissipated input energy for the three accelerograms scaled
in the PGA interval is plotted in Figure 7.24. Furthermore, in order to illustrate how the
performance would be if one single-level friction damper was optimised in order to cover the
intensity interval alone, the performance of one single-level friction element optimised for
0.35 g is also plotted in Figure 7.24.

Figure 7.24: The mean performance of the single-level friction damper with varying intensity,
where each element has been optimised for a diﬀerent intensity.
From Figure 7.24, it is illustrated how the single-level friction damper only can be
optimised for a certain intensity interval. If the slip forces are optimised for higher intensities,
their ability to dissipate energy at lower intensity is poor, and vice versa. This indication
of the single-level damper only being able to cover a range of intensities in an optimal
manner ﬁts well with results by Gewei and Basu (2011) for single-level friction damping
of a single-degree-of-freedom system. It is also clear that the optimum for the respective
single-level dampers are found at the intensity, which it is optimised for. This indicates
that the proposed optimisation procedure is working as intended. Furthermore, if the
investigation had been conducted using the total amount of dissipated energy, the result
had been the same, just with diﬀerent slip forces of the dampers.
The multi-level damper slip forces for the simulation are the obtained optimal slip forces
found for a PGA of 0.18 g and 0.48 g respectively. The si values are based on a factor
multiplied with the inter-storey displacements of the spectral modal response of the damped
mode for a PGA equal to the average PGA of the two PGAs that the friction levels are
optimised for. This particular response is simply found by equation (7.69) to (7.70), and
with the modal parameters of the system without dampers and bracing structures. When
the dampers are moving in the interval si, the structure will behave as if there where no
dampers and bracing structures, but with higher damping. The si values are obtained by
the following relation.
s = CML BT x
(7.79)
where CML is a factor determining the weighting of the ﬁrst and second friction element,
e.i. if CML large the dampers have to be displaced more before the second level is reached.
A value of CML = 0.14 is found to give equal weighting.
==PAGE:149==
7.4 Determination of Bracing Stiﬀness and Damper Slip Forces
125
In order to give the reader a better understanding of the diﬀerence in oscillation
magnitudes of the structure during the scaled signiﬁcant duration of the accelerograms, the
time history response of the top storey of the test building is illustrated in Figure 7.25. It is
observed that the size of the amplitudes is varying in time, which makes the investigation in
relation to input intensities and not directly to a speciﬁc amplitude. Another observation is
the number of cycles, which the respective dampers are activated. The single-level damper
optimised for the lower PGA is activated longer, which is why the performance of the set-up
for the lower PGA decreases less during higher intensities. Alternatively, the investigation
could have been related directly to amplitudes, but it is assessed that in this case the results
would illustrate a performance of multi-level damper that is too good in relation to real-life
situations with the single-level damper. Therefore, it is just mentioned that it should be
kept in mind, when analysing the performances of the two dampers. Furthermore, it is
observed that the dampers are only activated for a short period of time during the time
history. Identical calculations have been carried out, where the length of the already reduced
loads where halved, containing the absolute most severe part of the accelerograms. In this
case, identical results were obtained.

7.5
Results of Simulation
The primary objective of the simulation is to investigate if the multi-level friction damper
will be able to suﬃciently mitigate earthquake and wind induced vibrations in relation
to their respective performance criteria discussed in the previous section. In the previous
section, the necessary design parameters of the multi-level dampers was found and discussed
in a context, where each level of the dampers was analysed separately in order to ﬁnd
the optimal set-up. In this section, the multi-level damper is simulated with the obtained
parameters and compared to the situations, where the building is not damped and where
the building is equipped with single-level friction dampers designed to mitigate earthquake
load. With the primary objectives of the simulation, the research questions become; Can
the multi-level damper reduce displacements during severe earthquakes as suﬃciently as
the single-level damper? And is the multi-level damper able to reduce accelerations during
storms?
The ﬁrst research question is answered by calculating the time history response of
the three situations for the three earthquakes in question. For each time history of the
building equipped with the single-level damper and the multi-level damper, respectively,
the reduction of the peak displacement response of the top ﬂoor compared to the building
without dampers is found. The results are listed in Table 7.5.
==PAGE:151==
7.5 Results of Simulation
127
Table 7.5: Comparison of displacement (disp.) reductions (red.) of the building with
single-level and multi-level damper subjected to earthquake loading.
Earthquake
Cape Mendocino

It is clear from the results in Table 7.5 that the multi-level damper is almost as good
as the single-level damper for reductions of displacements during earthquake loading. On
average the multi-level damper reduces displacements of the top of the test building with
76.2 % compared to the situation without dampers, which is only a decrease of 9 % compared
to the single-level dampers. From the results, it is observed that the diﬀerence in peak
displacement from the single-level damper to the multi-level damper is approximately equal
to the accumulation of ﬁrst level interval lengths si. In Figure 7.26, the time history
displacement of the top ﬂoor of the building during the Kobe earthquake is plotted for the
three compared situations.
Many critics of the friction dampers focusses on the non self-centring part of the damper.
The friction damper is not self-centring, but due to the elastic behaviour of the structure
and the dynamic behaviour of the structure when is oscillates back and forth, the structure
always returns closely to its initial position after the earthquake loading. However, it was
found that if the slip forces was set with a relative distribution, where the main oscillation
mode was disturbed signiﬁcantly, then some storeys could end with inter-storey drifts larger
than 15mm. At maximum for both the single-level and multi-level damper, the displacement
of the top ﬂoor is 10-15 mm, where the inter-storey diﬀerence is distributed between the
ﬂoors. Compared to general tolerances in the building industry, 10-15 mm distributed over
four ﬂoors is very acceptable.
In Figure 7.27, the force-displacement relation of the dampers and bracing structures at
each ﬂoor for the Kobe earthquake for both the single-level and the multi-level set-up are
plotted.

Figure 7.27: Force-displacement relation for the four dampers and two bracing structures
at each ﬂoor for the Kobe earthquake for both the single-level and the multi-level set-up.
It is clear from the relation of the multi-level dampers that the diﬀerence from the ﬁrst
level to the second level is very large. The ﬁrst level is barely visible. The diﬀerence in size
involves three complications. For the algorithm not the skip the ﬁrst level, the time step
have to be very small, which increases calculation time. Secondly, having an interval, where
the system is able to build up extra kinematic forces before the second level is reached,
generates larger displacements, larger accelerations and impact forces on the damper or the
structural mechanism that creates the second level. These forces must be considered if a
set-up like the present test case is used for damping of both earthquake and wind induced
vibrations, where the intensity of the loads are very diﬀerent. Lastly, these intervals that
’suddenly’ ends in a large stiﬀness induces higher modes by energy spillover. When a ﬂoor
suddenly reaches the second level, an ’impulse like’ force is introduced to the system. The
energy transport to higher modes can to some extend be interpreted as damping in terms
of reduced displacements (Weber et al., 2010). However, setting the slip forces and ﬁrst
level intervals to increase the amount of spillover without disturbing the damped mode
is very diﬃcult. An extra optimisation can be gained by obtaining slip forces and ﬁrst
level intervals through advanced optimisation algorithms, which can handle the inclusion
of spilling. But the extra gain is assessed to be very small and as discussed, it can involve
unfortunate disturbance of the damped mode and unfortunate end positions of some of
the friction dampers resulting in large permanent inter-storey drifts. Hence, spillover is
not included in the proposed method for damper optimisation. Furthermore, it is assessed
==PAGE:153==
7.5 Results of Simulation
129
that the spilling can be reduced when the ﬁrst level intervals, si, are balanced as much as
possible in order to reduce the disturbance of the damped mode as much as possible.
Before the results of the simulated wind loading is presented, the frequency content of the
responses of the three situations are discussed. For this, a discrete Fourier transformation
(DFT) of the displacement and acceleration response of the top ﬂoor for the three compared
situations is carried out. It is chosen to normalise all Fourier transformations to unity, since
the main purpose is to illustrate the distribution of the response over frequency and not the
individual magnitudes. The results of these calculations are plotted in Figure 7.29. From
the DFT of the acceleration response of the top ﬂoor with multi-level dampers, activation of
higher modes is indicated. In order to assess and illustrate the activation of higher modes
further, the beginning of the time history of the acceleration of the top ﬂoor during the
Kobe earthquake for the three compared situations is plotted in Figure 7.28.

From the Fourier transformation in Figure 7.29, it can be seen that the building without
dampers has clear resonance of the ﬁrst mode. The building equipped with single-level
friction dampers has responses in the displacement, ranging from the ﬁrst mode without
bracing stiﬀness to the ﬁrst mode with the bracing stiﬀness, with a clear peak on the latter.
This indicates that the building response for this situation is ranging from a situation with all
damper in stick regime to all dampers in the slip regime. The clear peak in the stick regime
shows an undisturbed response with all dampers stick without energy being dissipated,
indicating that the dampers are only activated during the utmost severe oscillations. These
observations of the building with single-level dampers, ﬁt well with observations from the
displacement time series in Figure 7.26. For the building with the multi-level dampers, a
wide peak around the ﬁrst mode without the bracing stiﬀness is observed for the Fourier
transformation of the displacement series. This indicates that the dampers are mostly in slip
state, which is very reasonable with the low slip forces for the ﬁrst level. The peak around
the ﬁrst mode is wide, indicating that a lot of damping is introduced. In general a wider
peak is the result of energy dissipation. For the acceleration response of the building with
multi-level dampers, the energy spilling to higher modes is very clear. However, the energy

spilling is not large enough for the higher modes to eﬀect the displacement response. The

to be due to the momentum of the inertia forces, where the acceleration is integrated twice
in relation to the displacement. Overall it is concluded that the Fourier transformations in
Figure 7.29 can tell a lot about the diﬀerent behaviour of the three analyses situations.
On the subject of energy dissipation, it has been investigated have much energy the
respective single-level and multi-level damper set-up has dissipated during the Kobe earth-
quake. It was found that the multi-level dampers did dissipate a bit more energy in total,
but the ratio of dissipated input energy is only 0.82 compared to 0.89 for the single-level
damper set-up. Clearly the input energy is a bit less for the single-level damper set-up,
which is assessed to be due to general larger stiﬀness. The diﬀerence in the ratio of the
dissipated input energy ﬁts well with diﬀerence in reductions of peak displacement responses.
The second research question is answered by calculating the time history response
of the three situations for the ﬁve wind loads in question. For each time history of the
building equipped with the single-level damper and the multi-level damper, respectively,
the reduction of the peak acceleration response of the third ﬂoor compared to the building
without dampers is found. It is chosen to obtain results for the third ﬂoor, and not the top
ﬂoor, since it is assumed that the roof is not occupied. The results are listed in Table 7.6.
From the results in Table 7.6, it is clear that the multi-level damper is able to reduce
accelerations during storms. The single-level damper on the other hand is also able to

reduce accelerations with around half of the eﬃciency of the multi-level damper. However,
the reduction is varying a lot and in one case the accelerations are actually increased.
This indicates that the eﬀect of increasing the frequencies are considerable. The peak
accelerations of the building equipped with multi-level dampers are kept very low, with only
small variations, and all peak acceleration values are lower than the comfort criteria of 10.9
milli-g. The same relation is not found for the single-level damper. It is concluded that the
multi-level damper is able to eﬃciently reduce accelerations during storms.
Another interesting behaviour of the building equipped with multi-level friction dampers is
the position where the building ends after the storm has ended. In Figure 7.30, the time
history displacement of the top ﬂoor during the ﬁfth wind load for the three compared
situations is plotted. It can be seen that the single-level damper is not activated and it
returns to its initial position as the building without dampers does. The multi-level dampers
on the other hand are clearly activated, but the end position is still very close to the initial
position. In all cases, the top ﬂoor is found around 1.5-2 mm, where the inter-storey drifts
are distributed between the ﬂoors. It is assessed that 1.5-2 mm distributed over four ﬂoors
is very acceptable. Furthermore, it is concluded that the building is moved to its initial
position by its elastic behaviour and the maximum inter-storey drifts are equal to the
position, where equilibrium is found between the damper forces and elastic stiﬀness of the
building. Thus, the maximum inter-storey drifts after activation can be found by:
∆x = BT K−1 B sf 1
(7.80)
When observing the behaviour of the oscillation of the three diﬀerent situations, it is
clear that the building without and with single-level dampers behave linearly, but with the
two diﬀerent response frequencies. The building with the multi-level dampers on the other
hand, does not behave anywhere close to linearly. From analysis of the results, it seems
that the building moves plastically back and forth with a very low frequency as the main
intensity of the wind load changes. The building moves in one direction with the mean wind
load, but the dynamic oscillation around this position is markedly reduced by the energy
dissipation of the dampers.

at each ﬂoor for the ﬁfth wind load for both the single-level and the multi-level set-up are
plotted.

From the results in Figure 7.31 it is clear that the building with the single-level dampers

forces. When comparing the force-displacement relation of the multi-level damper during
wind and the single-level damper during earthquake it is clear that the slip forces are set very
diﬀerently in order to function diﬀerently. The friction damper for acceleration reduction
has a lot more loops dissipating energy somewhat continuously, where the friction damper
during earthquakes only has a couple of loops in order to dissipate energy during the utmost
severe oscillations. This diﬀerence is also contributing to the large diﬀerence in slip forces
for the two levels.

removed. The time history of the acceleration of the top ﬂoor during the most severe part
of the response for the three compared situations, is plotted in Figure 7.32.

dampers oscillates with a higher frequency. The building with multi-level dampers are able
to suppress the acceleration response enough to satisfy the comfort criteria.

From the Fourier transformations in Figure 7.33, the previously mentioned two diﬀerent
linear behaviours of the building without dampers and with single-level dampers are clearly
visible. From the Fourier transformation of the acceleration response for these two situations,
the drop in power in the wind load for frequencies higher than approximate 9 hz discussed
for Figure 7.14 is also visible. However, within this frequency range, the ﬁrst and second
mode is as minimum located. With the ﬁrst mode clearly dominating the response, it is not
necessary to have higher frequency content in the load. As for the earthquake response, the
spilling of energy to higher modes is visible of the Fourier transformation of the acceleration
response. In the previous case the spilling was concluded to be caused by small impulses,
when second level was reaches individually, which can not be the case in this situation,
since the second level is never reached. However, in this case it is assessed to be the same
mechanism of small impulses that causes the spilling, but initiated by the slipping of the
dampers not occurring at the same time, and due to the uncorrelated nature of the wind
loads on the respective ﬂoors. The disturbance of the response shape by the dampers not
slipping exactly simultaneously is assessed to be insigniﬁcant and inevitable with the nature
of the wind load. As long as the higher modes are not visible in the displacement response,
the eﬀect is negligible. Furthermore, from the Fourier transformation of the displacement
response of the building with multi-level dampers, the main response seems to be located on
very low frequencies, much lower than the ﬁrst mode of the building with dampers in slip
state. This is observed for all the responses of the multi-level damper during wind loading.
The behaviour ﬁts very well with the previously mentioned observation that the building,
==PAGE:159==

during the friction damped response in wind loading, moves close to quasi static back and
force with the change in the mean intensity of the load.

subjected to wind loading, the choice of having a single-level damper between the third and

From the analysis of the magnitude of slip forces and bracing stiﬀness in all cases,
it is concluded that deviations of ± 15 % have a very small impact on the vibration
control performance. It is concluded that friction damping is a more robust form of damp-
ing and is capable of coping with the deviations from theory to practise, which are inevitable.
On a general note on friction and viscous damping, where comparisons often are mistaken.
Compared to viscous damping it is not fair to say that the friction damper dissipates more
energy only based on one cycle with the same displacement, which is often stated by
manufactures of friction dampers. For that one cycle it is true, but that one cycle does not
represent the diﬀerence in behaviour. The friction damper is not activated as much as the
viscous damper, and when it is not activated it not dissipating energy, but changing the
==PAGE:160==
136
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
dynamic properties of the structure. This feature of changing the natural frequencies of
friction-damped buildings with the amplitude of the vibration is contributing the vibration
control by avoiding the phenomenon of resonance. The comparisons must therefore depend
on the application type and the speciﬁc project, before such conclusion can be made.
Energy Dissipation Compared to Single-level Dampers
For the comparison of the amplitude and intensity dependency of the single-level and
multi-level dampers, the mean ratio of dissipated input energy is investigated with diﬀerent
slip forces for diﬀerent intensities of random loading.
As earlier described, the three
accelerograms are used to simulate the random loading and their PGAs are scaled in an
interval of 0.1 g to 0.6 g, which creates an intensity diﬀerence of 6. Two diﬀerent single-level
dampers are respectively optimised for low and high load intensities in order to have the best
overall performance in the interval. The multi-level damper set-up is set to have the same
slip forces and the s values are set in order to have equal weighting between the optimisation
for low and high intensities. In Figure 7.35 is shown the mean ratio of dissipated input
energy for the two single-level damper set-ups and the combined multi-level damper set-up
for the range of intensities of the three accelerograms.

performance during the entire load intensity interval, and it even performs almost as good
as the single-level dampers at the intensities that they are optimised for. It can be argued
that the multi-level damper may be an unnecessary complicated luxury, since the lesser
==PAGE:161==

performance of having one of the single-level damper can be good enough. With that said,
it should also be mentioned that had the investigation been conducted on steady state
responses, the optimal working range of the single-level damper had been smaller. Also, if
the interval of the intensities had been larger, the working range of the single-level dampers
would be relative smaller. These arguments leads to the applications of the multi-level fric-
tion damper. It is clearly better than the single-level friction damper, when multi-functional
vibration control or multi-functional optimisation demands are present.
