7.6
Application
The concept of having multiple levels of friction through a limited slotted bolted connection
can be applied for many other applications than the presented test case. In overall terms,
the multi-level damper is a local damper as the single-level damper, which means that it
requires diﬀerential movement within the structure or in relation to an external part in
order to function in any way. The main idea of the concept is simply to overcome some
of the shortcomings of the single-level friction damper. The general shortcomings of the
single-level friction damper is the strong displacement amplitude and intensity dependence
and requirement of some force before is activates. These shortcomings are based on general
energy dissipation and not in the case of displacement reduction during earthquakes, which
is the application where the single-level damper is strongest, and an application that is
also about structural control and not only energy dissipation. The stated shortcomings of
the single-level dampers makes it more diﬃcult to tune the friction damper in an optimal
manner to cover not just one mode, but also the entire displacement or intensity interval. If
the damper is designed for large displacements, it will stick during small vibrations, and if
the slip force is lowered, it will not damp large vibrations suﬃciently. In this section, some
of the applications, where mitigation of the shortcomings can be of additional interest, are
described and brieﬂy discussed.
In overall terms, the multi-level friction damper is applicable for cases with multiple
vibration control demands and in cases where it is necessary or favourable to optimally
damp oscillations of diﬀerent magnitude.
Mitigation of Accelerations During Earthquakes of Diﬀerent Magnitude
As mention earlier, the eﬀect on the accelerations was assessed in situations with earthquakes
of less intensity, where the dampers would not be activated. In these cases it was found
that the acceleration of the building would increase compared to the situation without
dampers. As argued by Chien et al. (2014), some buildings can contain equipment that is
very sensitive to accelerations, thus, it would be necessary to mitigate accelerations during
earthquakes with less intensity. The multi-level damper will be able to handle this issue, and
investigations of peak accelerations for the building without dampers and with single-level
and multi-level dampers respectively during diﬀerent earthquake intensities are expected to
be similar to the results obtained by Chien et al. (2014) shown in Figure 2.14.
Vibration Mitigation of Earthquake and Wind Induced Vibrations
As illustrated in Figure 7.27, the diﬀerence in the magnitude of the slip forces for the ﬁrst
and second level of the multi-level damper is a lot larger, than for the tested damper. As
mentioned, most of the diﬀerence is caused by the large diﬀerence in the magnitudes of
the loads, but also caused by the vibration characteristics that the respective levels are
targeted to mitigate. It is found that there is a factor of 50 in diﬀerence of the oscillation
amplitudes of the linear structure during the two diﬀerent load types, which will naturally
cause a factor 50 in diﬀerence of the slip forces. Another factor of 2 is in the diﬀerence of
the targeted vibration characteristics of acceleration and displacement. In order to mitigate
accelerations, energy must be dissipated continuously, which from Figure 7.23 and 7.21,
where the exact same conditions have been applied, are clearly found at around half of
==PAGE:163==
7.6 Application
139
the slip forces for displacement reductions. Mitigations of displacements of buildings on
the other hand are not conditioned by energy dissipation. From Figure 7.18, it is clear
that markedly reductions can be achieved by adding extra stiﬀness to the structure, and
the dampers are basically implemented in order to dissipate energy during the few utmost
severe oscillations preventing the bracing structure to collapse.
It can be discussed that the large diﬀerence in slip forces is conditional to the test case,
where a cubic structure is used. The diﬀerence is assessed to be less for high-rise structures,
which is also the target structure type for the multi-level device. For these structures, the
relative seismic base shear will be less due the increased vibration periods, and the wind
load relative to the magnitude of the system forces are increased due to the diﬀerence in
height, surface area and volume in comparison to the test case. The building could also be
placed in a region with less earthquake hazard and on stiﬀer soil. Reducing the diﬀerence in
magnitude of the loads will make the diﬀerence in slip forces considerably less. Nonetheless,
it will still be necessary to create larger diﬀerences between the two friction levels. It
is important to mention that from equation (7.80), it is clear that larger slip forces that
are exceeded in in-line wind induced vibrations, can cause larger permanent inter-storey
displacements.
In order to achieve a large enough diﬀerence of slip forces, the tested damper design
has to be alternated. That can simply be done by increasing the number of joints for the
second level. Instead of having 4 joints, the damper design can be made with 8 joints as
for the 5000 kN damper by Mualla et al. (2010). This change in the amount of joints is
illustrated in Figure 7.37. In order to increase the diﬀerence even further, the short plates,
pads and bolt force of the diﬀerent level can be designed with diﬀerent sizes and magnitude.
Furthermore, the slot lengths of the three diﬀerent short plates for the second level could be
set diﬀerent to create even more levels.

Figure 7.37: Sketch of the proposed change of the 4 joint damper to a 8 joint rotational
damper.
==PAGE:164==
140
7 Numerical Simulation of Vibration Control Enhancement
This design has some advantages.
The multi-level friction is created through one
damper, which can be tested and mounted as a hole, and with the rotational mechanism
compared to the translational mechanism, all of the sliding interfaces are covered at all
time. When all of the sliding surfaces are not covered at all time, the uncovered part can
corrode or be covered in lubricating substances. However, the rotational mechanism also
has the disadvantage of introducing a moment arm, when transforming the friction force
to damper force, which requires higher friction forces and results in diﬀerent wear and
energy dissipation over the frictional material. Even with four extra joints it is still dif-
ﬁcult to achieve very large diﬀerences in the friction level, but it can be enough in many cases.
Another approach could be placing two single-level friction dampers in series, where the
multi-level behaviour is created by structural limitation of the stroke of the ﬁrst damper,
instead of creating the multi-level within the damper. The structural limitation can be
made by steel plates and slotted holes or through a device that will lock up depending on
the stroke like the so-called Lock Up Device (LUD), which locks up depending on velocity.
The LUD is used in bridge engineering for mitigation of earthquake load, but allows the
bridge to elongate due to temperature and creep. A sketch of the proposed mechanism for
alternated design of the multi-level set-up can be seen in Figure 7.38.
x
N1
N2
Locking or displacement
interval mechanism
N1 < N2
N1
N2
Figure 7.38: Sketch of the proposed mechanism for alternated design of the multi-level
set-up for mitigation of earthquake induced displacements and wind induced accelerations
in buildings.
One practical design of the employment of the mechanism illustrated in Figure 7.38, could
be obtained by changing the current set-up just a bit. Instead of creating the diﬀerence
in force in the damper, the slotted hole is added to one end of the connections of the
single-level dampers to the structure. This addition of the slotted hole creates an interval,
where the implemented single-level dampers with high force are not activated. The small
friction force is then created by having a small translational friction damper (TFD) in
the steering plates of the bracing structure. This design is illustrated in Figure 7.39. The
advantages of the design is plentiful. Large diﬀerences in the friction force can easily be
created. The small damper, which will be activated a lot more, can be maintained and
replaced without replacing the friction damper part that has not been activated and still
appear new. Furthermore, the impact force when the second level is reached, is occurring in
a position of the damper system, where the strength can easily be increased. However, some
==PAGE:165==
7.6 Application
141
disadvantages are also present. The ﬁrst level friction force has to be relatively small since
it is introducing a moment to the bracing structure, and the dampers have to be tested
individually.
Slotted connection for ﬁrst level interval
Small TFD for low force
Figure 7.39: Sketch of the proposed design where two large rotational single-level dampers
are set in series with one small translational friction damper.
The last alternative design mentioned in this thesis, is the exclusively use of the trans-
lational mechanism creating a multi-level translational friction damper (MLTFD). When
not creating the multi-level behaviour through the rotational mechanism, the limitations in
the diﬀerence of the force of the two levels is much less. The size of each level is simply
increased by increasing the length of the respective friction part. Th implementation of the
MLTFD and a possible design of the MLTFD are illustrated in Figure 7.40. The advantages
and disadvantages of having translational friction instead of rotational friction is already
mentioned, however, one particular disadvantage of the design in Figure 7.40 is that the
impact forces, when the second level is reached, are occurring in the four bolts for the ﬁrst
level friction part, which increases the capacity demand in this part of the damper. If this
issue becomes a problem it might be solved by introducing a stopper through plates welded
to each proﬁle. These plates are then stopping the motion of the ﬁrst level part instead of
the bolts, see Lee et al. (2016).

Internal Damping in Tuned Mass Dampers
One option of application of the multi-level friction damper could be to use it for internal
damping in tuned mass dampers (TMD). The option of using a single-level friction damper
for internal damping in TMDs has previously been investigated by Lee et al. (2005); Gewei
and Basu (2011); Pisal and Jangid (2014). For a TMD, the three parameters of the damper,
stiﬀness, mass and viscous damping, are all tuned in order to achieve optimal damping of
the structure. However, Lee et al. (2005) and Gewei and Basu (2011) point out tuning issues
of the internal damping, when using a friction damper instead of a viscous damper. Lee
et al. (2005) experienced problems with achieving the correct amount of internal damping
in order not to have the optimum balance between a large peak response of the structure
and two large peak responses next to the design frequency caused by the TMD. Gewei and
Basu (2011) experienced that the optimal design of the friction damper was depending on
the amplitude of the oscillation of the structure, which made it diﬃcult to tune it optimally
to one frequency, and to tune it for an amplitude interval of the vibration. Pisal and Jangid
(2014) proposed to overcome some of these issues by using ﬁve friction damped TMDs
instead of one. He had some success, but having ﬁve friction damped TMDs require more
space and a lot more tuning in order to achieve optimal damping.
==PAGE:167==
7.6 Application
143
The mentioned issues can be improved by using a multi-level friction damper instead of a
single-level friction damper for the internal damping in the tuned-mass damper. However,
in order to investigate if the issues can be improved enough for the friction damped TMD
to be as eﬃcient as the viscously damped TMD, it requires numerical and/or experimental
simulation with consideration of the heavy non-linearities of the multi-level friction damper.
Damping of Cables
When using a single-level friction damper for damping of cables, the previous mentioned
drawbacks are again present, which limits the achievable damping ratios. With only one
level of the friction force, the damper can only be set to a compromise between locking the
damper at small amplitudes with more damping on larger amplitudes, or damping of small
amplitudes with less damping of large amplitudes. Therefore, in order to have amplitude
independent damping and avoid locking of the damper, the damper force must be changed
in relation to the magnitude of the vibration.
The option of changing the magnitude of the damper force with the displacement amplitude
has been investigated experimentally by Wagner (2014) and numerically by Weber et al.
(2010). In both cases, semi-active Coulomb friction dampers were used or intended, in
order to change the magnitude of the damper force with the displacement amplitude. The
damping ratios achieved in comparison with the single-level friction damper were higher,
clearly indicating better vibration control of the cables. By using the multi-level damper,
the same beneﬁts of having diﬀerent damper forces depending on the amplitude of the
vibration could possibly be obtained without using semi-active control strategies, which
have other drawbacks as mentioned in Section 2.1. Hence, investigations into the application
of the multi-level damper for control of cable vibration are interesting.
In Combination With Base Isolation
Combining damping with base isolation is not a new idea. In the literature many diﬀerent
kinds of base isolation without damping and in combination with various diﬀerent types of
damping can be found. DAMPTECH has also proposed a design of a rubber bearing friction
damper, which uses the single-level damping mechanism. DAMPTECH also has practical
experience from using rubber bearings, where single-level rotational friction dampers are
placed in the same section as the bearings, between the building and foundation. For these
applications, the multi-level damper will be more beneﬁcial in the same way as for the
internal damping in TMDs and for damping of cables. When using the multi-level damper,
it enables more optimal tuning of the set-up of the base isolator and damper, whereby it
can be optimised for more than just one amplitude interval of the displacement.
Damping of Diﬀerent Modes
This idea is less argued, tested and very project dependent. Consider a structural system
that for some reason require extra damping of the ﬁrst two modes, independently. It could
be in a case of a very narrowband dynamic load that in time changes its main frequency
position. In these situations, where the building oscillates entirely in one mode or the
other, the requirements and optimum relative distributions for the slip forces of the friction
damper would also change. For a case like this, the multi-level damper can enable the design
engineer to set up diﬀerent ﬁrst and second levels of the multi-level damper in order to
mitigate the two diﬀerent vibration situations.
==PAGE:168==
144
==PAGE:169==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusion
Through this thesis, the passive friction damper has been intensively investigated and
developed with emphasis on creating a passive multi-level friction damper, which is able
to overcome some of the shortcomings of general passive vibration control and the general
friction damper with one constant friction force. The developed multi-level friction damper
was aimed to have multiple levels in order to have multi-functional vibration control and
have amplitude independent friction damping. The thesis involves three main topics with
the following conclusions.
Design of dampers: Through a study of passive dampers in general, existing research on
passive multi-level dampers and tribology, the design of the single-level and the multi-level
friction dampers were scrutinized. It was concluded that friction is a very complex phe-
nomenon, which gives rise to many requirements for the frictional material in the sliding
surfaces. In practice, it was succeeded to created a passive friction damper that is me-
chanically changing the damping force depending on the displacement amplitude through a
slotted connection, which is limiting the movement of the low friction force. From this work,
it was concluded that using a slotted connection is the best way to maximise the energy
dissipation at each target amplitude. Furthermore, it is concluded that the bolt choice
for friction dampers, in general, is the second most important part, next to the friction
material. If the wrong kind of bolts is used, the behaviour and force of the damper can, in
time, change much more than allowed for in applying codes.
Mathematical modelling of dampers: In order to create a mathematical model of the
dampers, the diﬀerent behavioural and mechanical characteristics inﬂuencing the damper
was analytically described and calculated numerically using the programming software
Matlab. The frictional behaviour was modelled using Dahl’s dynamic friction model, and
the multi-level behaviour was implemented through an algorithm. In order to have better
compliance of the numerical model and the experimental results, the numerical model
was updated based on the observations during the experimental work, which the model
did not describe. In the end, when comparing the numerical model to the experimental
results, an almost perfect ﬁt was found. It is concluded that a numerical model for design
of multi-level friction dampers that fulﬁls client requests of speciﬁc force-displacement re-
lations, when the damper is supplied for civil engineering projects in the future, was obtained.
Experimental work: The single-level friction damper and the multi-level friction damper
were extensively tested at the laboratory facilities at DTU in order to investigate their
performance during various conditions representing real-life demands of the dampers. Based
on the experimental results, it is concluded that the mechanical change in damping force
depending on the displacement amplitude was successfully obtained. In relation to the
performance of the tested friction dampers, the following conclusions are made:
==PAGE:170==
146
8 Conclusion
• The single-level damper is linearly dependent on displacement and the multi-level
damper is bi-linearly dependent on displacement.
• Both dampers has a linear relation between the normal forces of the bolts and the
damper force.
• Both dampers are independent of frequency within applicable range.
• Both dampers are stable in force, have no signiﬁcant adverse impact due to heating
and have no change of behavioural characteristics during continuous use.
• None of the discussed friction phenomena have been found to have a signiﬁcant adverse
eﬀect on the characteristics of the dampers.
It is concluded that both friction dampers are performing well within the requirements
of applying design codes. It should be mentioned that the origin and compound of the
frictional material used throughout the project is a kept trade secret of DAMPTECH. In
relation to other friction materials, this material performed very well with a kinematic
friction coeﬃcient around 0.38-0.40. Alternative frictional materials have been investigated
in the previous thesis based on these dampers by Tarasaitis (2016). Furthermore, it is
concluded that a suitable relation was established for determination of torque from a desired
bolt force based on experimental results.
Numerical simulation: Numerical simulations were carried out as a feasibility study of the
multi-level damper in order to investigate the possibilities of vibration control enhancement.
The feasibility study of using the damper for multi-functional use was based on a test
case, where the dampers ability to mitigate earthquake induced displacements and wind
induced accelerations was investigated and compared. For this purpose, a reduced ﬁnite
element model was created of a ﬁctive four storey building, which was subjected to wind
load based on wind tunnel measurements and severe earthquake loads based on measured
accelerograms matched to a target spectrum. A method for optimisation of slip forces and
bracing stiﬀness was then proposed and used in order to ﬁnd the best damper set-up based on
the performance criteria of the study. It came about that the multi-level damper can reduce
displacements during severe earthquakes almost as well as the single-level damper. The
achieved average displacement reductions were 76 % and 84 %, respectively. However, the
multi-level damper was also able to reduce accelerations during wind loading with 65 % in
average, where the single-level damper did not reduce accelerations during all wind loads and
only had a mean reduction of 27 %. The diﬀerence in magnitude of the dynamic forces of the
simulated earthquake and wind load was very large, which led to larger diﬀerence between
the slip force of the ﬁrst and second level of the multi-level damper than the experimentally
tested damper design can manage. Therefore, alternative designs were discussed for cases
with large diﬀerences in the multi-functional demands. It is concluded that the passive
multi-level friction damper is able to mitigate multi-function vibration control requirements.
Furthermore, the amplitude dependency of the multi-level damper compared to the single-
level damper was compared by calculating the ratio of dissipated input energy with varying
PGA of the accelerograms, which, to some extend, was representing random vibrations of
diﬀerent magnitude. It was found that the single-level damper could only be optimized for
a certain magnitude of the oscillations, while the multi-level damper could be optimized
for a large range of load magnitudes. It is concluded that the multi-level damper is able to
overcome the amplitude dependence of the single-level friction damper. Basically, it can be
stated that a passive device with some of the beneﬁts of semi-active damping is developed.
==PAGE:171==
CHAPTER 9
Future Studies
The work of the thesis has given rise to a number of potential future projects in the area of
multi-level friction damping. In the following, some of the ideas are brieﬂy presented.
From the numerical simulation of the multi-level damper, it was found that it could be
necessary to have very large diﬀerences of the ﬁrst and second level of the friction force. In
order to create a design with the necessary forces, one could look into the detailed design
of the mentioned ideas in Section 7.6 to expand the damper design in order to cover these
diﬀerences. As mentioned, an important implication with these large diﬀerences is the
impact force, when the second level is reached, which the diﬀerent steel parts should be
able to withstand. The impact forces could be investigated numerically and experimentally.
Unfortunately, the laboratory at the Technical University of Denmark is not able to carry
out these impact tests in full scale, due to the requirements of high velocities with large
forces.
Furthermore, on the subject of the damper design, the relaxation phenomenon of the bolts
should be looked into with long term tests. When the dampers are supplied to a project,
they are, in many cases supposed to last for the entire lifetime of the structure. In order to
know the exact eﬀect over time with the used set-up including the disc-springs, long term
tests over 1, 5, and 10 years could be conducted. Based on experiences gained through these
test, the damper capacities can be predicted through the lifetime of the damper.
In Section 7.6, several application possibilities of the multi-level damper are described.
All of these ideas can be investigated further both numerically and experimentally to assess
the full potential. Among the applications, it is assessed that the tuned mass damper, cable
damper and base isolation would beneﬁt the most by introducing the multi-level damper.
The last idea mentioned in this chapter, is to develop a fair method for estimation of
equivalent viscous damping, in order to have fair estimates of the responses with the applied
damping using simple linear dynamics. It may be that some smaller projects want to use the
multi-level damper, but the approach with non-linear dynamics is to costly. Based on the
bi-linear displacement dependence of the multi-level damper, the easiest approach is assessed
to establish equivalent viscous damping in the traditional sense for each level, respectively.
When the response is estimated for each of the load intensity that the respective part of the
damper is optimized for, the matching equivalent viscous damping is used.

Belfast
European Seismic Hazard Map
Europe has a long history of destructive earthquakes, and seismic risk can severely aﬀect our modern society, as recently 
shown by the 1999 Izmit (Turkey) and the 2009 L’Aquila (Italy) events.  Seismic hazard deﬁnes the likelihood of ground 
shaking associated with the occurrence of future earthquakes, and is the ﬁrst step to evaluate seismic risk, the likelihood 
of damage and loss depending on vulnerability factors (e.g., the type, age and value of buildings and infrastructures, 
population density and land use). High hazard does not necessarily imply high risk: frequent large earthquakes result in 
high hazard but pose limited risk if they occur in remote areas, while even moderate earthquakes may expose densely 
populated areas to high seismic risk.
The collaborative project “Seismic Hazard Harmonization in Europe (SHARE)” was supported by the EU-FP7 to deliver the 
ﬁrst state-of-the-art reference hazard model for Europe, replacing older maps. The SHARE hazard contributes to the 
Global Earthquake Model (GEM) and serves as input for risk mitigation policies such as the design of earthquake-resistant 
multi-storey buildings and critical infrastructures such as bridges or dams.

Peak Ground Acceleration [g]
SHARE
Map Content
The European Seismic Hazard Map displays the ground shaking (i.e. Peak Horizontal Ground Acceleration) to be reached 
or exceeded with a 10% probability in 50 years, corresponding to the average recurrence of such ground motions every 
475 years, as prescribed by the national building codes in Europe for standard buildings. SHARE maps also the higher 
ground shaking recurring only every 1,000-5,000 years, of importance for critical infrastructures such as dams or 
bridges.
The ground shaking values depicted in the map reach over 0.5g (g is the gravitational acceleration).  Low hazard areas 
(PGA≤0.1g) are colored in blue-green, moderate hazard areas in yellow-orange and high hazard areas (PGA>0.25g) in 
red. 
The SHARE seismic hazard is assessed with a time-independent, probabilistic approach. Models of future ground shaking 
are based on the history of earthquakes of the past 1,000 years, on the knowledge of active faults mapped in the ﬁeld, 
on the style and rate of deformation of the Earth’s crust from GPS measurements, and on the instrumental recordings of 
strong ground shaking generated by past earthquakes.
The SHARE results do not replace the existing national design regulations and seismic provisions, which must be obeyed 
for today's design and construction of buildings.
edited by D. Giardini, J. Woessner, and L. Danciu, Swiss Seismological Service, ETH Zurich, August 2013
Distribution of over 30,000 earthquakes with magnitudes larger or equal to 3.5 for the period 1000 – 2007, documented by their damaging eﬀects 
through history or recorded with modern instrumental seismic networks.

Active faults and subucting plates in the Euro-Mediterranean region, diﬀerentiated by color from rapidly slipping (red) to 
slowly slipping (violet). Over 1,100 active faults have been mapped, covering more than 64,000 km of fault length. The 
background depicts the estimated rate of deformation of the Earth’s crust derived from geologic and geodetic data.
Supported by the EU 7th Framework Program, the 4-year SHARE program brought together a core-team of over 50 lea-
ding scientists from 18 research institutions and 12 countries from Europe, North Africa and Turkey, and more than 250 
additional European experts participating in workshops, providing their expertise and data.

==PAGE:193==
Duhamel Note
September 2016
Calculation of Dynamic Response Using Duhamel
Integral
Tobias Friis
Department of Civil Engineering
Technical University of Denmark
September, 2016
Unit-impulse Response Function
The response of a single-degree-of-freedom system to an arbitrary force of varying
magnitude can be calculated from the concept of the impulse response. The procedure
is to divide the exciting force into impulses of inﬁnitesimal area, calculate the responses
to these individual impulses, and add the individual responses together to obtain the
total response using the concept of superposition [1].
Figure 1: Illustration of the procedure to divide the exciting force into impulses of inﬁnitesimal
area and adding the individual responses [5].
Page 1
==PAGE:194==
Duhamel Note
September 2016
The response of an underdamped single-degree-of-freedom system (SDOF) (0 < ζ < 1)
with zero initial displacement (x0 = 0) and initial velocity of v0 = F∆t/m is
x(t) = v0
ωd
e−ζωnt sin(ωdt)
(1)
Inserting v0 with ˆF = F∆t the expression yields
x(t) =
ˆF
mωd
e−ζωnt sin(ωdt)
(2)
It is convenient to write this solution in the following form
x(t) =
ˆF
mh(t)
(3)
Where
h(t) = 1
ωd
e−ζωnt sin(ωdt)
(4)
Equation (4) is known as the unit-impulse response function, since it expresses the
response of the SDOF system to a pure impulse of unit magnitude applied at time
t = τ. Generating response using the Duhamel integral is one means of obtaining the
response through the time domain. It is important to note, that this approach may
only be applied to linear systems, because the response is obtained by superposition of
individual impulse responses [1] [2].
The Duhamel Integral
The derivation of the Duhamel Integral expresses the response of a viscously damped
system to a general dynamic loading. For the damped case the free vibration response
initiated by the diﬀerential load impulse f(τ)dτ experiences exponential decay. By
expressing equation (2) for a diﬀerential response with the diﬀerential load impulse
f(τ)dτ and in terms of t −τ instead of t the following is obtained.
dx(t) = f(τ)dτ
mωd
e−ζωn(t−τ) sin(ωd(t −τ))
(5)
Equation (5) shows that the exponential decay begins at soon as the load is applied.
Integrating these diﬀerential response terms over the loading interval 0 < τ < t it
yields:
x(t) =
1
mωd
Z t
0
f(τ) e−ζωn(t−τ) sin(ωd(t −τ)) dτ
(6)
Equation (6) is known as the Duhamel Integral [5]. For numerical evaluation of the
response of a damped system equation (6) can be written in the following convenient
form.
x(t) = A(t) sin(ωdt) −B(t) cos(ωdt)
(7)
Page 2
==PAGE:195==
Duhamel Note
September 2016
where
A(t) =
1
mωd
Z t
0
f(τ)e−ζωntau
e−ζωnt cos(ωdt) dτ
(8)
B(t) =
1
mωd
Z t
0
f(τ)e−ζωntau
e−ζωnt sin(ωdt) dτ
(9)
The integral expressions can be evaluated by an incremental summation procedure [2].
The Simpson Rule
Consider the ﬁrst numerical integration of y(τ) ≡f(τ) cos(ωt) as required to ﬁnd
¯A(t). For convenience of the numerical calculation, the function y(τ) is evaluated at
equal time increments ∆τ as shown in Figure 2, with the successive ordinates being
identiﬁed by appropriate subscripts.
The integral ¯AN ≡¯A(t = N∆t) can now be
obtained approximately by summing these ordinates after multiplying by weighting
factors, that depend on the numerical integration scheme:
¯A(t) =
1
mωd
Z t
0
y(τ) .= ∆τ
mωd
1
ζ
¯
A
X
ζ
(t)
(10)
Figure 2: Numerical integration scheme [3].
For the Simpson Rule the summation of the ordinates after multiplying by weighting
factors ads up to:
AN = AN−2e−2ζωn∆tau +
∆τ
3mωd
 yN−2 e−2ζωn∆tau + 4yN−1 e−ζωn∆tau + yN

N =2, 4, 6...
(11)
Page 3
==PAGE:196==
Duhamel Note
September 2016
From equation (11) it should be noticed that y1 ≡f1 cos(ωdt1), y2 ≡f2 cos(ωdt2) and
so on. The same method is applied for B(t), but when integrating to ﬁnd B, cos is
replaced by sin [2]. For more on the derivation of equation (11) see [2].
For convenience the following equations are deﬁned since they are constant through
the calculation:
M1 = 4 e−ζωn∆τ
(12)
M2 = e−2ζωn∆τ
(13)
F =
∆τ
3mωd
(14)
And as before mentioned yN for AN and zN for BN:
yN = fN cos(ωdtN)
(15)
zN = fN sin(ωdtN)
(16)
With equation (12) to (14) AN and BN becomes:
AN = AN−2M2 + FN(yN−2M2 + yN−1M1 + yN)
(17)
BN = BN−2M2 + FN(zN−2M2 + zN−1M1 + zN)
(18)
And the response of a damped system becomes:
xN = AN sin(ωdtN) −BN cos(ωdtN)
(19)
When calculating the response M1, M2 and F are calculated ﬁrst, since they are
constant. yN and zN are easily calculated for all time steps. Next AN=2 and BN=2
are calculated and then AN, BN and xN are calculated for the time step interval
4 : 6 : 8 : ... : N. The last step is to obtain the calculated response, xN, without the
zero-values for the time steps 1 : 3 : 4 : ... : N −1.
When calculating the total displacement of the diﬀerent degrees of freedom, the re-
sponse found by the Duhamel integral for each mode is respectively multiplied by the
modal participation factor, MPF and by the eigenvector for the given mode. At last
all displacements for the respective DOF are ’combined’ by simple summation of the
contributions from each mode.
Damped Natural Angular Frequency
The damped natural angular frequency for each mode is used for the time history
analysis [4].
ωd = ωn

% B: Damper placement matrix, n by n
16

17

function [x,xd,xdd,t,Fd,y,v1] = ...
newmarkNONLIN_MDOF_ML(K,C,M,B,dt,N,P,Fc1,Fc2,S,Kb,beta,gamma,epsR);
39
nom=length(M);
%Number of modes in iteration
40
t(1)=0;
%Initial time value
41
Fd(1:nom,1,1)=0;
%Initial values for nonlinear resisting force
42
KT{1,1}=B*Kb*B';
43

44
v(1:nom,1,1)=0;
45
v1(1:nom,1,1)=0;
46

47

%Initial conditions
48

49

%Iteration coefficients
51
a2=1/(beta*dt)*M+(gamma/beta-1)*C;

a3=(1/(2*beta)-1)*M+dt*(gamma/(2*beta)-1)*C;
53

70

72

73

A.8 Essential Matlab Scripts for the Numerical Simulation
189
74

116
